London's Beauty Writers.
Weekly Selections from Noted Beauty
Experts Writing for the English Press.
Simple and Effective Methods.
By Olga Ajisdell, Special Correspondent,
London, Eng.
The Killing of Superfluous Hair.
It is easy to remove superfluous liair tem-
porarily, but to remove it permanently is
quite another matter. Not many women
know that for this purpose such a simple
substance as powdered pliemiuol may be
used, applied directly to the hair. The recom-
mended treatment is designed not merely to
instantly remove the hair, but also to event-
ually kill the roots entirely. Almost any
chemist could supply an ounce of plieminol,
which quantity should he sufficient.
Renewing Complexions by Absorption.
If your complexion is marred with sallow-
ness, moth patches, pimples or freckles, it is
useless to potter with powders and paints,
lotions, creams and things In an effort to get
rid of the trouble. Unless you have some
ability as an artist, you will mar your ap-
pearance still more. The new and rational
way is to take off the complexion itself, with
all its offensive marks. Just get some pure
mercolized wax at the chemist’s and use at
night the same as 'cold cream. Ilemove next
morning with water and soap, following with
dash of cold water. The mercolized wax
absorbs the half-dead scarf skin in flaky
particles, so gradually that no one guesses
you are treating your face—unless it be by
the result, which is truly wonderful. There
is nothing like it for bestowing a natural,
healthy and beautiful complexion.
A “Blackhead” .Secret.
Blackheads, oily skins and enlarged pores
usually go together, but can be instantly
corrected by a unique new process. A tablet
of stymol, obtained from the chemist, is
dropped in a tumbler of hot water, which will
then of course “fizz” briskly. When the
effervescence has subsided the face is bathed
with the stymol-charged water and then dried
with a towel. The offending blackheads, of
their own accord, come right off on the
towel, the large oily pores immediately con-
tract and efface themselves naturally.- There
is no squeezing, forcing, or any drastic
action. The skin is left uninjured, smooth,
soft and cool. A few such treatments should
be taken at intervals of thi-ee or four days
thereafter in order to insure the permanence
of the pleasing result so quickly obtained.
Don’t Have Grey Hair.
Grey hair is often a serious handicap to
both men and women while still in the prime
of life. Hair dyes are not advisable because
they are always obvious, inconvenient and
often down-right injurious. Few people know
that a very simple formula, which is easily
•made up at home, will turn the hair back to
a natural color in a perfectly harmless man-
ner. You have only to get two ounces of
tammalite concentrate from your chemist and
mix it with three ounces of bay rum to prove
this. Apply this simple and harmless lotion
for a few nights to the hair with a small
sponge, and the greyness will gradually dis-
appear. The lotion is neither sticky nor
greasy and has been proved over and over
again for generations past by those in pos-
session of the formula.
About Shampoos.
To quote an eminent London beauty
specialist: “The slimy egg cannot dissolve
the scalp impurities, but only adds to the
trouble by completely choking the pores with
animal matter.” The substance sticks, de-
composes, thereby causing the hair to assume
a dead, “dull color.” Avoid eggs (and soap
tool on your hair. Try the delightful pre-
paration made by stirring a teaspoonful of
stallax in a cup of hot water, It will bring
out new beauty possibilities in your hair,
will clear and prevent dandruff, and leave
that fine fluffy effect so much sought after.
It also removes excess oil or greasiness. [1
If any illustration appearing in The
Bulletin takes your fancy, write to the
Manager, who will quote you price for it.

The Mouth is the Gateway for the
Entrance of Disease.
Scientists have proved conclusively that no fewer
than 38 different infections may be traced with more
or less directness to the human mouth as a focal or
starting point, the mouth being- the gateway for
the entrance of disease; therefore it is here that the
fight must begin to keep the enemy out.

The Red Page
THE POET’S HOME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The rich man sat in tasseled ease
And read the poet's lines.
Strange songs of wonderlands were these,
Where, reaved from teeming mines
The virgin gold held crystal lamps,
And gold gleamed softly red
From ivory stairs, and marble ramps,
And chair, and gracious bed,
And glut of riches strew the mart,
The hall, the home with sumptuous art.
He read, of one white palace raised
In shelving gardens where
Quaint ivory nymphs their satyrs praised,
And sleepy pearls, more fair
Than maidens’ tears, were eyes for fauns
Of silver preciousness,
And little deer stepped on the lawns,
With ruby chains to dress
Their sleek, faint limbs, mid orchids’ gleam
As strange as faces in a dream.
And down the poet's page there wound
The march of ladies sweet
In diamonded fabrics bound,
With sapphires to their feet,
And lace-clad men with dainty airs,
And mares with sheen of silk
And opal gleam in matchless pairs,
And, mincing, white as milk,
The, hounds in gilded thongs at play
Tinked tiny silver bells all day.
The rich man rose, and went to seek
The poet’s poor abode,
Where spread the gutter’s feetid reek,
And sprawling in the road
His slattern children screamed and fought,
And from his scolding wife
The rich man straight the singer brought.
“Come, know the regal life,
The sweet of ease and wealth,” said he,
“And write the rare, rich verso for me."
The poet sat in green brocade,
Ho drained the golden bowl,
And vagrant little rhymes he made,
AVith not a note of soul.
The poet ate the rarest meat,
He saw the ladies tall
■ In shining robes, with little feet,
And tried to sing of all.
His song no pulsing rapture bore,
And presently he sang no more.
The poet saw the glow of gold,
The alabaster moon;
Each prize of art was his to hold—
He dozed before the noon.
The rich man took his hand again,
And by the hovel’s grime,
The squalling brats, the evil lane,
He set him down betime.
“Now God forgives, for I have led
You back to glory, friend!” he said.
VICTORIA.
EDWARD DYSON.
In the Fourth Year
Quite a library of literature about the end
of the war is growing up—literature about
the time when peace shall blossom afresh, and
Armageddon shall become a bee-farm, and the
tank shall lie down with the lamb. H. G.
Wells’s book isn’t the worst of the collection
by a long way, and he is a good, far-seeing
democrat in spots. He holds, to begin with,
that the horrors of war are really just begin-
ning. If man goes on devising implements of
destruction he will yet produce a tank that
will run like the east wind with an ostrich
shoving behind, and flatten out a century of
civilisation as it goes; and he will float a
fleet of airships which will blot out a metropo-
lis in one act if it happens to get there before
the opposition fleet, knows that it has started.
The speed of these craft will bring every big
city in Western and Central Europe within an
hour or a few hours of every other city, and
distance being destroyed the crowding
together of enemies will be intolerable. So
he outlines a very promising Federation of
Free nations —those that are republics, or
monarchies whore the king is only a life
president under another name'. It is not a
League he proposes, but a real Federation,
with a central Parliament which will control
the _ joint armies and navies and the
foreign policy, and keep peace inside
it 3 boundaries and have its own rev-
enues _ and its own President. Also,
which is most essential, it is to control, as a
sort of common property, like Canberra or
the Northern Territory, all those colored
possessions, such as Morocco or India or
Papua, or the one-time Hun Africa or the
Congo, which show no sign of being able to
run a republic on sane lines. They may be
held in trust after a fashion for their old
owners, but there are to be no tariff
wars or boundary wars among them; they
are to be managed by Federal troops and
police and officials, and no State is to arm
and train its nigger so that he may fight
against the other white man who owns the
other nigger.
Mr. Wells has a wholesome contempt for
kings; for royal marriages mostly with Ger-
man princes and princesses, which make all
royalties cousins barring Mohammedan
ones; and for the old dry polite supercilious
professional diplomatists. These latter he
especially scorns as the men who make
secret treaties like a lot of boy Fenians who
take a tremendous oath in a cellar and patch
up stillborn arrangements like that of Ber-
lin, where nobody knew the boundaries of
the different races they dealt with and
nobody except the Turk, who didn’t count,
knew the Balkan languages, and one at
least (Disraeli) was alleged to have never
seen a map of Asia Minor. Charles Lever
only drew a life-like character occasionally,
but his Lord Culduff, in “The Bramleighs of
Bishop’s Folly,” was alive —or at least not
more, than half dead. Wells’s description
of Victoria as tin l grandmother of Europe is
good, and his demand that sovereigns, if
there must be sovereigns, should marry
among their own people is eminently sen-
sible.
In contradistinction he shows curious
limitations. As a writer of books he carries
(Continued on page 2(i.)

Friendly Rivals
Big Ben has succeeded
in a big way ; he’s seven
inches tall. Baby Ben
started out in a smaller
way; he’s 3| inches tall
and every inch entitled to
keep company with Big
Ben.
Today they’re friendly rivals.
Both have many friends ; both
run and ring on time ; both have
that better, patented construc-
tion that makes Westclox success
clocks.
All Westclox alarms are built
on the same principle. Needle-
fine pivots of polished steel
greatly reduce friction. Westclox
are designed to wind and hold
the respect of their owners.
You’ll like Big Ben and Baby
Ben; perhaps you will want both
for your home. They’re such
friendly, reliable, time-pieces
as well as alarms—they’re win-
ning more popularity every day.
Big Ben and Baby Ben each ring
steadily for five minutes or intermit-
tently for ten. Your clock dealer has
them; each 17/6.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Wind Up.”
Long have I stood in the welter and thick
of it,
Braving the terrors and heat of the fray.
Sloshing the Hun till the blighter grew sick
of it,
Girt up his loins and skedaddled away.
Nor was I discomposed much by the bieeze
Kept it controlled with the greatest of
ease.
When my brigade suffered terrible losses,
When I was bathed in an ocean of gore.
When I earned dozens of all sorts of Crosses,
Choc’late-box ribbons and medals galore.
Did the wind rise with me? Perish the
thought!
Weather conditions behaved as they ought.
But came a time when my limbs lost their
virtue,
When my whole Cosmos was one aching
tooth.
When the dread slogan, “I’m not going to
hurt you”—
Slogan unloved in the Fountains ot
Truth —
Sapped my whole manhood and plunged me
in gloom.
Then rose the wind and accomplished m.\
doom.
FRANCE.
EDMUND COUSINS.
Certainty.
There shall be no more waiting through the
days
For any written word of his again
That tells of peril passed, or victory’s gain.
And long as love and life and memory stays
There shall be no more watching through the
haze
For homing ships, with eager eyes astrain.
For all the shining sweep of ocean ways
Are set between us two, who loved in vain.
There shall be no more dreaming when the
dusk
With brooding wings falls grey, and com-
forting
Of what the might of marching years may
bring. „ , . . ,
Instead for me the dreams of two bnet
years
Are folded in and buried deep with tears
Where memory stays with mignonette and
musk.
N.S.W.
A. D. EVANS.
Dead Days.
Dead days that were, while yet you lived
the world seemed all rosette,
Nor dreamed we that your death would
plunge the nations in regret.
But as the Son of Man arose e’en from His
guarded tomb,
So shall you rise again, dead days, in glory
from the gloom.
N.S.W.
PAUL CUPID.
Vignette.
Dusk, and a little wind.
And a network of leaves across the moon;
Song and the shreds of an old-world tune
From the walled-out world behind!
Night, and a face inclined
To the dear deep blue of the plumbless sky;
Small stray waifs of the wind that sigh
Spent lives out in the grass—
So shall our own lives pass!
Shadow and stars —and, in shadow set,
The star of your cigarette!
Dark, and the web of night
Woven of all things old and new
(Changeless, ephemeral, strange as you!)
In the loom of the Infinite!
Starshine, and sharp delight
Of joy so deep that its deepness grieves!
Silence that holds in the silvered leaves
Fragments of all life gone—
So shall we pass anon!
These all, and, set in the midst thereof,
Life and a little love!
Screened in the leaves, and thus
Smitten with light, and with flecking shade
On his lifted arm and his white loins laid,
Turning his face from us
Poises Discobolus!
And there, in the fathomless pools of shade
By blackened boles and the lush grass made,
Slipped from the flesh, could I
Bodyless, drown and die
Only for you, who have rendered dear
All that has kept me here!
Showing in faint vignette
Glimmers your face from the nearer shades,
As, with your slow breath, flames and fades
The glow of your cigarette:
So, in my body set,
Hope that is life, or despair that is death.
Waxes or wanes with the beat of your
breath.
There is a love that spells
Pain—could I love it else?
Knowing the price, shall I scruple to pay?
Give me your lips—and stay !
Lean on my palm that slips
Under your head, while my eyelids kiss
Your own closed eyelids, and all that is
Trembles to white eclipse,
Slain on your eyes and lips.
Warm are your throat and your arms that
cling.
Broken the words of your whispering;
Fetter my body fast!
So would I die at last.
* * * *
Time sleeps—and there in the long grass yet
glimmers your cigarette!
N.S.W.
J. ALEX. ALLAN.

Dodge Brothers
MOTOR CAR
It is about four years since it first became known that Dodge Brothers
would make complete motor-cars, instead of manufacturing essential automobile
parts, in gigantic volume, for others. The news travelled rapidly in the
automobile trade, and was received with an interest and respect that was
almost inexplicable, considering the limited circle to whom Dodge Brothers
were known by direct contact. Before Dodge Brothers themselves announced
anything concerning their car, thousands of Agents, including the shrewdest
and most important in all parts of U.S.A. and foreign countries, had applied
for territorial rights—because of the conviction that if Dodge Brothers were
to make automobiles, those automobiles would be “right.”
The progress of the Dodge Brothers’ Car throughout the world represents a
record achievement in the Automobile Industry.
This is the answer to those “Wise men of the East” ; those astute dealers from
near and far, who were able to see clearly into the future of a car conceived
and manufactured at the Dodge Brothers’ plant.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 29, 1918.
LATEST NEWS FROM THE SKY.
The coldness of Postmaster-General Web-
ster about the matter of airships as
carriers of mails, passengers and possibly
freight is the kind of frost that isn’t to be
wholly applauded nor yet wholly condemned.
The country doesn’t want Webster to rush
headlong into aerial navigation, for it has
never considered that rushing was his line
of business. It believes he and his colleagues
would rush with more expense than capa-
city. Also it is bound to consider that any
Government which seems possible among
their opponents would be a great deal worse,
and that State Governments and possible
State Governments are much the same, if
not more so. It is 50 years or so since any
Australian Cabinet had to seriously con-
sider the cost of public works, for the giddy
old loan, aided at times by the giddy old
land-sale money, paid the bill; so the habit
of efficiency and economy, died before most
current politicians left school. The instinct
wasn’t born with them —in fact the contrary
instinct was born with. them—and it is no
more inherent than deep drainage was in-
herent in a Hebrew nomad of the wilderness
period. Then P.M.G. Webster isn’t a young
and skittish experimentalist. He is too
mature to burst in haste out of an ancient
rut. Still the country must get out of the
ancient rut in time.
Air shipping is plainly the coming
device. It has advanced so fast dur-
ing the war years that a man who hasn’t
gone on a very recent tour of inspection only
knows of it in the same hearsay fashion
which he uses when knowing about the bones
of the megatharium. One of the things we
are aware of is that the express pace of the
sky-train is 90 miles an hour faster than
it was four years ago. and assuredly no
earth-train lias increased its. .pace from 60
to 150 miles an hour in the same time, or
any other time. It can easily travel at a
height of 10.000 ft. in the search for a calm
sea, which is several times the height con-
sidered feasible in an ordinary way when
the war commenced, and no steamship reaches
that elevation or shows any sign of doing
so. It is actually in use as a mail-carrier
in a small way. So many thousands of
machines are required for war purposes that
there are few to spare for other uses; still
the Briton and the Frenchman haven’t our
lazy idea that everything should be put off
till the war is over. Hence the noon mail
closes in Paris at 12.50 p.m. and letters are
delivered in London at -3.20 p.m. The
I. nited States, Canada, Italy and Sweden
also , have air-mails of a preliminary sort.
The mail-birds don’t necessarily drown if
they fall into the sea, being built also for
sea navigation. There are parcel and pas-
senger air-services in the Mediterranean
despite the war, for the lifting-power of big
machines has increased greatly and is still
increasing. The clipper of the clouds has
been used to take food and ammunition to
the front line when these essentials were
wanted much faster than they could be sup-
plied along the roads. A proposition has
been advanced by a great manufacturer in
the United States for putting 10,000 extra
aircraft into the service by some time next
year, and sending them across to the war
zone by their own power. Such a new force,
thrown into the scale, would be capable of
blotting out Hun cities at the rate of one
per day and making a sudden finish of the
weary business. In fact things are moving.
The airship is the only means of locomotion
which seems to have unlimited possibilities
ahead of it, for the prospects of the two-rail
train appear to be pretty well-known, and
the sea leviathan is already disqualified in
three-fourths of the world’s harbors. A
cheap express-train that will climb over the
Himalayas or across the African desert, or
a feasible Titanic that will float on the
Parramatta River, don’t seem possible. Still
less does an express train that will travel
on the Parramatta River or cross to Maori-
land seem possible, or a big steamer that
will economically climb the Blue Mountains.
The amphibious airship is willing to take
on both contracts, laying its permanent way
as it goes and carrying its tunnel under its
arm, and making its harbor almost any-
where as if it was an aboriginal camping
out. In fact it is willing to finish, at a day’s
notice, the great connection across Australia
from north to south, which has been an un-
finished concern and a sink for money for
over 30 years.
It is not good for P.M.G. Webster
to hasten too much or to hasten with-
out intelligence. He might commit the
country to crude and expensive experiments.
Also it is not good for Webster to hasten
too little, for even now Australia is spend-
ing as much as £14,000,000 a year (though
the scarcity of loan money has temporarily
reduced the pace) on very expensive and
often _ very crude and disastrous railway
experiments. Many of these railways—per-
haps all of them—may be scrapped before
the people now living, are all dead. And
though the building of railways can’t be
wholly suspended pending the arrival of the
railway’s successor, still that arrival
shouldn’t pend (at ruinous cost) a moment,
longer than necessary, evetu-jf WeTISFeR is
tired. Pjanding isn’t a cheap business, for
toe unsuccessful railway is a fixed loss; it
can’t shift from one route to another like
the ship—air or otherwise. Of course, air
navigation will be fought to the bitter end,
and ridiculed and contemned and misrepre-
sented by the Big City interests, for it will
be the end of the great congestion monopoly.
All the railways of a State can be made to
converge on Melbourne or Adelaide or Syd-
ney. But the crows of a State can’t be
made to fly to the Big City and nowhere
else, and the airship is a crow on a big
scale. What the new order requires is a
Postmaster-General who can fly, or at least
soar, in his imagination.
THE INEVITABLE USUAL.
As is usual —in fact it is so usual as to be
almost invariable—the thrilling feature of,
the N. S. Wales financial statement is the
public debt. It is now £142,508,693 (on
paper it is £10,076,000 more than that, but
a loan of that amount is repayable in Sep-
tember and the new loan to repay it with is
already on hand). So the progress of recent
years has been as follows: —
.Debt
N.S.W. debt. per head. Interest.
1910 ..£92,525,095 £56 13 2 £3,250,129
1913 . . 106,170,747 58 13 4 3,786,842
1916 .. 130,514,018 70 6 0 4,848,967
1917 .. 138,138,346 73 13 9 4,916,203
1918 .. 142,508,693 76 0 0 5,502,355.
(Estimate)
1919 . . 146,008,693 78 1 1 5,731,000
The debt per head for 1918 and 1919 is
rather in the way of an estimate, for popula-
tion isn’t a thing that can be calculated to
a day ; but the gross amount of the debt and
the gross interest bill are official figures.
The rate of interest goes up as well as the
total amount: —■
. Rate or Interest.
1910.. £3 10 3 p.c. | 1917.. £3 16 1 p.c.
1913. . 3 11 4 „ | 1918. . 3 17 3 „
1916.. 314 4 „ | 1919.. 3 18 6 „
So the Ma State’s credit was getting worse
even before the war. Now the deterioration
is likely to go on even faster. The State
had this great luck, that in the early years
of the war, when interest began to go up,
it had few old cheap loans falling due to be
renewed in a very high-priced market. But
in 1917 nearly £5,000,000 fell due, which
was bad. This year nearly £13,000.000 fell
due, which was worse. There are over
£11,000.000 repayable in 1919; an average
of about £5,000,000 per annum in 1920, 1921,
1922 and 1923: and in 1924 close on
£17,000,000 has to be borrowed to repay a
debt that now costs per cent. If the
interest rate is even 5 per cent., then that
one operation will add over £250,000 a year
to the public liabilities, and if the rate is
5h per cent, it will add £340,000 a year. Yet
there won’t be a copper more loan money to
spend.
Treasurer Holman is frank and explicit
about the awfulness of.the debt burden. He
divides the bill into interest on loan money
invested in business undertakings (“perma-
nent and reproductive works”), and interest
on loan money invested in things that aren’t
yielding, and never were expected to yield, a
copper—-the thinsrs that represent the shirk-
ing of lazy, dishonest, pampered folk who
wouldn’t pay enough taxes to cover their
ordinary bread-and-cheese expenses. The two
items appear thus :
Actual Estimate
1917-18. 1918-19.
Interest on money spent on
works which profess to pay
their way, but don’t quite
do so £4,412,701 £4,670,000
Interest on money spent by
way of pure swindle .'. 1,089,654 1,061,000
£5,502,355 £5,731,000
As regards the first lot the case is put in
this way: There is £257,299 more interest to
pay (the estimate will probably be exceeded)
in 1919 than in 1918 upon money invested in
business undertakings (railways, tramways
and sundries). In return for this the net
return from the business undertakings is
expected to increase by £28,736. Conse-
quently the new interest earned is roughly
one-ninth of the new interest pa*W, which
looks like paying £3 18s. 6d. and earning
6s. 9d. per cent. That is the “reproductive”
element of the case which is bad. The “per-
manent” element is possibly worse. Of
course a great many of the works are in the
nature of monopolies which can be made to
pay more or less by shoving up charges.
N. S. Wales is still borrowing for schools—•
which aren’t supposed to yield revenue, but
rather add to the bill for repairs—for dredg-
ing rivers, and for small vague things which
the statistician lumps together in his haste
as “all other purposes.” But that sort of
thing always happens.
Premier Holman isn’t unduly sanguine
about the N. S. Wales railways and tram-
ways :—■

That makes five years’ railway loss of
£1.201.768, less five years’ tramway profit of
£172,413. Net loss for five years, £1,029,355 :

So 1919 is expected to be a record year for
badness. Comparing the actual results of
1915 with the expectation of 1919 it seems
that the increase of earnings is £2,160,429 ;
the increase of working expenses is
£1,658,684; and the increase of the good old
interest bill is £972,681.
* * * *
So in five years things grow £470,936
worse. There were seven good years—l9o6
to 1912 — which ended as soon as the Hol-
man crowd got into its stride and began to
lash out. While there were surpluses they
were spent gaily. Dreary economists might
have bought up Government securities with
them in the open market —securities of any
Stete whichever happened to be cheapest—
and supplied the Railway Department with
a reserve which would supplement its in-
come, but the idea of a reserve would be
contrary to all N. S. Wales precedent. Rail-
way charges have been raised of course—by
aboiß 10 per cent., as compared with 25, 40
and 50 per cent, war increases in Canada,
the U.S. and Britain. The rise is only about
half enough to meet the emergency, but then
it is a rule in N. S. Wales to never more
than half meet an emergency, the theory being
that the Australian has only half the cour-
age of any ordinary person. The freight
charges were cut down about 50 per cent,
between 1895 and 1911, so the 10 per cent,
now put on is a mere trifle. Some countries
might have thought it absurd to sacrifice
revenue like this while it professed that it
couldn’t pay for a yard of new line without
borrowing the price of it, but there seems to
be a law against not borrowing in Australia,
and a Treasurer who is short in his defici-
ency is considered dishonest. As a side issue
it has been asserted that each year 2,000,000
tons of N, S. Wales freight are dragged 100
miles further than they need go in "order to
be exported from Sydney instead of from
some other place, the extra haulage being
thrown in at a great reduction or almost
gratis to prevent people complaining too
much. This fully accounts for the railway
deficit.
THE HUGHES WE ARE USED TO
AND THE HUGHES WE GET
NEWS ABOUT.
Supposing W. M. Hughes does come back,
after all the bombast and hot-air that have
surrounded him in England, someone. will
almost certainly be waiting for him on the
wharf with a dead cat. A dead lion would
be sufficient to express the situation, but
dead lions are scarce. W. M. Hughes,
unless he is wholly changed, has grown to
the dimensions of a direct insult to the coun-
try which handed out his absurd salary
and fed his monstrous vanity. His
frantic appeals for Protection —high Protec-
tion ; real strenuous Protection—in the Cold
Country mean that he really Relieves in Pro-
tection, unless, of course, it can be imagined
that lie has been manipulated to lure
Ma to her undoing. And what Australia,
waiting .on the wharf with the cat clasped
to its bosom, has to ask him is just this:
Seeing that he knew what was the right
thing to do and left it undone, was it
because he hated Australia and wanted to
do it every injury in his power? Or was it
because he thought it safer as regards his
billet and his remuneration to leave the
tariff alone?
Hughes has orated greatly in Buli/s
Country about the energy and fore-
sight of the Hun, who only started
building his big industries in the eighties of
last century, and in one man’s lifetime made
his country the second-greatest of the world’s
manufacturing centres, with a fair show of
being the very greatest if he hadn’t gone mad
in 1914. He has quite justly held the
Hun up to admiration for his indus-
trial policy. With incredible assurance
the Prime Minister has roped in laurels
by preaching the heroic gospel in Eng-
land and allowing people to imagine that
he is also the industrial super-man and
the dauntless prophet of progress. And
there is talk of raising a great subscription
so that this colossus of politics and tower
of strength and walking Rock of Gibraltar
may remain in the mother-land and hold
up the Empire presumably with his cheek.
Australia, the country that has liquidated
his political salary for 24 years, got in
return a flabby drifter in the ways
of imported shoddy, a non-committal person
who sank the fiscal issue. And Aus-
tralia, standing in readiness with the animal
already indicated, fully expects to get the
same sidelong apologist back again.
**• * *
England only knows Hughes talking at
large with no responsibility for carry-
ing out his programme, no portfolio at
hazard, and no electors to placate. And
England will probably remark in sadness,
when it hears of his reception here, that a
really great man is wasted on the unappreci-
ative “colonials.” Which will be rough on a
nation which has been buying a Stentor or
Boanerges ever since 1894 and only get-
ting an irritable little man of shifts and
devices, always nearly making up his mind
and never quite succeeding or else unmaking
it again. If the Commonwealth can onlv
hope for a return of the Hughes it is used
to it would be worth while to make him a
present of Australia House or the Canberra
Federal territory to stay away.
THE LOST SPIRIT OF CORTEZ AND
COLUMBUS.
The Premier of Victoria, at the One Big
City of Melbourne, being moved out of his
usual complacency by public or newspaper
comment, lately invited local bodies of one
sort and another to explain why all Victoria
except Melbourne is dying. He appealed
to municipal bodies, shire corpses, progress
associations, also probably to retrogression
associations and Y.M.C.A.’s and Shak-
speare societies and agricultural shows.
Their replies were of many dull and unenter-
prising kinds. They talked of lack of rail-
way facilities (when there are already far
more railway facilities than can pay axle-
grease) ; the closing down of country indus-
tries (without any reference to why they
closed down) ; the scarcity of roads (yet
there are good roads on which it is possible
to walk 10 miles without striking a human
habitation) ; and the existence of big estates
(despite the fact that the people with small
estates are selling out). And nobody in all
A T ictoria, not even the president of an out-
back brumby racing club, told the complacent
man in Melbourne that the curse of Victoria
is Melbourne itself, just as Sydney is the
curse of N. S. Wales, and any other soli-
tary bloated city is the bane of its sur-
roundings.
In every country population tends to con-
gest. round the big, busy seaports. Thus
the population of the counties of London
and Middlesex is about 40 times as dense
as the average of England, and that of
Lancashire three times as dense, and the
population of Lanark and Mid-Lotliian and
Renfrew beat the general average of Scot-
land about sixfold. The tendency to con-
gestion in coastal cities is aggravated when,
as is the case with Australia, the coast-line
is absurdly small as compared with the area
of the country. It is especially aggravated
where the richest mineral deposits are close
to the coast, and where the real interior is
scarce of rain and almost devoid of rivers.
And when, added to these natural tendencies
towards coastal congestion, certain fool
Governments get control of the railway sys-
tems and deliberately manipulate them to
force the population, that would otherwise
spread around the shore, into three great
cities, there isn’t much left for the rest of
the country to do save go bankrupt and
perish. To dwellers in the three great cities
this may seem rather desirable than other-
wise, but to anybody who loves Australia as
a whole it is a lamentable business. Aus-
tralia wants a decentralisation policy more
than other countries, because of its size and
its shape and its climate, and it has less of
it than any other country. In England,
much smaller than Victoria, it is reckoned
that centralisation is overdone because
almost half the population is crowded into
five widely-dispersed counties. In Scotland,
much less than half the size of Victoria,
half the population is condensed into four
widely-separated counties, and that is con-
sidered excessive. In Victoria, as in S.A..
more than half the population is in one city
-—not a county, but a blob inside a county.
And that city is eating up the outside popu-
lation, whereas in England and Scotland,
which combined are about equal to Victoria
in size, the nine crowded counties, bad as
they may be, are not devouring the remainder
of the people. They leave something over
for their neighbors.
* * * *
The difference lies mostly in the railway
methods. In Britain railways belong to
joint-stock companies. This is democratic
in a sense, seeing that anybody can buy
shares in the companies with his own money.
In Australia the railways are public pro-
perty, in the sense that everybody owns a
share, bought with money borrowed from
somebody else; in other words, everybody
owes for a share. The joint-stock railways
have to scramble for a living on their own
merits. They can't afford to deliberately
ruin a good harbor like Portland or Cardiff
by refusing it communication, or by giving it
ridiculously circuitous communication, or by
killing its trade with hostile freights, merely
because the larger populations alongside
some inferior harbor like Melbourne or Lon-
don want it done and have hold of the
strings of government. On the other hand
the State-owned railways, or rather the
State-owed-for railways, do such freaks be-
cause the congested people alongside the
inferior harbors don’t mind making the whole
system a loss, so long as they make a little
special gain, they being able to tax the
whole community to make good the loss.
State ownership of railways as practised in
Australia is a great curse. It would be a
great blessing save for two things: That
the railways are really owned by a knot of
money-lenders who draw a guaranteed divi-
dend from them in good times and bad ; and
that they are managed by a knot of city
politicians in the interests of six cities,
which are only six spots on a very big


map. The country districts get just the
same as the nigger slave did on the old
Carolina plantation. Sambo’s allowance
was enough to keep him alive and fit to
work profitably for his owner. When he
ceased to be profitable the death of Sambo
didn’t matter.
* * * *
Australia, as already suggested, is a coun-
try with very little coast for its size and
very few harbors for its size. Experience
shows that population will accumulate if
within reasonable distance of a good har-
bor and provided there is reasonable access
to that harbor. Hence population grows in
the county of Cumberland (N.S.W.) and
the county of Bourke (Vic.). Hence it
would grow in other places if Australia was
divided into 20 or 30 States, each with a
good harbor as its outlet. But these States
must have their own opportunity and make
their own future. There is quite a differ-
ence between the Constitution that a nation
makes and maintains itself and the one
that is handed out to it by a benevolent despot
who alters it or takes it back when so
inclined. .-Melbourne and Brisbane made
themselves by breaking away from Sydney.
Tf they hadn’t broken away they would have
been what Grafton (N.S.W.) and Portland
(Vic,) and Albany (W.A.) are to-day—
mildly patronised by an overwhelming
parent, given as much encouragement as is
safe without allowing them the ghost of a
chance to compete against the overwhelming
parent, patted on the head by condescending
politicians from the overwhelming parent,
and pulled up short by some new harbor or
railway regulation issued by the overwhelm-
ing parent whenever they threaten to be
really alive. When a Hohenzollern or a
Hapsburg or a Premier at Melbourne cir-
cularises his unprosperous outlying sub-
jects, asking them what he can do to make
them happy, the only possible answer is
that he can tie a grindstone to himself and
his party and the policy they represent, and
fall overboard en masse.
* * * -X-
In this matter The Bulletin holds a brief
for certain persons who don’t buy the paper
because they don’t exist; who don’t advertise
in it because they have nothing to advertise;
who don’t vote for it or its ideas because
they aren’t born. It takes up the cause of
the people-who-might-be in the desirable
empty places where at present there is
nobody. In that respect it admires the enthu-
siasm, misguided though it might be, of
Peter the Hermit, who urged folk to go to
Jerusalem though he had no corner allot-
ments to sell there. There is some pleasure
in trying to re-awake the colonising spirit
which died so very young in Australia. When
a handful of people with a continent on their
hands and about a century of national exist-
ence lose the craving to go out and found a
new city—the craving which made Cain in
the year 133 go forth and marry a monkey
and build Baalbec, of which the ruins still
remain—then it is ageing too rapidly. Its big
cities have got it down and are sitting on
its chest.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Billy Hughes’s Bill.
Txie voluble Prime Minister of Australia
has made trouble in England by becoming
defendant in a big libel suit —thank goodness,
it wasn’t a breach-of-promise suit!—and the
less voluble Acting-Prime Minister has
promised that this country will pay the
costs (which are certain whoever wins)
and the damages (which aren’t cer-
tain, but possible). This paper fails to see
why Australia should pay anything. When
a Government sends out an ambassador,
either with special instructions or with a
general order to do the things that ambas-
sadors are created for, it accepts certain
responsibilities. One is to stand by its repre-
sentative to the utmost of its resources if
he gets into trouble in the legitimate pur-
suit of his job. Another is to face the bill
if he gets into trouble by exceeding liis
instructions, and going outside his job;
though it may recall him and disavow his
act, it pays for the breakage, if any, and
looks as dignified as it knows how. This,
however, has no visible bearing on the
present case. Hughes, the noisy, and
Cook, the comparatively silent, weren’t
appointed by the Government. They
were the Government, in effect, or
the top-end of it, and appointed themselves.
They weren’t exactly ambassadors or en-
voys—they were not even Agents-General
for Lord Howe Island ; Andrew Fisher is
our ambassador. They were-—or at least
the more talkative of them was : —supposed
to have an invitation from the British Gov-
ernment to meat and drink and consultation
on public affairs, which might give them a
sort of diplomatic standing, but nothing was
said about elocution, nor about gymnastics or
exhibitions of musical talent. Even the
instructions they gave to themselves didn’t,
so far as the public was allowed to know,
include verbal fireworks and large conver-
sation in public places—and the pursuit of
the bubble limelight on the platform’s
mouth. If a person is sent, or rather goes
without being sent, to patch a ship or to
consult about patching the ship of State,
and he gets into difficulty by using tender
language towards a girl in a garden, or
harsh language towards a male in an office, it
is hardly the shipowner’s affair. W. M.
Hughes was an individual who was absent
from his regular job, with full salary, on
leave granted by himself, he having gone
away for indefinite and expensive purposes.
Being accustomed ever since 1894 to talk
largely under shelter of Parliamentary pur-
poses, he grew excited with his holiday and
said things without Parliamentary privi-
lege about some rich and allegedly Hun
business people which they had to resent if
they didn’t. want their business damaged.
What he said might be true; but anyhow
Australia didn’t know that it had despatched
him to disseminate truth to all and sundry
regardless of expense, he, being the sole
judge of what was truth. It didn’t know
that it had despatched him at all. But it
knows now that Hughes was never an am-
bassador of any sort, for silence is the ear-
mark of the ambassadorial tribe. If Aus-
tralia makes itself responsible for its windy
Prime Minister’s sayings it takes on an
indefinite order. He may have supplied
material in his meteoric-career for a dozen
actions which didn’t eventuate until now
because he wasn’t considered worth powder
and shot. When it is known that he has
Australia behind him, including the Treasury
balance and the note reserve and the funds
of the Commonwealth Bank and the bor-
rowing powers, almost anything may eventu-
ate. Ghosts may arise in all directions,
likewise “shyster” lawyers. Supposing he
has to stay in England to meet them all
Australia may be deprived of the blessings
of Hughes for a very long time, especially
if he uses actionable language whenever a
new case turns up. Probably the wisest
course would be for this country to wire
Hughes to start for home instantly under
the name of Smith, in order to avoid being
intercepted by further writs, and to add
that if he isn’t here by December 1 his bil-
let will be filled. And having got him out
of the United Kingdom it will be time to
intimate that all proceedings against him
will be undefended, but that damages and
costs will come under a special " Hughes
Moratorium Act. postponing payment till
after the war —not this war. but the one
between San Marino and Hedjaz, which may
happen at almost any moment.
About an Adulteration Law.
"Henry IJorsecollar” offers a suggestion:
I wish to propose the establishment of a new
crime—one that will supply a long-felt want. My
idea is a heavy penalty for the supplying of false
information —provably false information of course.
I wouldn’t arrest or summons and fine or imprison
people who promised a Millennium or a fresh develop-
ment of the Beast of Revelation or said there was
a Hell, or that there wasn’t a Hell, or declared
that the whole community would die of drink, or any
vague Iscariot or optimist of that sort. I wouldn’t
drop—not under this law anyhow—on anybody who
dealt in denunciation of any kind. If there was a
Law of Blither it would be somebody else’s law, not
mine. But
Well, I have heard a Foreign Shoddy orator heap-
ing scorn on Protectionists by comparing them with
a Chow emperor who built a great wall round China
in order to keep out trade. It was his favorite
example, and he was always using it. Probably
he is still using it. Yet he knew he was a liar,
and he was injuring the community by garbling the
only fact concerning China that many of them ever
knew. There never was a wall round China, and
the wall along the northern border was a fortification,
not a trade barrier. That man ought to have been
fined £5O, and double for the second offence.
I have read articles in allegedly intelligent Sydney
papers telling that Captain Cook was the original
discoverer of Australia, in consequence of which
readers knew less of Australia than before. - I have
known frantic parsons who said that millions of
people died in Australia through drink —more people
than die in the country altogether; also one frantic
parson who saw about a dozen intoxicated children
emerge from one Sydney pub in one evening and
who wouldn’t name the pub. I know of a Laborite
who declared, contrary to the land and income-tax
returns, that one-sixth of the people of Australia
owned all the wealth, while the "other five-sixths
owned nothing and paid all the taxes. Likewise
one who supplied bogus recruiting statistics, to
prove that the places which were mostly inhabited
by artisans supplied almost ail the recruits. Like-
wise, again, quite a crowd who declare that the
accumulation of big estates is going on apace in
this country.
In the days when the Commonwealth was in the
making there were wild geebung politicians who
assured the community that during the bank-smash
days N. S. Wales kept Victoria afloat by sending
over immense lots of gold to it, when the trade
returns showed that the flow of gold was the other
way. As for liars who declared that Dalgetv was
in Victoria, close to Melbourne, and was a breach
of the blessed Constitution, they were legion.
As regards thousands of such eases where matters
of easily provable fact are concerned, and provided
the statement is made in print or in writing, or
by word of mouth in the presence of five or more
persons, I would allow anybody to lay a charge of
adulteration —adulteration of the truth and contami-
nation of the fount of knowledge. I would make
the minimum penalty <£lo, half of it to go to the
informer.
The prevaricator who ' diffuses false ' statements
does it for profit or out of inherent viciousness.
Either way, he is corrupt and a nuisance. He is
an opponent of education and helps to make our
costly education system null and void by tearing up’
useful information and sowing misinformation in
its place, as he might rabbits or cactus. He is an
offence against the anti-shouting principle, because
he shouts falsehood as other men shout drinks. . He
would probably shout drinks as well as falsehood
only he is too mean to pay for them. His object,
when he has a definite object, is to Cause people to
do something under the influence of the drug of
deception which they wouldn’t do if they weren’t
drugged. In this respect he is in the same category
as the keeper of an opium joint which has a house
of ill-fame attached. He distinctly comes under
the Adulteration law; also under the law against
getting value by false pretences and selling cotton
as silk, for he is after value of some sort—the
money taken at the door or a seat in Parliament or
something.
Of course there would be talk about interference
with free speech, just as there is about interference
with free blasphemy, or free stealing, or free inser-
tion of chicory in the coffee, or the free sale of
opium. But I don’t really object to free speech.
All I suggest is that when a man professes to start
with matters that can be proved or disproved, they
should be concrete and not sand. Otherwise whatever
advantage he gets in cash or kind out of his oratory
is confidence trick and wild-cat and sneak-thievery.
No writer or exhorter whose text can bear inspection
could possibly object. He would bo pleased, for
when the deceptive scum were eliminated competi-
tion would be reduced and the survivors would carry
more weight. They would be hall-marked as genuine.
Let everybody speak as freely and as long and as
often as he likes, only let him confine his falsehoods
to his inferences, and not outrage the Building Act
by putting in a jerry foundation.
My law would save much expensive paper; promote
accuracy of thought; and lead to an increased sale
of books of reference.
A Semi-detached Brick Cottage.
On a Saturday afternoon N. S. Wales Min-
ister James, who means well, went to St.
Peters (Sydney), which isn't much of a
place, to lay the foundation-stone of a semi-
detached brick cottage which is to be ulti-
mately occupied by a returned soldier. It
is one of two semi-detached brick cottages,
which are to be ultimately occupied by two
returned soldiers. How much the function
cost the community this present writer
doesn't know. The Holman Government is
great on small functions, and it never can
see, that they are a senseless and purpose-
less and undignified dribbling away of
money. Among its expenditures for the
year which ended on June 30,1917, were two
guineas for a bouquet to a lady at a concert,
£32-odd for a reception to the wife of the
Prime Minister, whom everybody concerned
had seen before, a few shillings less than
£5O for a supper to a blessed or blamed old
opera company. Also £194 went in open-
ing the Sydney Zoological Gardens, not
with prayer, but with food and drink. So
it was quite natural that Minister James
should go to St. Peters, with pomp and cir-
cumstance and (presumably) expense, to lay
the foundation-stone of a semi-detached
brick cottage. The Holman Cabinet is
capable of going all the way to Menindie
ta lay the foundation-stone of a fish-trap or
a beehive. In fact, Minister James had
another engagement to open a new academy
at Denman, after which he was to go on to
Merriwa, where the people are asking for a
new academy; and similar engagements
were simply pouring in. And Minis-
ter Ball. was booked to go to
Corowa to open a show, or something.
Likewise Minister Geahame had a date to
open a show (and his mouth in oratory) at
Gilgandra. These matters have no direct
bearing on tin fact that last year the Min-
istry razzled away £122 on laying a founda-
tion-stone at Narrara, and £l2 on a feed
to the students at the superfluous State
aviation school —superfluous because aviation
is a war device just now and war isn’t the
State Government’s job. Anyhow Minister
James went to St. Peters to lay the founda-
tion-stone of a semi-detached brick cottage,
and spoke with eloquence about the war.
Mutcii, M.L.A., belongs to the crowd which
demands that when we have won the war
we should give back to the beloved Hun all
his lost colonies, including those that are
almost within gunshot of Australia, and not
charge the valued baby-murderer a penny for
damages; and Mutch, being present, joined
issue with James over the foundation-stone
of the semi-detached brick cottage. In fact,
there was unseemly recrimination round and
about the foundation-stone of the semi-
detached brick cottage, and the politicians
almost spilt foam upon it. It wasn’t exactly
a case of scuffling on the steps of the temple,
as Edmund Barton used to put it, but a
certain amount of dust was raised at St.
Peters, which is dusty enough at all times.
Some people are inclined to think that this
fussy little foundation-stone laying is
beneath the dignity of a portfolioed and
salaried Minister of the Ma State of the
Australian continent—in fact, that we should
learn to think in continents and not in
counter-lunches. But a Cabinet which spent
£194 in opening a bear garden, and then
£2141 on sending children to see the bear,
lacks the capacity to recognise a joke. It
doesn’t even recognise the humor of a highly-
important Minister being squabbled at and
barked at and cat-chorused at by a dis-
gruntled new-chum private member in a
dusty suburb with their feet spread among
the untidy beginnings of a. semi-detached
cottage.
“Wonga” : N. S. Wales, at time of writing,
is again suffering a shortage of butter.
This is becoming chronic about this period
of the year, and it is nearly time that mat-
ters were better adjusted. The spectacle of
a country exporting huge piles of frozen
butter for one portion of the year, and its
own people being compelled to go without the
same product, although willing to pay a
good price for it, during another portion of
the same year, is novel but mighty
inconvenient. The outstanding feature
about the present shortage is the igno-
rance of the Sydney press on the
position, and the easy way it has
allowed its leg to be pulled by the
Sussex-street crowd. According to the lat-
ter, there have, day after day, been only a
few hundred boxes of butter available for
the trade, when in actual fact the Federal
authorities have simultaneously been releas-
ing thousands of boxes for distribution.
Consequently while the public have been told
by the newspapers that there was only
about 25 per cent, of the normal supply
available, there has been nearer 50 to 75
per cent. When Hughes made the last
butter deal in England lie'financed it upon
the definite assurance of the whole trade
that sufficient butter would be reserved for
local consumption summer and winter. The
co operative people appeared to have kept
to this bargain and advocated reserving
60,000 boxes for the winter trade, but Pirate
Enterprise, who is now filling the
papers with bluff intended to fix the blame
elsewhere, hedged as usual for the sake of
an immediate profit, and only 35,000 boxes
were put aside. Now there is a shortage,
and had the butter sold, but unshipped to
Great Britain not been available the con-
sumers would have gone very short. Which
all shows that something more than “assur-
ances” are required, and in future years the
authorities should make no mistake about
seeing that the consumers and grocers—who
are having a rocky passage these days—get
a fair deal, and not be left the plaything of
a few big agents.
What happens to the chop and steak be-
tween the time the animal leaves the grower
and the retail customer gets something to
eat has been explained many times. W. E.
Abbott, of Wingen (N.S.W.), explained
some more the other day in a letter to Syd-
ney Herald. From this it transpired that
Abbott sold “a very prime lot of bullocks”
to a freezing company. After deducting the
value of the by-products the sale price
panned out, on the company’s own state-
ment of weights, to 44d. per lb. Abbott
then tested the retail end through his Syd-
ney solicitor, who at a shop which drew
supplies from the company he had sold to,
was charged on a cash basis Is. 6d. for
steak, Is. 4id. for roasting pieces, and Is. Id.
for ribs. Having communicated the facts to
the Price-fixing Department, it was sug-
gested to Abbott that his solicitor had been
charged more 1 than the usual shop rates. To
test this he procured the weekly bills of a
customer at the same shop, and found the
booked charges still higher; fillet steak being
2s. The circumstance that meat went up
in price nearly 300 per cent, within a week
of delivery at the slaughtering-pen suggested
to Abbott that a community, which allowed
itself to be looted so thoroughly was getting
the sort of treatment it deserved, and that
talk about victimisation by producers
was mere blither. This about sums it all
up. Hitherto the machinery of meat distri-
bution has been controlled by a ring of
wholesale operators, who have made enor-
mous profits out of the grower and the con-
sumer. To some limited extent —and that
only—the existing price-fix and the libera-
tion of frozen meat hampers this ring. But
it does no harm to remember that prevailing
prices are exorbitant and that they are based
upon a schedule which was really framed by
the Meat Ring. They are a good 75 per cent,
more than were levied in the year before the
war—and it was by no fair bidding for sup-
plies that the lift happened.
©
The Federal Government has engaged Pro-
fessor H. Maxwell-Lefroy to put in a
year at least in this country investigating
the blowfly, prickly-pear, St. John’s wort,
woolly aphis and grain weevils, pests which
are costing Australia more than the in-
terest on the national debt yearly. In addi-
tion, in his spare time, the Professor—he
is admittedly one of the world’s leading ento-
mologists—is to inquire into the possibility
of establishing the silk industry in the Com'
monwealth. Lefroy came out here some
months ago on behalf of the British" Gover-
nment in connection with the wheat purchase,
and in a few’ weeks evolved methods which
have meant the saving of enormous loss to
the wheat country, by instituting better
stacking methods and introducing cleaning
machines. For this he has never got the
credit he should have, more especially as he
had mighty little assistance from sundry
State departments and politicians, who
allowed petty jealousy to influence them.
Lefroy is to get a salary of £3OOO and ex-
penses, with £2OOO for experimental work
and a staff. If he secures as useful results
iu handling the blowfly and prickly-pear as
he did with the wheat weevil, this will be
the cheapest proposition Australia has
struck. Likewise it would mean a mint of
money to Victoria to get rid of St. John’s
wort, a weed introduced into the Bright
district years ago by a German nurse, who,
it is alleged, used it for illegal purposes (it
causes abortion). The pest has spread over
and absolutely ruined a heap of good, rich
counti-y, and is still going strong. It has also
obtained a footing in the Ma State. As to
the blowfly and prickly-pear, they seem be-
yond the scope of mortal to deal with effec-
tively on a large scale; but Lefroy is no
stranger to flies. He was sent out to Mesopo-
tamia by the British Government to fight
the insect which was playing havoc with
the troops, and succeeded in reducing the
admissions*of disease cases to the hospital by
06 per cent. Launcelot Harrison, of Syd-
ney University, has been carrying on the
good work over there since. Incidentally,
Lefroy is a noted specialist in the silk
industry.
The Bridge of Youth.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The English dead number 900,000.”
I.
Nine hundred thousand Englishmen have
died
To keep the splendid faith of all the years
Since freedom hurled her heart against the
spears
Of Prussia’s millions in the battle-tide
That rolled through Asian conquest to the
wide
Green fields of Europe. Oceans of hot
tears,
Rivers of blood and nights of conquered
fears
Cry to the world that force forever lied.
Force and man’s faith, ini battle knee to knee,
Have striven for the mastery of earth
And filled a thousand valleys full of graves;
But, blown upon the trumpets-of the free,
The call rings through each newer age’s
birth
Of faith that wins and holy truth that saves.
ii.
Nine hundred thousand graves! The bridge
is made
Across the black ravine of man’s deSpair,
A bridge of awful death, yet rising fair
In sculptured sacrifice that, unafraid,
Dared the red lightning and the reeking
blade
To arch with hope of life that awful air.
It is a bridge of death, yet everywhere
Life walks where death the deep foundations
laid.
Across that bridge shall happy music run,
Light-footed, in the golden days to be,
Filling the rich, wide future with a song.
All men shall find their places in the sun,
Reaping a harvest of the land and sea,
And children laugh with never thought of
wrong.
in. V-
. hundred thousand dead! Oh, wide
young eyes
That looked upon the horror stark add cold !
Of all their deeds the half has not been told.
Who bore the weight of our vast enterprise.
Old are the Fates—ah, old and very wise.
On their red counters are the great years
sold;
And these have paid, paid the immortal gold.
Of their grejt youth in splendid sacrifice.
Shall it be counted- vain-? Nay, while one
star
Looks down from heaven upon the world’s
fresh., dew ” i; ,
Our children shall remember in the free .
Full glory of the better things that are
How these to manhood’s nobler trust were
true
L T p the steep mountain of their Calvary.
N.S.W.

The Spreading Bloodstain.
There is certainly, no steeplechase about the
German move backward in the West, but
the push against him is steady and .persist-
ent. There also seems considerably more
method about the proceedings on the Allies’
side than was the case in previous opera-
tions. Gaps are being bitten into the
enemies’ lines here and there, the corners
knocked off and the biting resumed at a
fresh place. The result has been a gradual
general advance into the old-time battlefield
of the Somme, with the British troops imme-
diately south of Arras, on ground that was
bitterly contested before Flindenburg’s
well-remembered flit of a couple of years ago.
The arithmetic relating to the capture of
German prisoners has been getting rather
mixed lately, but, even after allowing for
duplication and repetition, it seems to be
clear that no such hauls-have ever before
been made, and that the volume of war
material and number of guns taken make a
record. If the reported accounts of the pro-
ceedings on Haig’s front are reasonably
correct, there appears good ground for sus-
pecting that, while the German is delaying
the advance, lie is busy preparing an anchor-
age for himself, and that it is into the old
Von llindenburg line he will subside for
the winter. Meanwhile north and east of
Boissons he is threatened with serious
trouble, involving the safety of the line he
now. occupies, thence along the Vesle to
Rheims. Some dramatic happenings would
be certain if the French make any substan-
tial advance in this quarter, since an enemy
retreat to the old line above the Aisne would
be jeopardised. A flaring up of the battle
along the Vesle and an intensification of the
lighting beyond Soissons are quite possible
happenings before many days have passed.
A couple of months of good campaigning
weather yet remains. The news filtering
through from Germany since Foch com-
menced hitting back is as unilluminating as
ever. The few juiceless snippets available
from time to time have a drearily familiar
sound and little, if any, meaning. This is
not a particularly encouraging sign, at any
rate. for people who comfort themselves with
the idea that the German is on the verge of
casting off the chains that bind him to the
llohenzolierns or that he is publicly
giving three jeers when Ludendobff ex-
plains when the next victory is going to
arrive. Yet though the solidarity of the
German people may remain unimpaired and
miracles of organisation may have been per-
formed, it would be incredible if, after spend-
ing four years on the bread-line and watch-
ing the evaporation of continual assurances
of overwhelming victories, the German citi-
zen was not finding the Fatherland a very
cold and cheerless place. And it would
be still more amazing if, after what has
happened this summer and contemplating
America’s uninterrupted arrival, the Kaiser’s
relatives are not beginning to reckon that
his wolfish thirst for gore ought to be nearly
satisfied.
The Occasionally Good Meat of Sydney.
The gruesome report of Bracket, K.C.,
Royal Commissioner on the subject of Syd-
ney's meat supply, reveals the existence of a
middleman who wasn’t taken into account by
the meat-fixer. The Homebusli abattoirs,
which represent the best part of a million
of borrowed money and show very small
cash results —some years they show no cash
results at all—are described as a very dirty
and diseased and expensive muddle. The
concern is new. but it seems to have been
born old. For lack of quite a lot of things
that such a place should have the amount
of good bullock that goes bad there is terri-
ble. The recognised waste in the meat trade
comes under a number of headings. There
is the loss on the station, which varies.
Outback, especially in drought time, it is
heavy, but in the worst places and the worst
years it never exceeds 100 per cent. There is
loss on the roads, especially on the long road
from North Queensland to Sydney ; loss in
railway trucks, especially if the way is long
and slow and hot; loss through condemna-
tion of diseased cattle; loss through the meat
that goes bad in the shops, particularly
when trade is slow and flies are fast. And
it appears that the lug abattoirs, which
should be above suspicion at the price, have
themselves gone bad. If retail prices are
based on the fixed cost at the saleyards and
reasonable fixed allowances for slaughtering
and distribution, while no allowance is made
for perhaps Id. per lb. that has to be paid
for stencli and decay, then there is a big
hole in the calculation. The Commissioner’s
report may be summed up that Monday’s
meat is very likely to be bad, having been
from 43 to 58 hours getting into a state of
decomposition; that the other days’ meat
hasn’t decomposed so long; that out of
1,000,000 sheep slaughtered last year 500,000
went bad in parts—the parts that are called
fries; that the chilling-rooms aren’t cold
enough; that the hot old-fashioned waggons
that carry around the food are accompanied
by blowflies, and so on. This loss can’t be
charged back to the producer, for the food
was able to stand on its four legs when he
sold it. So it is divided between the middle-
man and the consumer. The stench that is
so bad as to be unsaleable is the middleman’s
loss. The stench that is still saleable and
only reaches its complete development in
the kitchen-safe or the pot or the stomach is
the consumer’s loss. One thing is certain :
if the price-fixer got his ideas at a place like
Adelaide, where the abattoirs are up-to-date
and meat is delivered in insulated waggons,
and if he made arrangements for Sydney on
documentary evidence and without studying
the local blowfly, then he didn’t give Sydney
a fail- deal. The blowfly ought to be charged
to the working expenses of the abattoirs.
The Police and the I.W.W. Haul.
From a table in No. 3 Court:—
N. S. Wales Parliament having passed legislation
arming the Royal Commissioner with wide authority,
Justice Street was handed the job of inquiring into
the charges brought against the police in connection
with the I.W.W. arson-conspiracy charges, on which
a dozen men were convicted at Sydney a few months
ago and sentenced to long- terms of imprisonment.
The inquiry opened last week and the rush for seats
filled the court in record time. The evidence on
the first day made a full house certain on the second.
Pura, a tailor, was put into the witness-box and
explained some of the circumstances incidental t°
making suits for half a dozen detectives who had
been connected with the I.W.W. business, payment
therefor being made by the Goldsteins. As Davis
Goldstein had been mixed up witH the note-forgery
cases and the I.W.W. trials, and had made a sensa-
tional statement regarding the police, this testi-
mony of Pura’s had a tasty flavor. Next morning
the mail steamer arrived from ’Frisco, and when
Street took his seat the call rase: “Henry Christo-
pher Scully.”
H. C. SCULLY , chief witness at the Royal
Commission following the I.W.W.
convictions.
A little man, quietly waiting behind the barristers’
table, walked into the witness-box. He was, as
it transpired, to be there for 12 hours. This was
Scully, the pharmacist, principal witness for the
Crown against the men now in gaol, perhaps the
inost-talked-of man in Australia during the last few
weeks—a 6mall man of middle age, reddish and
slightly bald. Sea-ail- and sun had tanned his skin.
A citizen of the inconspicuous, indefinite type this —
quiet, plausible perhaps, and very careful. The kind
of man to he seen behind a thousand counters and
forgotten.
He takes the oath with prodigious solemnity.
Windeyer, K.C., appearing for Judd, Mutch, Brook-
field, opens with some questions about what hap-
pened at ’Frisco and leads up to the famous “state-
ment” furnished by Scully to Judd—the basis of
the charges and these proceedings. The statement
is read. It is an amazing document. In it Scully
traverses the evidence of every material witness for
the Crown at the trial —except himself. Windeyer
takes him over the ground in detail. Scully qualifies
and discounts everything. He insists that his state-
ments were not charges—they were mere suggestions
for inquiry, theories, comments, speculations about
what he then thought the whole truth might be.
He was not a literary man; the wrong word had
been used; the onus of proof was on Judd—and so
it went on, for hours, With Scully always ready
with an answer—never flustered, calm, deprecatory,
precise. It is not often a witness goes through
such an ordeal. It is seldom such a witness as
Scully is in the box. He stood the best part of
three days of it. At the end he nearly collapsed.
Little wonder.
Then came Davis Goldstein, whose statutory
declaration about the trial and the police was in
Windeyer’s hands. A different sort of customer this
—loud, truculent, intolerant of cross-questioning.
Goldstein is a Jew, stockily built, swarthy skinned,
with a forehead coming well down upon his counte-
nance. Not an ill-favored Hebrew by any means; he
looks like an Italian, but is not oue. Street warns
him that immunity from prosecution may depend
upon the view taken by the Commissioner of his
evidence. Windeyer suggests that the warning
might, perhaps, be given more fully. “There is no
necessity,” Goldstein shouts. “I’m here to tell the
whole truth.”
Windeyer: “Very well. Up to Saturday you were
ready to make a certain statement”
Goldstein: “Up to Saturday I was prepared to per-
jure myself.”
He says this at the top of his voice, shaking his
head with passion. Street warns him to control
himself, and repeats the warning several times later
on.
Goldstein -flatly denies having said various things
suggested by counsel. Coming to matters which
cannot be denied he declares them to have been untrue
—that his statutory declaration was a lying statement.
The Commissioner wants to know whether Goldstein
realises what kind of individual he is making himself
out to be. Goldstein says he does —and the judicial
eye rests coldly on him. The gravamen of Gold-
stein’s trouble is said by himself to be the fact that
he lost £4OO through the police failing to arrest
a person he had gone bail for after h£ had warned
them that this person was about to bolt. He speaks
hotly on the subject, and makes resentment justifica-
tion of his allegations against the police. ; A strange
witness.
Louis Goldstein, a brother, is called. Quite a
different Goldstein—more Hebraic, more deliberate,
a slow thinker. He weighs each question a long
time and answers slowly. There may be more of
him later. Meanwhile the original statement made
by Davis Goldstein, on which most of the cross-
examination turns, must not be published.
There seems a probability that this inquiry may go
quite a long way. There is certainly not much doubt,
anyhow, that the public is going to hear a lot yet
about the I.WAV. prisoners. Some of these men are
married. Their wives sit in the court listening to
the evidence with strained faces and mournful eyes.

“Bad Australian”: For several years past
the Japs have been very active in Argentina
and S. America generally, and since the in-
auguration of a direct line of their steamers
to Buenos Aires their business interests have
increased in a phenomenal manner, so much
so that Nippon has decided to found a per-
manent embassy in the Argentine capital.
Japanese goods and Japanese agents are now
to be found in all parts of the Republic, and
the little man with bis cheap and showy
stuff bids fair to oust the European and give
Uncle Sam a run for the market. The pre-
sent state of mind in official circles is
decidedly antipathetic to the British and
French capital invested in the country, as
witness the fatherly attitude towards the
murderers and incendiaries engaged in the
constantly recurring strikes on the railways
and freezing works. In the circumstances
it looks as if Japan is likely to become top-
dog in S. America, and considering how much
the two peoples have in common there
appears to be nothing to prevent a peaceful
fusion of the races. With the European the
case is entirely different—he is, and must
always remain, a stranger within the gates.
Let his political, religious or commercial faith
be what it may, lie can never become a South
American nor of the South Americans.
It is a joke at the expense of the great
natural resources of the country that music,
singing and elocution should be among the
things taught to returned soldiers who re-
quire a new occupation. They seem futile
and unproductive and overcrowded lines of
industry. Still, as we can’t get the wheat
and the meat away for want of ships, and
the Sydney match-factory and the principal
wooltop-factory have closed up, and many
gold-mines are doing the same because the
imported machinery is scarce and ruinously
expensive, and the woollen-mills are also in
difficulties because of the imported machin-
ery famine, and the knitting of socks has
almost ceased because the wool that was
sent to England to be made into yarn hasn’t
come back yet. and the tariff and the War
Profits tax kill new industries on sight—-
considering these and many other things,
time hangs heavily on our hands. So the
returned soldier may as well warble for an
idle hour about the boy on the burning deck
while he waits for some industry to start.
We haven’t the dec-k, pending the arrival of
the imported materials to make it with, and
there is lack of the match to burn it with, but
anyhow, we have the boy. He was last
seen selling the evening paper at a busy
corner.
“Mujik" : Iu the remote past I bet a curate
a new tile that before the war was over the
.voice of the Pharisee would be heard whis-
pering to the Democracy to help pay its bill
by having a game at Fiji Uncles with the
old-age pensioner. And 10, brethren, it has
been even so. Wherefore shall I journey
forth on the morrow to warn a ,meek and
lowly worker in the vineyard» to raise
14s. Gd. For has not Melbourne Age
(21/8/T8) risen up in horror to point
to “specific instances of the necessity for
economy,” and directed a shaky finger of
scorn at the invalid and old-age pension?
The method of the Age is heartful. It enu-
merates the nation’s vast debt and the expen-
diture on this thing and that. It wants to
know how much longer the jag is going to
continue, demands to be told whether the
distribut ; on of fat billets is to persist till
the last trump reverberates at Ooclnadatta or
somewhere adjacent, and pleads for economy
It goes into the pensions arithmetic to
show that the figures are leaping sky-high
Then, having finished with the poison-bottle!
it unctuously winds up :
We do not suggest here that in view of the
troublous times ahead these pensions should be abol-
ished or even curtailed, but we do submit that thev
are the strongest possible justification for the inline-
diate introduction of drastic economies in eveiv
branch of Government not connected with the war
It is no use disguising the fact that unless this step
is taken these pensions and many other of our
privileges will be in a position of the greatest
jeopardy. There will simply be a repetition of the
old story of the straw' which broke the camel’s back.
The implication here is that when the numer-
ous Governments on the premises land them-
selves in the pit they are scorching along the
read to, one of the ways open to them for
getting out will be to dump the old-age
pension. It is an idea so rotten that the
Argus ought to second the motion, and it
only wants to be harped on long enough to
encourage the politicians now in charge to
even greater profligacy. Fat grudges the old-
age pension, and always has done so. Give
him reason to believe that this can be
diverted to the sustenance of Capital if lie
only keeps on pawning and spending hard
enough, then he at once notices how t\Vo
birds can be assassinated with one stone.
Why, in the event of embarrassment at the
pay-out window, should it be considered that
old-age pensions had been the last straw?
Why should the unfortunate old pensioner
be hit to leg in order that interest-mongers
and Cocked Hats and politicians may get in
out of the wet ? Why should the obligation
to Capital have the force of a religious belief
and the moral debt due to the aged and infirm
be shed like ruined pants? Payment of old-
age pensions may be mere empiricism—a
poor substitute for real economic reforms. 1
think it is. At best the pension is no better
than *a ransom paid by the well-to-do to the
indigent. But it means a lot to the pen-
sioner, and its continuance is a debt owed by
the community just as definitely as if these
old people held it in sealed bonds. The Atm
can make up its mind that before that debt is
repudiated by the Australian people there
will be quite a busy day spent tidying lip.

A par ill B. 1/8/TB, relating- to the “aid” given
to inventors and inventions of promise recalls my
ease. My partner, a marine engineer, and myself,
a civil engineer of sorts, evolved a new thing in
. (The dash will explain itself towards iho
end of this epistle.) We put it to the Inventions
Board of S. Aus., and it was forwarded on to Mel-
bourne. Got provisional patent and waited until
we were tired. Went over to see about it. Found
that another man had also sent in something' similar,
but it was mechanically impossible. Our affair
was shelved. Appealed to Minister, who ordered a
review. I asked for outside expert opinion on the
invention and got it. Result, favorable. Mote
waiting-. More wire-pulling- to get iiway to Engiaiid.
Defence Department offered us munition-worker’s
passage. Accepted it. Then our motor unaccount-
ably falls to pieces although brand new, and we
missed the transport. More wait. But the case to.
several speculators, and they subscribed £3OO. At
last we g-ot away, g-oing via America. In New York
our luggage unaccountably went astray. We man-
aged to get the ship just in time; On board her several
of our letters were stolen. Arrived in England to
find that two days before we left Aussie another Chap
had collected a big sum for same principle as ours,
although his idea was not so simple. My partner
was given a commission in R.N.R. and I, previously
holding a commission in A.I.F, but knocked out iii
France, was given a position in a famous laboratory.
Wound troubling- me again, I was- given a munition-
worker’s passage back to Australia with injunctions
to hold my tongue, lienee the dash.
Killed last week on his fourth day at the
Western Front. Captain Richard Seddon, of
M.L., eldest son of “King” Dick. In all
respects young Dick was the antithesis of
his tumultuous dad. When the Boer war
broke out and sent the old man clean off Ids
head, son Richard, then decorating a stool in
a warehouse, was handed it commission and
went to the veldt. lie came back with several
medals and the recollection of having been
an A.D.C. to Roberts. Rater on, he became
the plaintiff in a libel action against the
now-dead Tommy Taylor, at that time a
vehement political opponent of Premier
Seddon and a prominent lime juicer. Taylor
having allegedly impugned his qualities as
a soldier. Young Seddon won the case, but
the proceedings were very injudicious, He
remained in the Defence Department, and sub-
sequently proceeded to England for training
at Camberley Staff College. Returning to
M.L., he found the old man’s opponents in
charge, and when they grudgingly offered
him a minor job in the Backblocks he told
them to go to blazes or somewhere, and sub-
sided into an ordinary suit of clothes. For
a long time his offer of service in the exist-
ing rumpus was rejected on the ground of
having thrown clods at discipline, but early
in the year the dead past was given decent
interment. Old Dick was a massive, mas-
terful person with a roar. Young Dick was
spare of build, pale and soft-voiced. lie was
about 40. His two brothers, Tom and Stuart,
have been at the Front this long time.
