Auditorium (Aug. 31) Leila Doubleday
THE INVISIBLE FOE.
Now, Miss Polini’s daddy dear a pair of nephews had:
Young Hugh (the one she loved) was good, the other
bloke was bad.
The bad one wore an evil smile when telling wicked
lies;
To indicate lias nature vile lie rolled his pencilled
eyes.
Besides, his name was Stephen, which, since drama
first began,
Has mostly stood for proof of sin and wickedness in
man—
The world’s respect all Hughs may win; a Stephen
rarely can.
Ten thousand pounds of petty cash the godless
nephew “shook,”
And very blaCk ’gainst brother Hugh the evidence
did look.
From uncle’s mansion in disgrace the good young
nephew slunk,
AVhilst Miss Polini hid her face and truly sad was
mine.
On Hugh, he knew, his daughter’s hopes of happiness
were built;
So, when he spotted Stephen’s crime and proved it
to the hilt,
He made the villain write a full confession of his
guilt.
Between two pages of a book the paper he concealed
(The book it was a copy new of “David Copperfield”),
And then, a sadly stricken man, he tottered round the
room—
The crime that Stephen had confess’d spelt ailing
uncle’s doom—-
And, ere of Hugh’s sweet innocence his girl he could
acquaint,
He staggered off in deep distress, like one about to
faint,
And, oh, in the adjoining room he (lied of heart
complaint.
Well, there you are! Dear daddy dead ; the heroine
in grief;
The villain letting folks suppose his brother was the
thief,
Yet very anxious all the time, obsessed by dark
intent
To search the corpse’s sitting-room and pinch that
document.
He did not dread the buried dead, he only feared
the quick:
Yet something, something in that room turned
Stephen’s tummy sick;
It was, as Miss Polini knew, the Spook of uncle
Dick.
Invisible to mortal eye, in vain the worried Spook
Directed poor Miss P. to try to find the Dickens
book.
She felt the Presence striving hard to tell her
what to do;
She felt It wanted her to prove the innocence of
Hugh.
But neither Hugh nor she could read the Spirit’s
hints aright,
AVhilst, as for Steve, he stared aghast, half-paralysed
with fright,
And dared not search dead uncle’s room, except in
broad daylight.
At last —at last the long lost truth was quietly
revealed.
The Presence made the girl repeat some lines of
Copperfield:
A doctor took the volume down, and, prompted by
the Spook,
The cat leapt lightly from the bag, or, rather from
the book.
The wicked nephew cried “My Gawd!” and vanished
from the show,
And Miss Polini thanked the Spook in accents sweet
and low—
To her a friend invisible, to villainy a foe.
* * * *
If “The Invisible Foe,’’ staged at the
King’s (Melb.) last Saturday, is an English
play it serves as an indirect apology for some
of the American plays lately introduced to
us. They, at least, moved rapidly in all
eases and were bright, as a rule. The Taits’
new drama is slow of movement, dull in
spirit, clumsy in construction, conventional
in characterisation and crudest in its comic
relief. And it has the audacity to demand
an almost impossibly-strong performance of
the unfortunate villain, who stands convicted
at the outset of reckless fraud, and is
subsequently required to show all the terror
of Richard the Third without the assistance
of a A’isible or audible spectre. Cyril
Maekay’s undisguised methods of melodrama
missed the ’bus in this trying situation. His
lurid sneers, obtrusive dissimulations, theat-
rical gestures and general loudness of style
intensified the crudity of the author’s con-
ception and produced titters instead of
thrills. Miss. Wilton rather over-emphasised
lier comic-relief points in the festive charac-
ter of a husband-hunting widow, and the rest
of the acting, with the exception of Maurice
Dudley’s safe and stodgy confidential clerk,
was a' trifle on the underdone side. The end
of Act 1 provided one convincing moment in
the performance. Harmon Lee, as a stereo-
typed elderlv uncle suffering from awful
pains at the heart, staggered artistically into
the next room, and the sound of a falling
body and a rush of alarmed persons to the
catastrophe was adroitly stage-managed.
After which Miss Polini received a gentle
intimation from l)r. Kay Souper that her
daddy was no more. Whereupon she said :
“He will want me near him,” as she rose to
go. Just then the supposititious corpse in the
imaginary next room seemed a cold, solemn
fact. The beautiful voice of Miss Polini
and her naturalness in sitting down to think,
or standing up to say something, made her
interesting in her spook-haunted part. But
she didn’t tend to accelerate the pace of a
slow-going piece.
* * * *
“The Thirteenth Chair,” at the Royal
(Melh.) seems to be giving large audiences all
the mystery they expected. The stage is merged
into the auditorium what time darkness
reigns throughout the house, and something of
an impression of having joined the stance
circle remains with the public to the end.
They feel like paying-guests of Gaston Mer-
vale, the convincing host, and would be
hardly surprised to hear that lan Maclaren
got his death stab from an irate fiddler, who
had objected to Mac’s undue assertion of
himself, and crept up from the orchestra to
reduce him to silence. Also they would like
to have another dark seance, with the arro-
gant detective in the 13th chair, so as to give
the murderer one more chance to commit a
capital offence.
“Katinka,” the melodious opera at Her
Majesty’s (Melb.), will be entitled to call
itself The 13th Week on Saturday. The
31st instant will he Katinka’s last Saturday
night. “Oh, Oh, Delphine!” is billed for a
week later.
At the Tivoli (Melh.) the news of a
change of revue next Saturday is thankfully
received. There was no fault to find with
“Time, Please,” in its earlier days and nights,
hut in old age it, grew monotonous. Age
seemed to stale the infinite variety of the
show, or perhaps its variety was insuffici-
ently infinite. “Honi Soit” comes to the
rescue this Saturday, and a new principal,
John Junior, will help Barry Lupino to
make things go the pace.
The Bijou (Melb.) has staged a new first
part called “The Cave of Shells.” Chas.
Pope and three black-faced solemnities hold
down the end seats in this unpretentious
seance. The second part is stronger for
the appearance of Daisy Harcourt, who has
put a hard Yankee finish on an unimpressive
voice. Fred Bluett is also present, and his
riotous fun is a welcome antidote to the
tedious Sa-Hera, who is still posing as an
occult fount into which the credulous can
dip for information.

One of Melba’s prize canaries, Peggy
Center, was the principal Chappell trophy at
the Auditorium (Melh.) last Saturday night.
Peggy’s programme was decorated with the
confectionery of Guy d’Hardelot, Liza Leh-

from “The Shropshire Lad” in an equally
confident fashion. Tenor Percy Blundell
was not so tasteful an entertainer as a com-

Mary Pickford is suffering spanks and
promiscuous charity, whilst unknowingly
heiress to a diamond-mine, in “The
Little Princess,” at Hoyt’s (Melb.) this
week. The film is based on a yarn by
Frances Hodgson Burnett, but Mary man-
ages to translate Frances’s artless' humor
into some spirited fooling. “Plis Mother’s
Boy,” which keeps close to “The Little

Hoyt’s Lyceum .Vi a y Pickford travels ahead
of Miriam Coopei in a plot called “Be-
trayed,” which looks like one of O. Henry’s
offspring brutally maltreated by a cruel
stepfather.
The Britannia (Melb.) is the current
refuge of “The Beast of Berlin,” and the
management has looted the dictionary of
opprobrium to describe the monster. The
film professes to reveal every “infamous
secret” of the tyrant’s court, and certainly
suggests that it must have been an asbestos
camera that gathered the information. At
the Melba, last Monday, there was a more
attractive menu with Charlie Chaplin in
“The Cure,” and Kitty Gordon in “The
Purple Lily.” To-day (Thursday) Earle
M illiams and Grace Harmond get into close
touch in a new Vitagraph,offering, “The Seal
of Silence.”
Abdy’s boxing kangaroo is a pleasant cir-
cumstance at the Tivoli (Adelaide). A sport-
ing audience admires to see-him act according
to Queensberry rules, and when ho gets
excited and forgets them he is admired more
than ever. Baron, a good ventriloquist, adds
to the strength of the show, and Leonard
Nelson is as popular as ever. As for Le
Blanc and Mack, they are the same old pair
of improbable Hebrews who are steadily
taken down for their good cash as no sons
of Abraham in real life ever were. The

“S’niee,” the revue which began at the
Tivoli (Sydney) on Saturday, promises to
be a success, though it would be a better
show if the management had a good chief
comedian about the house. Eve Kelland, the
new Australian leading-lady, is large and
fair, with a light contralto voice and a quiet
method. She is an experienced comedienne,
has personality, and took charge straight
away. Featherweight Fayette Perry, who is
like a nice little girl grown larger but not
grown up, provided a dainty contrast. She
scored heavily in a poster-kid song with
four clever Tivoli children. Espinosa (pro-
ducer), FI. de Robeck, Chris Wren and
Charles Albert attended by turns to the
comedy; but that department never whizzed
except in the mock bull-fight, where the rag-
ing panto, beast was knocked out by having
a bunch of onions thrust under its nose.
Leslie Gaze came back singing to the stage,
wearing a bright blue naval uniform and a
continuous smile that never flickered. Odds
are he still smiles like-that in his sleep.
Dancer Elsie Parkcs had a lot to do, and
the work of the girls who pirouette with her
seemed to indicate that Espinosa has been
teaching, busily. Still, Elsie has nothing
as distinctive as Ballets A and B of the
“Samples” show. Peggy Peate sang well,
but was a trifle heavy in her comedy work ;
and Harry Burns, a brisk stepper-, wasted
some time in a tiresome patter turn with
Eve Kelland as partner. The Clints and
Harry Whaite provided some notable cloths.
There was a dashing Alpine scene, but the
best effect was got with an elaborate pink
and grey color-scheme, in which the girls
even wore grey wigs to harmonise with their
surroundings. Will- Quintrell. .as usual,
attended to the music, and one excur-
sion on to the stage to play a piano accom-
paniment for Eve Kelland. It was very
tastefully done.
"BUSINESS BEFORE PLEASURE.” AT
SYDNEY CRITERION.
Abe (Jus. Waters) tries to explain to his
Rosie (Maggie Moore).
“Oh, Boy!” finishes its long run at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney) on Friday. Starting
on Saturday, “So Long, Letty,” will be
revived for a few nights.
“Business Before Pleasure” still goes
strong at the Criterion (Sydney). Profits
are good.
“Pierrot Pie” was partly re-made by Syd-
ney James during the week, and everybody
who had a slice of the original turn-out will
probably line up at the Playhouse (Sydney)
for a second helping. The double bur-
lesque. “The Masquerader in the Thirteenth
Chair,’” is still in the dish.
Guy Bates Post having held to his suc-
cessful season at the Palace (Sydney) till
Tuesday, “Gran’dad Rudd” had to make a
mid-week start on Wednesday night. The
Sundry Showman will have something to
say about the old pot next week.
“The Lure of the Bush,” the £5O prize
Australian picture, was screened privately
at the Australian Film House (Sydney) on
Monday afternoon. It deals graphically with
station life, and the photography is a credit.
The story follows the progress of an English
jaekeroo through the different: phases of his
education in the Sunny South. There is
shearing, droving and a real kangaroo hunt.
There are kookaburras and ’possums and a
woolshed dance. The hero (“Snowy” Baker)
and the heroine (Beta Tress) do some stir-
ring acts on horseback. Mr. Percy Reay,
a well-known Sydney pressman, is the
author of “The Lure of the Bush,” Franklyn
Barrett arranged the scenario, and Claud
Flemming was the “producer.” It is under-
stood that the film will be shown through
U.S.A.
:;s * * *
At the Strand (Sydney) Mae Marsh is
battling with the flotsam and jetsam of
New York while her lover is fighting for
civilisation on the battlefield. “In Fields
of Honor” is the title of the film. The Crys-
tal Palace has some wonderful scenery of the
Canadian backwoods, amidst which Kitty
Gordon flourishes as “The Purple Lily.”
Mary Pickford shakes her world-screened
ringlets from the sheets of Hoyt’s and the
Haymarket. “The Little Princess” is this
week's helping of Mary. Tb Lyceum has a
varied programme. “Who-. False Tongues
Speak” provides myste l . and sensation,
while Elsie Ferguson is the heroine in
Agnes and Egert.ai Castle’s charming
romance, “The Bose of the World.”
“Dry Gin” : When the ship Scammell went
smash on the Victorian coast down towards
the Otway, years ago, with a cargo of assorted
liquors and notions, one of the coastal
settlers buried his share of the firewater in
a paddock and sowed grain over it. He had
a. fine crop just coming on when someone
gave the show away, and the Customs officers
came down and dug up the goods. In the
days when shipwrecks were commoner on
that coast luxuries were cheap in the Otway
country. After the Eric the Red went to
pieces in the ’eighties there was free tobacco
for years along the coast nearly as far as
Warrnambool.

The Commercial of Australia during the
half-year bought out the little National of
Tasmania, which had its headquarters at
Launceston and had 15 branches, most of
them in the Speck’s north and west. This
purchase, the chairman told shareholders the
other day, “had given the bank a sound
and established business in fertile and pros-
perous parts of Tasmania, where they
desired to be represented.” The National’s
net profits for the past five years have
averaged about £9lOO each half-year, but its
assets were only taken over by the Commer-
cial on March 1 last.
The C. B. of A. had a particularly bad
time in the smash of the early ’nineties, and
the balance-sheet still carries the scar in
this way; Capital—llll,73s prefs. paid to
£lO. £2,117,350; 191,318 ordinaries paid to
10s., £95,659; total, £2,213,009. Then fol-
lows the line, “Deduct estimated deficiency
in connection with the Special Assets Trust
Co., Ltd., £612,660” —giving a net actual
capital of £1,600,349. In the clean-up the
bank could not write off fill its losses —there
was not enough capital to write off to cor-
respond. However, the depositors finally
accepted cum. pref. shares 3 p.c., to be
increased to 4 p.c. when things looked up.
This latter rate has been paid since June,
1914. The worst of the wreckage was
handed over to a separate co., the Special
Assets Trust Co., which finished its job about
a year ago. Any losses incurred by
realising on the old properties under book
value was made good from the bank’s profits,
and except for an almost regular £5OOO
written off premises, most of the amounts
shown as written off in the introductory
table have been utilised for this purpose.
Combining the operations of the Bank and
the Trust gives this table:—

From the profits of the last half-year £25,000
has been appropriated for “reinstatement of
capital,” and the report states that the
“realisation of further assets of the old bank
has increased the net actual capital to the
extent of £5000.” Six months ago £lO.OOO
was recovered in the same way.
The National of Tasmania balance-sheet
figures as at November 30 last were as fol-
lows :
Free deposits ..£389,994 Liquid assets . . £269,907

There were 39,050 shares paid to £5, and the
C. B. of A. offered £6 15s. per share, and
seemingly that was the price paid. But
apart from the purchase of the National and
the tacking on of its balance-sheet figures,
there have been some considerable move-
meats. Here is a comparison going back to
the last term before the war: —

The National added less than three-quarters
of a million of advances; most of the
remaining increases are explained by
advances made to customers to take up War
Loan and the financing of the Government
wheat scheme. Like all other banks the
C. B. of A. is proceeding very cautiously.
The chairman assured shareholders that
directors were “looking carefully into future
possibilities and making provision to meet
any depression which might be experienced
later on.” However, the financing of the
wheat and the War Loan have had a big
effect on the proportion of liquid assets com-
pared with liabilities. At June 30, 36 p.c.
of the total liabilities was held liquid, com-
pared with the large proportion of 48 p.c.
six months ago, and almost 49 p.c. a couple
of months before the war. However, the
position is apparently very sound, and, as
will be seen by the growth of deposits, the
bank has made good progress during the past
four years.
* * * *
The scheme for the amalgamation of the
National Bank of Australasia and the
Colonial Bank has been definitely and unani-
mously decided upon by the two manage-
ments, and, the Court having approved,
shareholders will shortly be called to-
gether to discuss the proposal. The present
authorised capital of the two banks is:
National, £3,407.904 ; Colonial, £737.931 10s.;
total, £4,145,835 10s. Paid capital is made
up in this way:—
National.
30,578 fully paid £lO prefs. £305,780
238,488 £8 ords. paid to £5 1,192,440
— £1,498,220
Colonial.
31,184 £9 15s. prefs. fully
paid .. .. '..£304,044
77,278 £4 ss. ords. paid
to £1 15s 135,236 10/-
'439,280 10/-
Combined paid-up capital . . . . £1,937,500 10/-
The pref. capital in both banks is prefer-
ential as to dividends only, and this only
up to 5 p.c. It is proposed to merge the
two banks into one, retaining the name
of The National Bank of Australasia.
Ltd., with a paid up capital of
£2,000,000. Colonial shareholders are to be
given shares in the National in exchange
for their present holdings; the prefs. receiv-
ing one fully-paid £lO share for each £9 15s.
Colonial share, and the holder of every 20
Colonial ords. (paid to £1 155.) will receive
seven new National shares (£S each and paid
up to £5). This relieves the holder of present
Colonial ordinaries of an uncalled liability
to the extent of £1 9s. per share- Tim
National pref. holders are to get a cash
bonus of £1 per share to adjust existing
relative interests and partly in considera-
tion of the proposal that after the amalga-
mated bank has paid six half-yearly divi-
dends at the rate of 8 p.c. p.a. all' prefs.
shall then surrender their preference rights.
After these transfers, the capital of the new
National Bank will be: —
61,762 prefs. fully paid to £lO ~ £617,620
265,536 ords. of £8 paid to £5 .. 1,327,680
£1,945,300
The new bank will then issue a further 238
£lO pref. shares, and offer present holders
10,464 new ordinaries at par. This will
give the total paid-up capital of £2.000.000,
while the nominal capital will be £5.000,000
(88,000 £lO prefs. unissued and 161,500 un-
issued ords. of £8 each). As at March 31
last, the National had reserves and undivided
profit amounting to £718,302, and the
Colonial £309,461; and it is now announced
that the amalgamated bank will start with
reserves of £1,000,000, apart from any sur-
plus profits made during the current term.
Thus, as The Bulletin expected, after both
managements had looked over each other’s
assets, there has been no writing down. The
National has 165 branches and 50 agencies,
about half in Victoria; and the Colonial 70
branches and 30 agencies, all in Victoria,
except for the London and Sydney branches.
Both banks are at present operating in
almost a score of Victorian towns, so besides
the added strength secured by the amalgama-
tion, considerable economy should be effected.
The principal balance-sheet figures of both
banks and their totals as at March last were
as follows:

Colonial chairman Bowes Kelly and F. G
Clarke on the new National Board, the
remaining three Colonial directors being
handed £2OOO each as a sort of solatium for
the loss of their jobs.

1916 war-time profits tax £3535 ) 12,480
During the past year the co. was called upon
to pay £3535 war-time profits tax on the
1916 profits. The directors did not antici-
pate having to pay anything approach-
ing that figure, and consequently the
profits for the past year have been
Severely cut into, and the directors
have had to go to the bank to help them
out. Going back to the 1916 figures, and
deducting this £3535 tax. it will be found
that the net profits for that year were £4949,
and that the 20 p.c. distribution to share-
holders (£6410) was not earned by a long
way. If the directors had known at the
time the intentions of the Government in
the matter of the war-time profits tax they
could not have considered for a moment so
large a distribution to shareholders. As it~
is, the politicians have humbugged cos., until
they don’t know where they stand in the
matter of taxation. As the table shows,
this woollen co. has had a hard struggle. In
1907 half the capital was written off as lost,
and since then, and including the recent good
years, the dividends have averaged only 44
p.c. p.a. on the written-down capital. How-
ever, war contracts and the extra business
that has come in as a result of the Protec-
tion afforded the industry by Wilhelm’s tin-
fish have given the co., a big lift. The sales
figures have moved this way:—
1908 ..£26,024 1915 ..£46,110
1912 .. 30,652 1916 .. 68,134
1913 . . 30,678 1917 . . 51,426
1914 . . 33,364 1918 . . 50,145
Here is a comparison of tlic balance-sheet
figures:—■
1908. 1914. 1917. 1918.
Capital .. .. £23,339 £23,339 £37,500 £37,500
Liabilities.. .. 25,248 15,929 6,422 11,044
Buildings, etc. . . 10,500 9,629 15,072 14,902
Plant, etc 22,332 21,548 22.558 24,578
Stock . . . . 12,908 12,868 12,943 11,103
Book debts . . 4,974 8,212 8,714 12,068
Since the war began £]4,l(>l of new capital
has been got in, and liabilities have been
reduced by £4SSS. Meantime £B3OB has been
added to land, buildings and plant and £2091
to the more or less liquid assets. So, after
paying dividends averaging about 11 p.c. p.a.
the co. is only a little over £lOOO better off
than it was when the rush of orders started
to come in four ago. The position may
not be actually as good as that, for the war-
time profits tax may take a cut out of last
year’s profits, and, further, everything
depends on the valuation of the plant, some
oi which has been bought at war prices, and
the amount of depreciation allowed. Alto-
gether, the history of this co.’s efforts to
establish the woollen manufacturing indus-
try at Ipswich does not make cheerful read-
ing. Certainly it is doing better now than
it has ever done; but there’s no guarantee
as to the future. With the present hope-
lessly inadequate tariff, the importer of goods
from cheap-labor countries must come into
his own again after the war if the politicians
don’t do their duty to the Commonwealth’s
industries.

The new chairman. Sir Charles Mackejlar

plains the leap in profits this way:—
The increased profit is actually due more to extra
business than to larger gross profits. Compared with
our turnover 10 years ago, the sales have more than
doubled, while the percentage of gross profit has
increased slightly over 2 p.c., the prices in this
market being consistently lower than in England
and America at corresponding periods. This higher
percentage cannot be considered as in anv way an
excessive advance in profit in view of ' the ‘ verv
considerable risks that we were obliged to take in
making heavy purchases at the present inflated prices,
and it only gives a moderate return for the extra
labor and capital employed and the risk of loss.
The co. during recent years has gone in
extensively for manufacturing instead of
depending upon overseas products, and its
up-to-date factory at Chippendale is turning
out a wide variety of goods. During the
year £25,000 of new capital lias been got in,
and the old debenture debt for a similar
amount has been extinguished. Here is a
comparison of the balance-sheet figures over
a period of live years:—
, 1913.. 1917. 1918.
Ca P ltal £75,000 £75,000 £lOO,OOO
Debentures .. .. 25,000 25,000
Other liabilities .. . . 24,569 37,751 38,719
Freeholds and plant .. 20,106 18,158 17,882
Stock 72,130 75,034 84,971
Book debts .. .. 47,691 59,659 57,526
Cash investments, etc. .. 3,135 11,149 10,502
Stocks are substantial, and no doubt most, of
it lias been purchased at abnormally high
prices. In the balance-sheet they are in-
cluded “at cost or under,” and, particularly
in view of the good time the co. has been
having, and also not forgetting the Board’s
conservative policy in the past, reasonable
allowance has doubtless been made to meet
any possible slump in prices. Plant, etc'., is
apparently also well written down, for
though many additions have been made dur-
ing the past few years, freehold, plant and
fixtures are now given a book value of
£2224 less than five years ago. After paying
a regular 10 p.c., the co. has improved its
position by at least £ll,OOO during the past
five years.

The metal contents of the concentrates was
14,366 tons lead, 531,7660 z. silver and 1603
tons zinc; and the recoveries were 74.6 p ; c.
lead, 52.4 p.c. silver and 8.6 p.c. zinc. Tin-
whole of the zinc tailings are handed ovbr
to the Amalgamated Zinc (De Bavay’s), who
divide profits with the South. The quartz
tailings, in which comparatively little metal
remains, are sent underground again for
filling. The whole of the 19,136 tons slimes
were handled by the lead section of tin-
selective flotation slime plant, which pro-
duced 2773 tons concentrates containing no
less than 78.4 p.c. of the lead contents, 84,2
p.c. of the silver and retained only 8.2 p.c.
of the zinc. Six months ago manager Wain-
wright explained that experiments with tin-
completed zinc section of this plant had
proved that an excellent grade zinc concen-
trate could be obtained from the de-leaded
slime residues at a reasonable cost and with
good metal recoveries. But as there is a
very limited market for slime-zinc concen-
trates it was decided to utilise the plant for
treating the old slime dump and obtaining
a lead concentrate for which there is a
ready market. A start was made early this
year, the slime being broken from the dump
b.V hydraulic jets and pumped to the plant,

out at about 15s. sd. for every ton of ore
mined. No statement of ore reserves is
given this half-year, but six months ago it
was announced that development had kept
pace with ore extraction and that reserves

extraction since June, 1911, has been 285,000
tons a year.

The June, 1918; accounts will probably be
the last presented by the South as a no-
liability co. The reconstruction scheme out-
lined on this page a few weeks ago will be
placed before shareholders on Aug. 30, and
it confirmed the co. will make a fresh start
as from July 1 last as the Broken Hill
South Ltd. Present paid capital of £163,727
is comprised of 130,90 S fully-paid £1 shares
and 69,092 contribs. paid to 9s. Gd. To
bring both shares into line it is proposed to
hand the holders of fully-paid £1 shares
10s. 6d. per share from the assets of the
present co. (=£68,727). That will reduce
the present capital to 200,000 shares each
paid to 9s. 6d., or a total of £95,000. For
each of these shares four fully-paid £1
shares will be allotted in the new co.. which
will have a capital of £BOO,OOO (800,000
shares of £1 each). The difference between
the new capital and the old (after the
repayment of 10s. 6d. to the fully-paids) is
thus £705,000. The reserves as at June
30 last were £781,470. made up in this way:
reserve fund. £395,000; equalisation reserve,
£73,955; undivided profit, £312,515.

The directors of the South Brisbane Gas,
the co. whose affairs were recently the sub-
ject of a scathing report to Parliament by
the Government Chief Audit Inspector, in a
letter to the Brisbane press state:—
We not only deny but assure the public that we are
in a position to refute every accusation made or
inferred against us in the report. Everything we
have done has been dorre properly and in the best
interests of the company.
They further say :
As these accusations have been made against us
publicly, and as we have no means of obtaining
redress by an action for defamation, we are forced to
make our defence before the public. As soon as
we read the report in the press we intended doing
this, but without the documents referred to in the
report we could not do so. Those documents were
not laid before Parliament with the report; they
have not yet been laid before Parliament, and so
far we have not been able to see them. Until we
do so we are unable to make our full defence.
Well, The Bulletin sincerely hopes, not
only for the directors’ sakes, but also for the
shareholders’, that they can satisfactorily
answer the charges made. What interests
shareholders and this paper more than any-
thing else is: what is the present actual
value of the co.’s buildings, plant, etc., and
how does it compare with the balance-sheet
figures? First of all, the Gas Referee fixed
the amount invested in the undertaking at
£160.051, and the co.’s balance-sheet showed
assets worth £318,588. The Government
Chief Audit Inspector, on the writing up
of assets by £lOO,OOO in 1914, reported that
“the assets were not in the business to war-
rant the issue” of the £lOO,OOO bonus deben-
ture stock. The directors say they are “in
a position to refute every accusation.” Well,
for a start, what about settling this valua-
tion question without delay and definitely
proving to the shareholders’ satisfaction that
the assets are worth every penny shown in
the balance-sheet?
While;the unwatering scheme on the Hill
End field (N.S.W.) is proceeding the Mar-
shall brothers, following the advice of Gov-
ernment-Geologist Harper, are bagging 3oz.
stone in a series of crosscuts. The full
strength of Harper’s interim report on the
field shows that the expert agrees with the
widely-held view that something worth while
could always be expected at the junction of
two geologically distinct formations that
wander all over the leases. That is why the
Reward mine has hung such a lot of faith
on the intersection of the present drives go-
ing north and the big Robert Emmett cross-
course, lying at right angles. But the big-
body of water on the other side of the cross-
course has been too menacing. Hence the
scheme fdr getting at the natural dam from
a much lower level. Meanwhile the gold
taken from the meeting of the minor reefs
is more than paying expenses. Amalgamated
Hill End, which retains the working of the
mica vein, is putting through 40 tons of
stone, expected to average 3oz. The Reward’s
last fortnight run returned 46 ounces.
The old Brown’s Creek mine, which lias
had as varied a history during the past 40
years as any other gold proposition in N. S.
Wales, is again the subject of litigation.
After the mine had absorbed tens of thou-
sands of hard cash in efforts to beat the
water, business was suspended some years
ago so far as actual returns were concerned.
Recently the leases were cancelled on the
ground of failure to maintain labor con-
ditions. On the date of cancellation —July
39 —and within one minute of the hour 6f
gazettal, James Bulloch, of Blayney, with
three others, armed with four watches and
any quantity of trenching apparatus, pro-
ceeded to mark out the boundary of 10 acres.
But A. A. Lycett, of Balmain, has asked War-
den Burke to declare Bulloch’s application
void on several technical grounds, including
inaccuracy in pegging out. A heap of evi-
dence has been taken in the case, but decision
has been withheld pending the visit of the
Warden to the field.
New Baker’s Greek, Hillgrovc (N.S.W.),
is collecting its second shilling call. Engine
troubles have caused some delay in starting
the battery, which is to be fed with 200 tons
stone per month for a start. When treating
400 tons per month the manager figures a
profit of £BOO, if the yield averages SOdwt.
per ton. Development included 500 ft. of
driving and winzing last half-year. In driv-
ing No. IS level to the south a crosscourse
has been met with, but though most of the
rich patches are near crosscourses there is
no invariable rule. No doubt there is rich
stone left in the mine, 'for the old co.
crushed 826 tons for 32070 z. in six months
before banding over the property.

Bendigo Amalgamated admits to a loss
of £58,841 from May 24, 1917, to July 2,
1918. This was because 132,351 tons sent
to the battery cost 325. 3d. per ton, includ-
ing capital expenditure, against an average
grade won of 225. per ton. If the tributors’
14,418 tons (which gave 69420 z.) be
reckoned in, then the battery crushed
346,769 tons for gold worth £161,906.
Moline’s costs sheet shows that money went
this way:—
Cost per ton.
s.
Development .. .. .. .. 5.07
Mining . . . . . . . . 5.97
‘Maintenance and surface super-
vision . . . . . . . . 5.09
Fuel .. .. .. .. .. 3.33
Mill and transport .. . . 4.48
Timber .. . . .. .. I.GB
Administration and drainage .. 3.87
30.09
Fault does not lie with these costs, though
they seem big. Maintenance and surface
supervision were high because with a swag
of shafts going, outgo was staggering. That
44 miles of prospecting and driving work
was done shows how the co. chipped along to
get on to gold. That pluck isn’t dead in.
Bendigo is proved by the 50 faces Dyason
is keeping going and his promise that if his
luck changes he will be working additional
ends so as to test fresh areas. His failures
are the Cornish, Unity, Hercules and Vir-
ginia ore bodies. Had that not happened the
battery would have handled 8500 tons fort-
nightly, instead of having to be kept down to
5100 tons. However, despite the big dis-
appointment, a feature of Dyason’s and
Moline’s talk to shareholders is their pluck to
recognise that the only way to battle
through is to keep going. One thing that
helps is the drop in costs, due to better
organisation and co-ordination during the
second half of the term, the result being that
the loss fell to £20,382 against £38,459 for
the first half of the year.
Though George Dickenson’s Al, Gaffney’s
Creek (Vic.), is a small doer, it yields good
profits. The past six months’ battery tally
was 6610 tons for £20,849 of gold, allowing
ss. per share to be paid in divs. As John
Till opened up a fair lot of ore from No. 9
to No. 12 levels, his reserves were saved
from shrinkage. Now he is being helped
further by the way in which the floor cut
in the rise above No. 12 level is opening out.
Costs have been stiff, roughly 365. tid. per
ton, but mining in the mountains always is
expensive. However, Till was lucky to be
able to keep his grade of ore at 655. for the
half-year.

Blythe River Iron Mines, Ltd. (Tas.),
is under option to the Commonwealth Gov-
ernment for £llO,OOO. Nominal capital is
£J ,000.000, and 500,000 £1 shares have been
issued and fully paid up. This capital is
represented by mining properties and rail-
way concessions, £469,993 ; mine expenditure,
£13,994 ; profit and loss debit, £10,556; rail-
way and survey expenditure, £4804; cash
and odds and ends, £4ll. One crowd, in
which were Wm. Jamieson, of the B. 11.
Prop., the late Fred Keats and Alfred Tol-
hurst, and Whitsitt, of the Speck, nursed the
show for a couple of decades. The Welsh
iron engineer, Derby, had a look at the
deposit, and though he gave it a reputation
as good as the best Swedish iron ore, nothing
came of liis report because the 14 million
needed to get things going was not forth-
coming. Japanese and Germans spent odd
moments having a look at Blythe River in
pre-war days. The B. 11. Prop, too, was
credited with eyeing the deposits as a pros-
pective second string to the Iron Knob. The
option is being taken up as a result of the
negotiations of the recently-knighted John
Higgins, honorary metallurgical adviser to
the Government, who, Acting Prime Minis-
ter Watt says, was offered the property by
the co. in his private capacity nearly a year
ago. So if the deal comes off Higgins is
to get a £lO,OOO commission cut from the
£llO,OOO “on the understanding that the
sum is to be invested and the income applied
to the promotion of the study of iron and
steel technology.”
A sensation was caused in mining circles
in Launceston (Tas.) lately when news got
abroad that a very rich gold show had been
discovered about 20 miles out, near Bless-
ing-ton, and close to E. Trethewie’s Whis-
loea estate. The ground is south of (the old
Burns Creek workings. I». 11. Whittle, a
well-known prospector, was the discoverer.
Assays from one portion of the lode went
4Soz. to the ton, but the average assays have
been between Goz. and Boz. A reef 12in.
wide has been opened up for five chains in
length. As high as 12oz. lias been assayed
from surface stone. The show is merely at
a prospecting stage at present, and further
developments are awaited with interest. Old
hands consider the lode to be a continuation
of the Golden Gate reef at Mathinna.
» * # *
Mount Bischoff (Tas.) made a profit of
£27.401 last half-year, which compares with
£7084 for the first half of 1917. Working
costs have risen to Bs. per ton crudes, and
Bischoff’s reserves are now valued at
0.29 per cent. tin. One-quarter p.c. tin is
worth from 15s. to 20s. at present prices;
but with tin at £2OO the value would be
about 10s. Just at present Bischoff is on
a very good wicket, as proved by the distri-
bution of £16,500 last half-year and the
recent increase in the monthly dividend to
7s. 6d. If this dividend can be maintained
while the boom lasts shareholders who
bought at £l5 will get a return of 30 p.c.
p.a. on their capital.
* * * *
W. Cawdry, the prospector who discov-
ered osmiridium in the serpentine rock at
Nineteen Mile. Creek a few years ago, has
made another discovery at Mt. Stewart
(Tas.), where there are many alluvial
miners working. The present find is claimed
to be very rich —one piece of serpentine
weighing 131 b. to 141 b. is reckoned to have
£5O to £6O worth of osmiridium in it.
There are said to be tons in sight, though, of
course, not much of it is as rich as the
samples brought in.
The North Mount Farrell co. has moved
its headquarters from Devonport to Mel-
bourne. The old Speck co., therefore, will
be wound up, and a Victorian co. created
with the same nominal capital of £50,000 in
80,000 shares of 12s. Gd. each. This trans-
fer is due to Melbourne having acquired a
big interest in the show.
* * * *
Having failed to make good with its
present holdings, Gwalia Consolidated is
now considering the purchase of some
adjoining shows at Leonora (W.A.). All
have produced gold at; some time or other,
but at present have little in sight and noth-
ing out of the way to recommend them at
depth. Others besides the Gwalia Central
are seeking their fortunes at this centre, but
nothing lias come their way so far; and, to
make things worse, the Sons of Gwalia
seems to be steadily declining. John Forrest
was the first white man to sight this once
prosperous field. That was in 1869, and lie
named the big rugged hill which towers,
above it Mount Leonora. He must have seen
the reefs that outcrop everywhere, but his
mind was on other things and he was
not a geologist. At any rate he made
no mention of them in his reports. For the
next 27 years the locality remained un-
known. Then a crowd of prospectors came
along from Coolgardie and discovered the
Sons of Gwalia and other mines.

Copper stocks are piling up in the Com-
monwealth, and the cos. are finding it difficult
to realise on their products unless helped
by very favorable contracts. Abercrombie,
Burraga (N.S.W.), which has been sending
concentrates to Port Kernbla for treatment,
has had to reduce its output. Wallaroo and
Moonta (S.A.) is also carrying heavy stocks,
and more shipping space is urgently "required
to relieve the position. The chairman even
hinted a few days back that the output
of the old show might have to be reduced.

Peter Waite, the veteran S.A. squatter,
believes in arranging his benefactions him-
self instead of leaving his executors to do it.
He has already given to Adelaide University
his house and park at the foot of the hills
close by, 230 acres in all, one half for a
public park and sports ground, the other half
for the study of scientific agriculture, xi
gift of that kind is not much use to a uni-
versity without funds for the working of it,
and Mr. Waite has now added to it Elder,
Smith and Co. shares worth about £60,000
to produce an endowment. As in the former
case, the gift does not take effect till his
death, but he has made a bargain with the
S.A. Government that, in consideration of
his public-spirited gifts, no death-duties shall
be charged when the time comes.
Professor Leslie Wilkinson, who lately
arrived from London to start Sydney Uni-
versity's new Architectural School, was
trained at the Royal Academy Schools and
peregrinated Europe as the Academy’s gold
medallist. He was Assistant Professor at
London University’s School of Architecture
for 10 years, and since the war began was
on the staff of the University Officers’ Train-
ing Corps. He was released from service
to take on the N. S. Wales job. He is 35
and an enthusiatic town-planner.
The Australian training-ship Tingira lately
got a recruit who, with ordinary luck, ought
to become an admiral at a record early age.
Young Forsythe enlisted with the A.I.F. in
Victoria when 13^ —he looked big enough to
be the requisite 18 —served for 18 months as
a machine-gunner in France, and was in-
valided home with shrapnel wounds in the
arm. That makes him fully 151!
Andrew McCormac, who died in Adelaide
last week at 92, was a popular portrait-
painter there last century. He
was an Irishman who started as
a Baptist minister, but preferred
art and literature. He wrote
a lot, in both prose and verse,
but will he remembered best by
his paintings of Speakers and
Mayors at Parliament House and
the Town Hall. The present
generation of public men was
hardly aware that he was still
living.
“Cobber”: Our repatriation
scheme is a bit more generous
than those fashionable last cen-
tury. In May 1834 Corporal
James Eller applied to Colonel
George Arthur, then Lieut.-Gov-
ernor on Van Diemen’s Land, for
a small grant of Crown land.
Eller’s honorable discharge
showed 21 years nine months and
seven days’ service, including 15
years O.A.S. He had fought his
way through all the disturbances
of his time. The battles of Sala-
manca, Valencia, Vittoria and
the taking of Burgos were among
his performances, and he was one
of the forlorn hope who scaled
the walls at the storming of San
Sebastian. He had another
rough-up at Bayonne, and butted
in at the final charge at Water-
loo. Subsequently he served
seven years in the West Indies
and was then detailed to \ T an Diemen’s
Land, where he cracked up. His applica-
tion for a bit of bush was refused, because
his Is. a day pension was considered to
be an adequate provision.
Wealthy Lionel Robinson, of the London
.Stock Exchange, is getting rid of a bit of
surplus cash by giving a clutch of scholar-
ships to Scotch College, where he learned
how to calculate profits before starting to
make them. Robinson began life as a clerk
with Clarke and Co., Melbourne, of which
the late (Alfred) Clarke was the king-pin.
Afterwards he became a jobber on the Mel-
bourne Exchange and later bought a seat
thereon, when seats were fairly cheap,
presently partnering with William Clark, of
Gorrie and Clark. The Westralian boom
shot the pair up into the higher financial
ether, and they have remained there ever
since, pillowed on rosy clouds of pi'osperity.
Brother Arthur Robinson is Vic. Solicitor-
General and Works Minister.
The name of C. A. Reinecke, auditor for
the Broken Hill Proprietary, will not be
seen again on the balance-sheets. With
a bulky body and a weak heart and the
strain of constant travelling to Port Pirie,
Iron Knob and "the Hill” in all weathers
he has not been "a good life” for some
years, and when a chill caught in Adelaide
turned to bronchial pneumonia he was
dead in 48 hours. Outside his work his
only hobby was music, and he owned some
really valuable old fiddles. He was born
in Adelaide 6S years ago, wenfc to St. Peter’s
College, aud had no interest in Germany
whatever. Indeed, he gave to the Allies’
cause his only son, George, of the Adelaide
Tramways Trust, who was killed in France
in 1916.
MAKING FOR HOME.

“Camelero”: It’s just about time that
someone inquired into the heroism of No. 2135,
Pte* A. G. Morrison, of the Imperial Camel
Corps (Australian Section). On December 17,
1917, Morrison did not answer at roll-call
and was posted absent without leave. It
seems that, wearying of Southern Palestine,
he had decided on an unofficial trip to
Europe to have a cut at Fritz. He got away
with a draft of reinforcements; but soon after
leaving the Egyptian coast he was placed
under arrest for desertion. After spending
Christmas in an Italian port he was placed
on a transport for return to his unit. The
vessel carried many English nurses, and
Morrison was the only Australian on board.
Within sight of Alexandria a torpedo struck
the ship, and in a few minutes troops and
nurses were struggling in the water. It was-
then that Morrison made good. He dived,
overboard to the rescue of an English nurse
and soon had her on the deck of an escorting
destroyer. Then he swam to two more nurses,
held them up in his arms and kept himself
afloat with his feet until help arrived. Later-
on he rescued six Tommies, and only allowed
himself to be hauled on board the destroyer
when there was no one else to save. Then
once more he became a camel-man under
arrest for absence without leave. He refused
to reveal his name, and as his papers were at
the bottom of the sea there was no means
of ascertaining same. The first official record
of Morrison’s bravery received in Cairo was a
statement signed by eight E.F.C. men to the
effect that the Anzac under arrest had saved
the lives of several nurses and men. Morri-
son certainly did not serve a sentence for
being absent without leave, but it’s up to
someone to see that his gallantry is
more adequately recognised.

“Bill Bowyang”: The fact is sometimes
forgotten that, the war correspondent to-day,
though he is behind the lines, runs much risk
in securing his “copy.” This is proved by
the experiences of Charlie Bean (Aus-
tralia), Malcolm Ross (M.L.) and others
on Gallipoli. On more than one occasion
when the boys were in tight corners Bean
volunteered to carry ammunition, and after
we had been holding an isolated post for 36
hours at Suvla Bay Ross was the first man
to risk a dash across a sniper-infested hill,
so as to get first-hand information. Here
in Palestine, Gullet (Australia) has had a
couple of narrow shaves from death. The
correspondents in this war are heavily handi-
capped. _ They are as courageous and as
enterprising as those who carried a note-
book in previous scraps. If allowed they
would dash to the front trenches, but they
are held back from the line of action, and
although they may be allowed some degree
of liberty to gather casual news they are
restricted for their general information to
the statements which the military authori-
ties are pleased to give them. They all
work upon the same material although they
treat it differently.

THE AIRMEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Aeroplanes—the army’s eyes that see, and its
beaks that peck t” —.4 tear-correspondent.
As high from the sweep of the cloud-line as the
clouds than the cities are higher,
We leave in the wake of.our winging a trail that is
fretted with fire!
Unseen from the greyness below us, where the
cannonades challenge and roar,
We ride on the stream of the sunlight, on the breast
of the cloud-wrack we soar,
Through the folds of the fog - in the lower, through
the glare of the uppermost spaces,
We drive with the blindness about us and the buf-
feting wind on our faces.
The stave that the singing propeller, that the wind
in the wires is humming,
Chants death to the junctions below 11s, the camp
that lies dark to our coming,
Ihe threat that we bring- shall you wake to, the
doom of your fattest dominions
With the beat of our engines’ approaching, the slant
of the silk in our pinions—
Fate s falcons that watch where you cower, down-
swooping, reversing and checking—
The eyes of the army for seeing, the beaks of the
army for pecking!
Far down on the white of the roadways, the green
of the meadowland grasses,
A serpent winds laboring onward —the brown of the
column that passes!
A thrust on the piloting-handle, the rush that
descends, and decreases,
The joy of the hawk o’er the quarry, the click of the
bomb that releases,
Your vapor-balls ringing me harmless, my answering
gunner’s derision,
Burst limbers and screaming of horses, tin- shreds
of the shattered division,
Torn fragments that cumber the roadway, rude panic
unloosed and unknowing,
AN EXAMPLE.
THE LADY: “Now, tell us. soldier boy,
what’s the strength o' this ’ere camou-
flage."
THE BOY : “Well, y' see these blue bands
on me arms? Well, that’s camouflage.
I’m camouflaged as a wowser."
Blipc! fear that goes hotfoot for safety, nor stands
on its order of going!
Swift, spies of your camouflaged cannon, your bough-
:woven screens that dissemble,
Keen hunters we haunt your reserve-lines, your con-
, voys and troops that assemble!
Small respite or rest shall we grant you, poor leisure
for ruing or recking—-
The eyes of the army for seeing, the beaks of the
army for pecking !
A speck on the rim of tlic ether, a mote on the
distant blue showing,
A formlessness merging and forming, a shapelessness
shaping and growing!
Drag back on the lever! Short mercy to him who
shall dally delaying,
Scant shrift to the one who waits under while the
foemen may stoop to the slaying!
The palm unto him who shall bear it—the race to
the speediest runner;
Fate compass the course lest they ram us —God steady
the gun and the gunner!
Gone past! And a bare yard between us ? Cross-fire
that still rattles and dwindles?
Hard down to the right, lest we lose him where his
rocking plane circles and spindles!
A shot through the tank —and to earthward, in
smoke and a smother of fire,
Goes reeling a crumple of fabric, a tangle of metal
and wire!
Winged prophets are we of disaster, red bringers of
ruin and wrecking—
The eyes of the army for seeing, the beaks of the
army for pecking.
N.S.W.
J. ALEX. ALLAN.

PENEOLDS
WINES
Although prospects look none too good
for the Hun there would still be hope
for him if he took to cannibalism.
Possibly he has done it under the rose;
certainly in the way of rape, mutilation, tor-
ture and burning people alive he has done
worse things. Organised cannibalism would
greatly increase the food supply, and thereby
make both the army and the civil population
more efficient. It would save a great deal of
difficult and expensive transportation, for
much victual would be on the spot even
during a retreat, though not nearly so much
as during an advance. It would give the
troops a new incentive to push the counter-
offensive ; they would advance with shouts
of “Victuals Ahead !” Desertion and surren-
ders would be reduced, for to leave the army
would also mean leaving the pantry, and
nobody desires to abandon a land of plenty
for a land of famine. The civil population
could be further “combed out,” fewer being
required for food-raising purposes, and there
would be a certain satisfaction in living on the
enemy, a device which the Huns have already
brought to some sort of perfection. Canni-
balism was certainly practised in Germany
during the Thirty Years’ War, which being a
religious war was specially violent, and it
lias existed in many places since till quite
recent times. Only one Australian bank man-
ager is supposed to have been eaten —Benja-
min Boyd, of the Royal Bank, who started to
build Boyd town on Twofold Bay as a rival
of Sydney, but was promptly snuffed out by
the One Big City influence. He met his
linish at Guadalcanal’ in the Pacific, where
the people were feeling hurt about some-
thing. One drawback about cannibalism is
its persistency. When once a person has
eaten a brother or a sister he never really
cares much for any other meat food. Another
disadvantage is that it retards settlement.
The law might restrict it in cities, just as it
does bushranging and cattle-stealing and
timber-felling without a license, but in the
great remote places where people could
least be spared, the habit, once acquired,
would be difficult to eradicate.
State-school teachers, who are about the
most valuable and the worst-paid public
officials that the Ma State possesses, grow
tired of being fed on the thistle of approba-
tion and the scant grass of compliment, not
to mention the dry husk of applause. In
other words, they think of inserting them-
selves under the Arbitration Act and secur-
ing a living wage from the Court. Nobody
has spoken with more enthusiasm about the
teachers than Education Minister James,
who off-handedly promised another £300.000
to Sydney University and laid the founda-
tion stone of a semi-detached brick cottage
the other day at St. Peters. So the idea is
to ask the Court to fix the teachers’ average
wage about half-way between what they get
and what Minister James says he is broken-
hearted because they don’t get, though he
adds that they ought to get even more than
that.
* * * *
The conviction of Henry Cook, on a
charge of helping to steal two bags of
money belonging to the Vic. Government
Printing Office, must have greatly disturbed
the nest of thieves in the Vic. Railways, but
it brought considerable relief to those Labor
members who want the railways’ vote, but
want it unsupported by “crook” doctrines.
Henry Cook was assistant-secretary of the
Railway Employees’ Union. The maxim that
pilfering is no robbery, especially when
operations were confined to the railways,
radiated from the school of thought which
lie adorned, and the school was tainting the
service. Every well-wisher of Labor must
rejoice at the object-lesson which the luck-
less bag-snatcher has provided. IJis convic-
tion will do something towards clearing the
atmosphere. A blow to Bolshevism, it should
enable the large majority of decent members
to organise a reform in their union control.
Cook was handed 10 years’ penal servitude.
* * * *
The spectacle of the' worker pushing
money away is interesting. At the sluggish
arms-factory at Lithgow it was proposed to
pay a bonus to employees every week when
the output exceeded 1000 rifles, which is
quite a humble quantity and not calculated
to glut the world’s market. The union took
a ballot, and shoved the likely money aside
by a large majority. The scheme savored of
speeding-up, and the sacred principle of go-
slow was endangered. Unless the Govern-
ment arrests the capable artisan and gives
him a bonus by force its scheme is dead.
Victoria’s unbuilt Smell City is a curious
business. Seven years ago it was decided
that noxious trades must be moved out of
Melbourne, so the Government acquired
2SOO acres, which wasn’t nearly enough, at
Laverton (131 miles distant), which wasn’t
nearly far enough away. But then the Gov-
ernment couldn’t screw up its courage to
take a fairly large population any further
away, so it fixed the Smell City there in
the hope that the capital would spread and
absorb it. Next, it couldn’t bear to tear
population away even that little distance,
and it let the site for grazing purposes, so
that tanners, fellmongers, etc., might not
go there. Now Melbourne is refusing per-
mits for new factories of various kinds, and
blocking profit and progress and employ-
ment, because it can’t stand any more Smeil,
and is too dog-in-the-mangerish either to
part with the old Smell or let any other
place have the additional Smell.

It has been enacted, very wisely, that red
flags and Hun flags and skull-and-cross-bone
flags must not. be flown in Australia without
permission. Now something might be done
about imported Australian flags. They are
a painfully erratic lot. The only reason why
any two of our imported flags are alike is
that the supply of blunders ran out.

“No. 2095” : Talking of conscription. The
Maoriland troops alongside our Light Horse
for the past six days have sent 60 men per
day back to a rest-camp, where they put in a
month recuperating. If they do not feel well
enough at the end of their month, they will
be given further time. As for the Aussies,
there are men here who have not been away
for 13 to 15 months, and are still hanging on.
For myself, I have been out for 10 months.
Anything doin’ at your end?

PEACE IN BETHLEHEM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The fine dust swam beneath our feet
Like seas of creamy slush;
AVe labored through the ruthless heat
Amidst the frantic crush ;
AVe grimly strove to reach the hills
Against the galling drouth,
AVhile torrid gusts increased our ills,
And smote us from the south.
The dark beset us like distrust,
No light of promise gleamed,
And like an adder in the dust
The writhing column seemed ;
The horses floundered through if all,
The waggons swirled behind,
Till we, beneath the choking pall,
AVere deaf and dumb and blind.
Each horse, it seemed, was nearly dead,
And shrinking ’neath its load,
When hoofs across the column-head
Assailed the mountain road ;
Then jaded “walers” ceased to grieve—
They pricked their ears to show
They’d bear their burdens all, and leave
The Jordan far below.
AA T e sniffed the fragrant mountain air,
The metal rang beneath,
And, like a blade, the old despair
AVas driven in its sheath.
Each man regained that goodly sight
Confusion ever mars,
And slowly, through the limpid night,
We rode beneath the stars.
The east awoke, and shyly fanned
The lifting sun ablaze,
And mellow gold caressed the land
That rang in olden days.
The Holy City seemed to rise
Above its ancient wall,
AVhile savage splendor laid its guise
A moment over all.
WORLDLY ADVICE.
PARISHIONER : "Do you think there should be
less extravagance in woman?”
THE CURATE: “Oh. no; merely less extrava-
gance in man.”
Then we rode out to Bethlehem,
What time a healing breeze
Made joyous song with twig and stem
Among the olive trees ;
And long before the sun had set,
AVe halted in a shade
That made us for a time forget
The cause of gun and blade.
Oh, peace was stealing from the west,
And stirring leaf and stem,
The day we laid us down to rest
1 u holy Bethlehem.
The grapes were clustered on the vine,
And all the thought of strife
AA as flooded with the warming wine
And happiness of life.

the sun-baked veldts of South Africa —on the snow-
clad Himalayas —in the sands of Egypt’s deserts, on
wind swept steppes of Russia and Siberia, in bush and
plain throughout Australia —all over the world, wherever
you find Motor Cars there also you will find the track of
the All Weather Tread.
Though Goodyear Tyres are sold in
every country in the world, they are
nowhere the lowest-priced tyres. Yet
the aggregate of Goodyear sales is
higher than any other single brand of
tyre. Motorists the world over have
realized that Goodyears give a lower-
cost-per-mile. It is this lower cost,
this better service shown by Goodyears
on all roads, in all countries, that has
made Goodyear the dominating tyre
value in the markets of the world to-day.
From whatever source Goodyear draws
its supply of materials, there a Good-
year man is on the spot, able at all times
to keep in touch with market conditions,
so that Goodyear can buy the best
materials to advantage.
Big production gives every scope for
lowering overhead costs in manufacture.
Money-saving machinery, standardised
manufacture, skilled specification, have
been fully developed in the produc-
tion of Goodyear Tyres.
Such a world market, such a universal
prestige, as Goodyear Tyres have won
is a double assurance to tyre buyers. It
is a prestige that must be maintained by
continued high quality. It is a market
that must grow by giving a uniformly
lower cost-per-mile on every Goodyear
sold. It is a reputation that must be
increased by the helpful, money-saving
service afforded by the many thousands
of Goodyear Service Stations and
Dealers scattered throughout the world.
Because Goodyear has a world-wide market, we can maintain extensive laboratories where all materials
are tested, new ideas evolved, experiments proven which make Goodyear a uniformly better tyre.
Branches & Agencies
all Principal Cities.
Service Stations and
Dealers Everywhere.

“M.”: The Australian doesn’t need his
clothes to make him distinctive; lie can be
picked, easily, even when in the slack gar-
ments of hospital, among an assorted crowd.
Shortly after I arrived at a casualty clear-
ing-station, where there were Canadian doc-
tors, American nurses. English orderlies and
a very mixed crowd of patients, the man in
the bed next to me said: “Aussie, aren’t you?
You can always tell an Aussie, somehow.”
This recognition tickled my pride, for I
didn’t consider myself so distinctly an Aus-
tralian ; but in my subsequent wanderings
through the hospitals I discovered that what
my neighbor said was correct, and the city-
bred Australian is as clearly hall-marked as
the man from the bush. The American in
pyjamas frequently does not place himself
until he speaks; the Canadian is much like
either an American (after he has spoken)
or an Englishman ; but the Australian shows
an easily detectable individuality. And, let
it be said, the Englishman who has been a
few years in Australia has his good share of
it. I can’t quite locate what the distinctive
quality is; but it’s there.
♦ * * *
Britain is trying these days to supply
not only itself but France, Italy and Portu-
gal with coal, and it is short of miners, be-
cause so many men have gone to the war,
and short of ships despite the hundreds that
it and its Allies are building. In fact there
is something like famine in view. In Aus-
tralia the Government has a scheme for
buying Coal with loan money and stacking
it up in order to keep the miners employed,
because it seems to have very little luck at
building ships. So it is missing the high-
priced markets for coal and wheat and meat
and ships and rabbits and other things all

“Tasso”: The finest elocutionist I have,
ever heard was a small boy encountered
recently on a Sydney ferryboat. I was
reading a paper when I became aware of a
thin, pathetic figure standing at my elbow
with a mass of very ugly crochet-work dis-
played in an open suitcase. “Will you buy
some, please?” I had all the parcels I
could carry and
very little change:
so I shook my head
resolutely. But the
small boy didn’t
budge. Presently I
had to cease read-
ing and look him
squarely in the eye.
“But I have no
father!” he sighed.
Observe that he
wasn’t so inartistic
as to say his father
was a soldier who
had died fighting
for me. He merely
implied it. The
odds are his
father was a healthy
bloke out on strike
for higher wages.
Anyhow, I had to
shell out and the
young pirate moved
on. It wasn’t until
he had carefully
worked every pas-
senger on two seats
that a lion-hearted
citizen boun d e d
downstairs and saw
the engineer about
it. Presently a
deckhand came up
and touched the
young actor on the
shoulder just as he
was delivering his
great orphan
speech to a poorly-
dressed little typ-
iste. He was taken
downstairs and we never saw him again.
Perhaps they threw him overboard. A
small boy with his acting ability doesn’t
need a father.

“The Oldest Inhabitant”: The Australian
Golden Jubilee of one of the many R.C.
teaching orders, the Christian Brothers,
comes on shortly. Three Brothers opened
the first school at St. Francis’s, Lonsdale-
street, Melbourne, in Bishop Goold’s time,
when the venerable John Fitzpatrick was
V.G. and the handsome and eloquent Rev.
G. V. Barry parish priest. There
are now 36 communities in Aus-
tralia and M.L., with 50 schools
and 15,000 pupils. The order
was founded in Waterford, Ire-
land, in 1802, by Ignatius Rice,
a wealthy commercial man, who
retired from business and de-
voted his wealth to educational
purposes in Ireland. And apropos.
St. Francis’s was the first R.C.
Church established in Victoria.
At the time that the Christian
Brothers opened their schools
there were two clergymen of some
note attached to the church. One
was a reverend father who, like
Cardinal Manning, had been mar-
ried and was a widower with a
daughter. The other was a
homely little man, Father Lorden.
who “did” the gaols, etc. In the
’sixties a felon who was under
sentence of death for stabbing
was in Father Lorden’s charge
spiritually. Great exertions were
made to save the man from the
gallows, and they were successful.
When the priest heard the news
he exclaimed : “Ah, he’ll never be
so well prepared again.” An
Irish laborer once waited upon
the holy man to make arrange-
mentg for his wedding. “Sure, , your
wife is only dead two months!” “Well,”
replied the applicant, “she’s as dead as ever
she’ll be, Father ■”
“Bill Bowyang” (from the spot) :
About to be issued with horses, so as to reinforce
the Light Horse in Palestine, the curtain goes down
on the Imperial Camel Corps (Australian Section).
Some day the history of this corps will be pub-
lished, and it will be one of the most thrilling
narratives of Australian heroism. When the Senussi
rose in Nor’-West Egypt in December, ’l5, the
military authorities, owing to the dry nature of the
country inhabited by the Senussi, decided to estab-
lish a Camel Corps, and the task of forming this
unit was left to Lieut.-Colonel Smith, V.C., of the
Egyptian Army. This officer asked for four com-
panies of Australians, who had fought at Gallipoli,
and who were than stationed at Tel-el-Kebir. Each
battalion was asked to supply a given number of
men, qualifications being that they must be of good
character and first-class rifle-shots. During February,
’IC, the first two companies left for Solium, on the
fringe of the Tripoli border, and the following month
the remaining two companies left for posts between
Alexandria and Mem Matruh. There being some
difficulty in securing Australians to form further
companies, English and Scottish troops formed an-
other six companies, and the name of the unit was
changed from Australian to Imperial Camel Corps.
Later on another eight companies of Australians
and Maorilanders were formed. The first four com-
WHAT WE HAVE TO PUT UP WITH.
“A bit lonely for us girls, this wan' game.”
“You’ve said it! Look at what is left to
keep us company."
panics arrived on the Nor’-West frontier in time to
see the last phase of the Senussi rising, but they
spent several months patrolling the Libyan desert.
It was weary work, and the men began to complain
at being in what they termed a non-combatant corps.
August, ’lO, saw No. 4 Company transferred west
of the Suez Canal, and they were the first lot to
face fire. They struck a warm corner at Romani,
and were only rescued in time by a Light Horse
unit. No. 1 and 3 Companies then arrived from
the Nor’-West and joined ip the big trek towards
El Arisli. At Maghdaba the Camel Corps made
good, and their fine charge was the talk of the
army for weeks afterwards. Following this
success they added to their laurels at Rafa.
It was at the second attack on Gaza on
April 19, 1917, that the Australians and Maori-
larders crowned all their previous achievements. Six
companies were detailed to charge 21 miles over
open ground and carry a strong Turkish redoubt.
A tank took the lead and the boys followed. Just
as it reached the enemy’s trench a shell struck the
tank and it burst into flame. Two camel companies
found themselves surrounded by Turks, the odds
being about ten to one. Both of these companies
were practically annihilated. The Turks called upon
them to surrender, but they refused. The English
newspapers stated that this heroic incident was per-
formed by British eamelmen. Every man who took
part in it wore the Australian uniform. The remain-
ing Australian and M.L. companies suffered severe
casualties trying to rescue their comrades. Anzac
veterans afterwards stated that this fight was the
worst that they had taken part in. The capture
of Beersheba and a few other posts in the Jordan
Valley saw the last of the Camel Corps in action.
This unit, considering its strength, won more honors
and suffered more casualties than any other unit in
Egypt or Palestine. There’s room in Australia to
erect a monument to this unique and heroic band of
Anzacs,
In England the dear duchess and the
others who can afford to keep dogs are
endeavoring to excuse Ponto’s existence by
shearing him and converting his wool into
garments for humans. There is a society
for promoting the commercial use of poodle
wool. The idea deserves encouragement, if
only because it affords weak man an oppor-
tunity of putting to a better use the hair of
the dog that bit him.

The Intelligence Officer was giving evi-
dence at the Seven Irishmen interlude in
Sydney, and Mack, K.C., was trying him
out:—•
Have you any information about Robert Emmett?
—Yes. I think he was killed in a motor accident
lately at Darlinghurst.
Having stopped this, Mack got his second
wind and tried another: —
About this Wolfe Tone we heard of. Who was he?
—A man who was mixed up in the Dublin rebellion
last year.
“Eye-witness” forwards this:—
He was a dinkum Billjim, and in the old days
punched bullocks in timber-jinkers on the North
Coast. His seething hot expletives would split the
hide of the biggest rogue in his team, and he could
pilot any number of the beast through the densest
timber country in the north. But there were no
bullocks to listen to his explosive vocabulary in
France, and the transport column found him a job
behind a team of mules. When I met him his old
rattletrap was bogged in mud and slime up to the
axle, and he artistically used his whip for 20
minutes without the slightest effect on his stubborn
half-breeds. Then he defiantly sat on the front seat
of his waggon, and, to give his arm a much-needed
rest, swore from 40 different angles. But the
obdurate beasts wouldn’t budge an inch, though
Fritz’s shells were splashing mud all over them in
unhealthy showers. In despair Billjim got down
from his box seat, affectionately stroked each animal
in turn, talked to them as only a bullocky can, then
suddenly jumped in front of them and piteously
pleaded: “For God’s sake, come on—your King and
country call you!” And Tommies and Frenchmen
who were watching the performance cheered the
quadrupeds as they lunged into the traces and set
out for Berlin, while a grinning Aussie took t.he
salute from the box-seat.

It being discovered that at least 400 young
men of Stepney (London) held bogus' mili-
tary exemption certificates, the matter was
traced to ft girl clerk, who said, in an
off-hand way, that she used to hand out
the forms and get chocolates in return.
Assuming the price was £1 per form,
which is little enough for forgery and trea-
son, it seems a very elastic girl who could
hold all that chocolate. Perhaps she took out
some of the money in motor-cars.

It occurred on the journey up to Pales-
tine. The occupants of the railway com-
partment were still suffering from the effects
of an arduous week’s leave in Cairo. In the
morning at Belah they compared notes on
the platform. The Major told one of his
friends that he had a most unfortunate ex-
perience during the night. The train had
stopped with a jolt, and he had been thrown
from the upper sleeping-berth on to the floor
with terrific force. His friend said: “For
such a fall, old chap, you don’t seem to be
knocked about much. Are you sure you
really did fall?” “Well,” said the Major,
hastily, “we’ll leave it at that. I thought I
did.” Just then a sub. joined the group and
graphically described an exactly similar ex-
perience. “Oh,” said the Major, much
relieved, “then it was you that fell out during
the night! I knew that somebody had come
down wallop!”

“Camelero”: Brig.-General Ryrie was in
his tent in Palestine one evening when he
hard a Billjim exceeding the cursing limit.
The General went outside and spoke forcibly
to the wrongdoer. Just as he was re-entering
his tent some bloke yelled from a dugout:
“You’ve both had a go, and I declare it a
blanky draw!”

ARCHIVES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
What matter if mv grave be low
Beneath Time-flattened grass and mould'-
The sun find wind shall find it out
And fingering my dust about
Will never let my heart grow cold.
What matter if the words you said
Sink back to silence? Loud and long
Some bird that was not dreamed of then
Will give its music forth to men
in sudden rhapsodies of song.
What matter if you should, forget,
You, who laid blossoms at my feet.
My spirit, stealing back, shall find
Old fragrances upon the wind
Because old roses once were sweet.
QUEEXSLAXI).
M. FORREST.

An “Adamax” Roof was put on this Building
because—the Architect knew all about the different types of roofing and, further, knew that the
man for whom he was preparing the plans was a particular man.
He had a big plant and he wanted it covered with a roofing that would give long service at low
cost.
Under such conditions the Architect knew there was only one choice, namely—to roof with
“Adamax” Roofing
because it gives longest service at a lower unit cost (the cost per sq. foot per year of service) than
any other roofing he could specify.
The Architect had before him the big orders that have recently been secured for “Adamax” Roofing,
and he knew that those “jobs” would not have been roofed with “Adamax” unless “Adamax” was
a roofing of a high standard of excellence.
Here is one order for “Adamax” —3500 sq. yds. ordered by the Sydney
Harbor Trust to roof Nos. 6 and 7 Wharves, Miller’s Point. Sea air
has no ill effect on “Adamax.”
Another of 5000 sq. yds. for Mine=buildings at Kandos, N.S.W. —because
it is a well=known fact that “Adamax” resists the ravages of chemical
fumes, gases and acids.
Other orders of note secured by “Adamax” include:
miles of “Adamax” Roof Covering being used on Wool=storage sheds erected in
Wentworth Park, Sydney, by Messrs. Stuart Bros, for the Central Wool Committee.
Again, recently, 13 miles of “Adamax” Roof Covering was ordered by the Imperial
Authorities in Perth for the West Australian Wool Stores.
22,000 sq. yds. for use on the Commonwealth Buildings at Molongo, Canberra.
20,000 sq. yds. on the Leeton Canning Factories.
13,200 sq. yds. for West Australian Government Freezing Works at Wyndham.
An order for 44,000 sq. yds. was placed for use on the South Australian Wheat Board
Buildings.
The Contracts for the N. S. Wales Railways for 1917=18, and again for 1918=19, were
secured for “Adamax” Roofing, the estimated requirements for the year being 25,000 sq. yds.
Secured because “Adamax” is a timeproof roofing that is not affected by heat, cold, wind, snow,
rain, etc.—because it costs less than one-third the price of Galvanised-iron, with a heavy saving-
in freight.
“Adamax” is supplied in rolls 36in. wide and 7 2ft. long, in three thicknesses: 1-ply (light), 2-ply
(medium) and 3-ply (heavy). Nails and cement for sticking the overlap where the roofing joins
are supplied free of cost with each roll.
A free booklet, “The Adamax Series,” gives full facts. Send for free copy.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Governor-General Ferguson on the sub-
ject :
We Avho know more or less about politics appre-
ciate the parly system as a stable instrument for
free government.
As “we” happened to be members of the N.
S. Wales Ministry, nobody Avas passed out
for interrupting. To that happy band the
system Avhich put them on the box-seal; natu-
rally enough looks as majestic and enduring
as Kosciusko. But his Ex. could reckon on
hearing A. Voice, Esq., if he worked off that
sort of patter on any reasonably representa-
tive gathering of the people Avho pay the hill.
This paper suggests to his Ex. that ho might
find it an interesting experiment to publicly
examine the innards of the party sysrem as
it operates in the Commomvealth before an
assemblage of Avorking Australians, and tak-
ing Avhat has transpired during, say, the
last ten years, and particularly during the
war period, disclose to the audience a feAV
reasons for continuing to regard the party
system as a stable instrument of free gov-
ernment.
If certain Labor politicians don’t “get
down to brass tacks” their crowd may yet
be known as the Indefiuite Party. They
have heard, as a matter of common report,
that Scully, the I.W.W. witness, was refused
a landing at San Francisco, allegedly because
he was an “undesirable,” but they don’t really
know. They have also heard that some Min-
isters belong to a syndicate which has been
'dealing in wheat certificates, but they have
no personal acquaintance with the matter
and make no direct charges. They learn that
a certain juryman was probably biased
against one of the I.W.W. incendiaries be-
cause he had given him the sack two years
previously, and as he must have kept the
fact in his mind ever since and nursed an
undying hatred, the trial was obviously null
and void. The Solicitor-General says that
the said juryman never employed or sacked
the said, incendiary. The same croAvd has
damaging confessions from some of the State
witnesses against the I.W.W. convicts, but
some are not signed and some not SAVorn to,
nor are they produced. One concrete fact is
that a Labor politician wants to be recognised
as Consul-General for the Bolshevik Govern-
ment in Russia, which is at war with the
British Empire, Australia included. Most
other things are haze and rumor.
If the Avar lasts another decade or two
the Australian State Parliaments and Gov-
ernments may economise in real earnest.
Some are making a start already. The other
day, for instance, only 22 legislators motored
from Victoria’s Parliament House to hand
the local representative of the Australian
Governors’ Union that weighty and lunatic
document called the Address-in-Reply. And
of the 22 only one, Davies, President of the
Council, was tricked out for the occasion.
The Speaker of the Assembly left his alpaca
dressing-gown and his horse-tail thatch at
home. Also the blessed mace Avas left in
the wardrobe —and when Victoria’s statesmen
give this holy relic the go-by things are get-
ting serious.
Australia is to have a real War Minister
in London, and as Australia House, one of
the most magnificent and most mortgaged
buildings in the city, is wanted for the High
Commissioner, the biggest of our seven
Ambassadors, there has been an idea of buy-
ing St. Paul’s to accommodate the neAv Great
Man. There will probably be an objection,
however, to parting Avith the sacred edifice,
especially on an lOU, and it may be neces-
sary to put up with Buckingham Palace.
Plain General Smuts, Avho War Ministers
in Europe on ’behalf of South Africa, will
probably have a goatskin tent alongside.
