“William” discusses the claims: —
“F. Born” (B. 8/8/’18) says that Smuts, and not
Hughes, is the parent of the Monroe Doctrine for
the Pacific. As a matter of fact the doctrine las
been in full working order ever since Australia had
politics of her own. The annexation of the islands
was considered and discussed in N. S. AVales in
1871, Hughes at the time being a small boy of
seven years and Smuts an infant of 12 months.
Martin (who was Premier in 1872) gave up the
idea for Constitutional law reasons. A few years
later John Robertson, as Colonial Secretary, put in a
strenuous communication to Downing-street, in
which he demanded that Great Britain should “take
possession, not only of the magnificent island of New
Guinea, but of the islands of New Britain, New
Ireland, and the chain of islands to the N.E. and E.
of New Guinea, from Bougainville Island to San
Gristoval, the group of the New Hebrides and the
adjoining islands, and the Marshall, Gilbert and
Ellice islands, because the occupation by foreign
Governments of islands in the immediate neighbor-
hood of our coasts, and on the very tracks of our
ocean communication with the Mother Country
might, and probably would, be in time of war fatal
to our free navigation of the sea which adjoins our
territory.” Hughes has discovered nothing which
Avas not foreseen by old Jack Robertson and his
friends more than 40 years ago.
The weary old question of whether a
settler upon land resumed for occupation
purposes by the State should reside on the
property is again being Avrangled over in
Vic. The fact that there is no possible argu-
ment against compulsory residence is, of
course, a mere detail. The proposal of the
Lawson Government in a Closer Settlement
Act Amendment Bill is to abolish Clause 69
of the original Act, which impds.es upon the
selector the obligation of eight months’ resi-
dence in the year, and to substitute therefor
that the purchaser of a block of closer-settle-
ment land must make a statutory declaration
that he owns no other such allotment—a
farcical arrangement that would turn the
whole close-settlement theory into hash. In-
cidentally this is alleged to be of assistance
in repatriation, which is proceeding at such a
furious rate in Vic. that 90 soldiers have
been put on the land in a year, and a Royal
Commissioner is investigating “the drift to
♦the city.” The little finger of the land-jobber
is stronger than an army corps—and Lawson
ought to commemorate the incident by plant-
ing a symbolical statue somewhere.
One of the simple but annoying forms of
Parliamentary GoA T ernment is that essential
documents like the annual railway balance-
sheet can’t be issued until they have been
“laid on the table of the House.” This doesn’t
matter if the House is sitting, but if it is
aAvay for a long rest there is an irritating
delay. The Speaker always takes the table
home Avith him when there is a lengthy
adjournment, so that the floor underneath
it may be washed, and though the door-
keeper might easily lay the paper on a chair,
with the mace on top of it to prevent it
blowing away, that would be contrary to
precedent.
“0.K.”: The preamble of the N. S. Wales
liacles Union Oongress makes me SQuirm;-
(. apitalism can only bo abolished by the workers
unities in one class-conscious economic organisation
to take and hold the means of production by revolu-
tionary industrial and political action. Reyolutionan
action means the establishment of social ownership
by the whole community, etc.
Well, this country has been very considerably
run in the past by the chosen nominees of
Trades Unions, and where are the co-opera-
tive factories, warehouses and shops, to
begin with? Beef trusts and sugar trusts,
and all sorts of combines from which the
outside capitalist suffers in common with the
rest of the community were to be suppressed
by the Trades Union representative in office,
but the Millennium didn't eventuate. Mean-
while the principle of co-operation, wherebv
the producer, manufacturer, shopkeeper anil
consumer would be blended in one harmoni-
ous socialist, received no impetus whatever.
Labor Government, created by trades-union
forces, was too class-conscious, apparently,
to move in the direction of abolishing com-
mercial class distinction. The co-operative
principle aims at making every demanding
consumer a partner in every form of supply
business, but the precious preamble of the
N.S.W.T.U.C. doesn’t include even the word
co-operation in its revolutionary blather.

Bricks are frequently thrown at civil ser-
v&nts because of their dislike of anything
approaching undue haste; yet occasiotfally
they heat the pistol. Arthur Griffith, ex-
Minister for Works and ex-other things, told
the N. S. Wales Royal Commission on the
Public Service that when he contemplated
emulating the Children of Israel in the State
brickmaking industry he wrote to the heads
of the Departments expressing the hope that
they would use his output. “I received a
very courteous note from one of the heads,”
said the witness, saying that he had had the
bricks tested, that they were entirely unsatis-
factory, and that he could not use them. I
thanked him for his courtesy and said that
as yet we had not burned a brick.” Pharaoh
made the followers of Moses make bricks
without straw, but this wizard produced
intangible bricks as yet in the womb of
Time, so to speak.
In politics the art of liow-to-put-it-off
ranks next to the art of how-not-to-do-it-at-ali.
Last year the Victorian Government was
petitioned to regulate the hours of business
for fruit-shops—Dago and otherwise, but
mostly Dago—which are still licensed to re-
main open all the time, on every day of the
Aveek. The petition Avas ’ ignored on the
ground that it didn’t represent a majority of
fruiterers (Dago and otherwise). Probably
the Dagoes as a body were not in favor of it.
The Government couldn’t afford to continue
to ignore the fruit-shop scandal, hoAvever ;
so something had to be done, and the Min-
ister of Labor has done it. He has given
instructions for the preparation of a regula-
tion fixing 7 p.m. as the hour for the clos-
ing of fruit and vegetable shops during the
five coldest months of the year. But in
order that no malcontent Dago may be taken
by surprise the regulation is not to come
into force before next winter. Moreover, to
prevent any possible misunderstanding as to
the date of the promised reform, the Minister
arranged not to pass the regulation until
early in October. Here endeth the first les-
son in the art of how-to-put-it-off.,
The United States is alleged to have
adopted the simple, logical idea of prohibiting
voluntary enlistment and relying on compul-
sory service only. Under compulsion the
followers of .Tudd and Brookfield and Pren-
dergast and others Mve to go to the Front
and be killed or captured just as numerously
as the Carmichael kind of men. Voluntary
enlistment, on the other hand, takes an
undue proportion of the Carmichael sort
away from local politics and leaves an undue
proportion of the Judd variety in charge of
things. If Hughes had only had the courage
of his opinions, and shox’ed a compulsory-
service law through Parliament without re-
gard for the chances of the next election,
quite a lot of the No crowd Avould have been
out of his way before the election and would
have given him no trouble. Instead, he com-
promised on an arrangement by Avhich plenty
of his friends were to be buried beside the
Somme, but none of his enemies.
“Chron.” : Adelaide was the first city in
Australia to have municipal government.
The Act authorising it —the first municipal
luav in S. Aus. —was passed on August 19-
1840, by Governor Gawler and his Executive
Council, and the first city council election
Avas held on 81st October the same year.
James Hurtle Fisher was the first mayor.

THE HOLY ASPECT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Any attempt to penalise wealth or interfere with
the sanctity of capital would be disastrous.”
—Observation in the House of Lords.
Take off your boots. Tread softly. Bow the head.
Beneath this sacred emblem of three balls
Dwells holy Capital. The world is red
AVith flame and loud with honor’s dying calls;
But here there must be quiet at slirine
Where mortgage and foreclosure intertwine.
The people may be hungry. What of that?
Hunger is seed of revolutions red.
V starving babe may break a king’s gold hat;
The mob is mighty and it must be fed.
AVell, feed it; but remember every day
The great and holy maxim—“lt must pay.”
In freedom’s name lift up a mighty shout.
The foeman must be smitten hip and thigh.
Arise, ye A’aliant ones, and cast him out;
Be strong for victory, be strong to die !
On wings of aeroplanes let triumph mount ;
And we, be sure, shall turn it to account.
What’s that ? The men who fought will have a say.
Oh, sacrilege! Oh, treason to the past i
The war is mighty ; hut, oh, make it pay !
All else Avere mere dishonor at the last.
When the vast harvest conquering freedom reaps,
Brothers of Fat, play well and play for keeps.
N.S.AV.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

COMSTOCK’S “DEAD-SHOT” WORM
PELLETS.
A SAFE, SURE AND RELIABLE SPECIFIC FOR
THE ERADICATION OF WORMS IN CHILDREN
AND ADULTS.
The general symptoms of worms are a
tendency to pick tlie nose and a sensation of
itching in the anus. Other symptoms are
nervous affections, such as fretfulness, irri-
tation of temper, wakefulness or disposition
to excessive sleep, disturbed sleep, and grind-
ing the teeth, sudden starting out of sleep as
if from fright, dizziness, spasmodic move-
ments of the eyelids, dilated pupils, per-
verted vision, temporary blindness and deaf-
ness. General convulsions are not infre-
quent in children, and symptoms resembling
water in the head have been ascribed to
worms, and have ceased on their expulsion.
The long round or stomach worm is the
commonest kind of worm from which man-
kind suffers, and is of a light, transparent
color, resembling the ordinary ground worm,
except that the extremities are more sharply
pointed than the ground worm.
Young people are more subject to these
worms than middle-aged or elderly people.
They live in the bowels, and sometimes make
their way into the stomach and throat, caus-
ing sickness, vomiting and choking. Gener-
ally one or two would be the most a person
would be afflicted with, but sometimes a
much larger number is met with. The length
varies from four to eight inches, but young
ones an inch to two inches long are also
found. The first thing to do is to rid the
system of the worms, and Comstock’s “Dead
Shot” Worm Pellets, taken according to
directions, are an effective remedy for the
thorough eradication of the long round or
stomach worm —the common enemy of the
child, the youth, the middle-aged and aged.
A light, nourishing diet, out-of-door exercise,
and. if possible* a change of air will greatly
improve the general health.
Threadworms or pinworms are of very
common occurrence in children. They are
very small, from one-sixth of an inch to half-
an-inch in length, and resemble cotton
thread —they are sometimes called seat
worms, and reside in the lower bowel. The
chief symptoms of this worm are severe
local itching, restlessness and irritability, a
frequent desire to urinate, and picking at the
nose; loss of appetite and anaemia alre also
observed. To get rid of the worms and theii
eggs their nests also must be removed, and
that requires a specific remedy. Comstocks
“Dead Shot” Worm Pellets, taken according
to directions on an empty stomach, followed
by a dose of opening medicine, will succeed
where others' fail.
Comstock's “Dead Shot” Worm Pellets
are packed with full directions, and are for
sale by chemists and stores generally, at
Bs. 6d. per packet, or six packets for 205..
and if unobtainable locally will be mailed
post free on receipt of price. Prepared only
by The W. 11. Comstock Co., Ltd., 23 Lang
Street, Sydney. N.S.W.; Parish Street,
Wellington, N.Z.

BILLJIM : “ Well, this is red ’of. Here's me

“Melaleuca” : “Wart” (B. 25/7/18) states
that the Tassie abo. used no throwing-stick, hence
his spear did not do much damage. lie is quite
right about the throwing-stick being a missing link,
but he is not so about the effects of the spears.
These were generally long, up to 15ft., carefully
straightened by the manufacturer. When making
them he used his teeth as a vvee and outstretched his
arms to bend the spears to the required alignment.
The points were hardened in the fire. To make up
for the throwing-stick the abo. of the Speck used to
make his spears very thin, in order that they might
be grasped between the forefinger and thumb. In
this way a spin was imparted to the spear similar to
the spin of a modern rifle bullet. This had the
effect of carrying- the spear a longer distance and
making greater precision. If “Wart” consults the
old records of the “black war” he will note that
these spears could be used to good effect in order
to reduce the population. There are seven excellent
authentic specimens of these weapons in the Hobart
Museum.
“C. Coral”: In some of the islands of
Torres Strait, when it is
decided to give the cocoa-
nuts a rest for a year or
two to persuade them to bo
more fruitful in bearing,
they go in for an elaborate
ceremony which they call
“shutting” the cocoanut.
The owner of the palms,
with all his numerous rela-
tives, friends, neighbors and
their dogs, roll up in full
force on the appointed da}-.
After a kai-kai (no native
function from a christening
to a funeral is complete
unless they make a “little
bit feast”) the men swap
opinions and then get to
work. Bamboo and plaited
cocoanut leaf are first manu-
factured—something of a
cross—and then the coun-
cillors, in uniform or white
pants and red guernsey,
erect the symbols at the
corners of the plantation. 1
After that no one dare touch
so much as a dropped
cocoanut skin. In the olden
days the punishment for
transgression of the unwrit-
ten law regarding the vio-
lation of the “shut” patch
was the prompt passing out
of the offender per medium
of club or some other handy
weapon ; but now, of course,
there is nothing so drastic.
All the same, the tradition
is regarded as binding, and
even the dusky proprietor
and his household have to
go further afield when it
comes to arranging the
menu for the day, or shout-
ing visitors a cocoanut shandy.
“Pedess” : The boss was clearing, and his
assistant was a new arrival. Cutting and
grubbing was proving slow and tedious, and
Tony was surprised by the curt order to “Go up and
get the dog.” “Er-er—which dog?” he drawled.
“Why, the dog and chain!” yelled his exaspe-
rated employer. Tony fled and was gone about half
an hour. He reappeared towed by two yelping-
hounds, and shouting, “You didn’t mention which
one you wanted, so I brought them both.”

“Boree Bill”: This beats all I have seen
on the Barcoo. A well-known identity had been
having a good long spree, too long to be of any use
to him, for he ended up by hanging himself in a
police cell a strip of blanket after having failed
to cut his throat in the back yard of the pub. The
hotelkeeper handed him over to the police, and then
when the man on the jag had hanged himself he (the
publican) turned to and made a coffin for the body,
boxed it in and carted the remains to the cemetery.
But even that wasn’t the end of his con-
sideration for his late customer. He read the burial
service over him! So no man need be afraid of
being neglected out on the Barcoo, if he can find a
publican who will supply him with drink till he
gets the horrors and blots himself out, then make
his coffin, carts him to the boneyard and conducts the
burial service.

“Merrigal”: Re “Ida D.’s” par. (B.
l/8/’18). “The Myall” was the name of the present
Tomingley before the latter was a goldfield, and a
portion of old Tomingley station (Peak Hill dis-
trict),- which, like Bulgandrarnine adjoining (bulgun,
a boomerang; derra, hand; mine, a blackfellow) was
occupied in the ’forties by John Newell Gilmour, of
Bathurst (N.S.W.). The Croft brothers, from Dubbo
side, had the first claim; and Donald McPhail, of
Bathurst, followed. In the Wiradjuri tongue, which
obtained in the district concerned, dthollon means
tongue (dth sounded like th in than) ; dthom-mol-in,
writhing; dthom-min-bil, poison, something done by
death-adder; and dthom-miny, a death-adder.
Another local word for the same reptile is thamin,
and so the blacks say tahrningooli (place of death-
adder). The Kamilaroi terminal bri (place of) has
equivalents in Wiradjuri, in bone and bah. Thus
Gulargambone, place of galahs; Girilambone, place
for stars; Quambone, place of mists; Weemabah,
place of standing fire; Warregenbah, place for briga-
low trees. Collymongle (the station taken up by
W. IV. Bucknell in the ’thirties is intended, I pre-
sume), in the Moree district (N.S.W.), where the
Kamilaroi language prevailed, meant, according to
the interpretation of two of its best exponents, the
Reverends IV. Ridley and Archdeacon Charles Green-
awav, broadwater, from kooli (water) and mun-
.gool (expansive). Also, gurra was the word for
spider, while the primary signification of Boggabri
was place of creeks—bukki, creek: bri, place of.
Incidentally, the Royal Anthropological Association of
Australasia, much to its credit, has gqpe to great pains
to secure, with due regard to the dialect peculiar to
their respective districts, the translation of our

“Stargazer”: Two prospecting parties had
come into Crowther from the long swish of the
Petticoat Hills in the course of the. afternoon, and
the front of the Mechanics’ Institute was littered
with much choice paper advertising the “Blue Bells,”
a vaudeville collection of such vivacity that “Blue
Belles” might have been considered a more illumi-
nating title. Especially conspicuous among the
posters were the colored pictures of Bella Bell, the
Morning Magpie, a sumptuous serio-comic who gave
practical demonstrations of the almost inconceivable
fact that so much woman could go into so little
clothes. Macnaughton’s party and Ivyver’s party fore-
gathered and drank plenteously, and at eight
charged into the Crowther Mechanics’ Institute, and
took front seats, of which there were plenty to
spare. They found a tall man in black,* whose
dinners obviously disagreed with him, talking about
the insufferable attributes of beer. This man con-
tinued to dwell upon beer’s intolerable qualities.
For hours and hours he talked, and eventually Mac-
naughton arose and said: “I say, ole feller, be a
sport. Shut up now, ’n’ give the Mornin’ Magpie a
look-in.” The tall mat in black expatiated on the
essential depravity of beer for further teons, and then
Kyver arose. “We’ve arst veh t’ cut it like a
gennleman, ’n’ give the ladies a charnee,” said he.
“We won’t speak to you agin.” But nothing hap-
pened, excepting that the long, dank man insisted at
great length on the inalienable iniquity of brewed
chink, and then everybody went home. Really the
prospectors had dropped in on a pleasant Friday
mg-ht with a teetotal evangelist named Cox the
first appearance of the Blue Belles being fixed for
the following evening. The diggers were too wrapped
up in matters of graver import to discover their
error, however, but they did discover that Miss Bella
Bell was staying at their hotel, and thev assured her
slily and often: “It’ll be all right to night. We’ve
fixed him!” “You jes’ go ’head,” said Macnaugh-
ton, “we’re goin’ to see you get a fair deal. That'll
be about all. You leave it to us.” On the Saturday
the Blue Belles brought off their entertainment before
a delighted crowd, none more delighted than the
respective parties of Maenaughton and Ivyver. Kvver
congratulated Miss Bell on behalf of his party.
“What ’id we tell yeh, Miss?” said lie. “We
accounted for the bloke with the endless jaw well
’n’ truly. There was nothin’ of him crowdin’ out
the girls this trip—what-o ! ” It was not till Sunday
noon that the teetotal specialist was rescued from
the SOft. dry hole at Shinbone into which the pros-
pectors had carefully lowered him at 3 p.m. on
Saturday.
* * * *
“Pick”: When Peter Gehl, a Jugiong
(N.S.W.) hunter, undertook a contract to supply a
Melbourne furrier with 1000 lyre-birds’ tails, he
selected the jungles of the Jeremiah Forest, near
Gundagai, for the offensive. For weary weeks his
success was rotten. The lyre is the most wary fowl
of the Australian wilds, and to stalk him is a sheer
impossibility. Then Peter lobbed upon the scheme*
that enabled him to complete the contract inside a
month. Using his powers of mimicry to imitate the
love-call of the lyre, he at the same time danced a
tail, fastened to the end of a long sapling, from
behind a bush. Every male bird within hearing
came to knock the supposed serenadcr off his dancing
plot, and Pete did fearful slaughter with his pea-
ordnance.

“Silas Snell”: John Soft, of Min Min,
wanted to sell his house and lands, and advertise-
ments in Ballarat and metropolitan papers brought
along a possible purchaser in the person of Don
Mc-Don, a Western District farming man. Donald
ran an experienced eye over the paddocks and the
surroundings, studied the elements, and turned
'to John Soft. “Twenty-two puhd the acre!” said
he. “I wadna gi’ ye more. There isna a tree
whereby a man could hang hisself. There isna a
pool in which a man could droon hisself.” Then
up spake John Soft, with the seriousness the subject
demanded, urging the advantages. “But did yeh
overlook the railway runnin’ right by the hack pad-
dock? Folks hereabouts are using that for prefer-
ence.”

“Melos”: The “tailed spider” mentioned
by “N. or M.” (B. 27/6/’18) is not a spider, but
a springtail-—a primitive creature widely distributed
yet rarely seen owing to its habit of secluding
itself under stones, bark, etc. It is an unlovely trifle,
midway between a scorpion and a spider in shape,
and colored in harmony with its immediate surround-
ings. Most of the members of this order have a
peculiar spring arrangement working on the principle
of the “jack-jumper” made by children from the
wish-bone of a ebook, and consisting of a kind of
fork, under the abdomen, with the prongs projecting
forward and held in a clamped position by little

of harm s way, it simply releases the “catch” (pre-
sumably by inflating the body) and the spring, work-
ing automatically, does the rest. Don’t know how
far the springtail hurls itself, nor what keeps it in
its desired course.

IF WE HAVEN’T THE GRIT TO GO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They came from the South and they came from the
West
And across the plains from Hay,
From • the towns and the runs tl?at are further out,
And they’re off to join the fray;
And our hearts beat fast as the boys, swung past,
And the drums beat fast and slow,
But what will they say us they march away
If we haven’t the grit to go ?
“They’re better at home,” they’ll say, they’ll say,
“If they haven’t the grit to go away,
To mash the girls, and brush their curls,
And watch the pumpkins grow,
If they haven't the grit to go, you know,
If they haven’t the grit to go.”
We saw the chaps in the army caps
And the puttees all spick and span,
And we waved our hats and marched beside
And the children joined the van.
The ladies blushed and the ladies smiled,
And we all enjoyed the show;
But what will the ladies think of vs
If we haven’t the grit to go?
“They’re better at home!” they’ll smile and say,
“Much better at home l” they’ll say, to-day,
“To milk the cows and feed the sows
And hear the rooster crow—
I? they haven’t the grit to go, you know,
If they haven’t the grit to go.”
We stamped our feet, for we’re glad to meet
The lads who are risking death.
We are over the age, so we take the stage
And squander a lot of breath,
And the band will play and the parson pray
Where the big IClbertas grow ;
But we haven’t the luck or we haven’t the pluck,
Or we haven’t the grit to go.
And tPf did our best to enlist the rest
To tackle the job for us;
And I am as bad as the worst of them
And that’s why I am writing thus,
And I can’t be blamed if I feel ashamed
(For my heart is sinking low).
Be it wrong or right I will blush to-night
For I haven’t the grit to go.
“I am better at home,” I’ll say, I'll say,
“I am better at home to-day,
To drift, and drift as a dreamer drifts,
With my feet as cold as clay;
For I haven’t the grit to go, you know,
I haven’t the grit to go.”

‘Azack”: It is astounding how few people
on the land (and off the land) know which birds are
useful and which are destructive. J. B. Cleland,
Microbiologist of the Department of Public Health’,
N. S. Males, has just compiled a publication giving
the result of an investigation into the stomach and
BRAINS!
FIRST BEER-EATER : "Who’s that cove?”
SECOND DITTO: "That? That's one o' the cleverest blokes in the city 'E won a thou-
sand in tolls about a gear ago."
crop contents of Australian birds, from which it
would appear that our feathered friends and enemies
fall conveniently into four distinct classes ; namely,
useful birds, pests, birds of doubtful character and
birds which arc neither good nor bad, as follow:
Useful : magpies, magpie-lark, butcher-bird, pectoral-
rail, plovers, straw-necked ibis, owls, mopokes, dollar-
birds, kingfishers, cuckoos, swallows, jacky-winter
robins, fly-catcliers, fly-eaters, etc., cuckoo-shrikes
and caterpillar-eaters, ground-thrushes, scrub-wren,
coachwhip-bird, twelve apostles, white-fronted and
tri-colored chats, tits, wrens and wood-swallows
native-thrushes, tree-runners and tree-creepers, dia-
mond-birds, honeyeaters, ground-lark, grey jumper
white-winged chough. Pests: the starling, the spar-
row, silver-eye, hawks, parrots and cockatoos, the
bee-eater. Of doubtful character: the crow, (this
bird is said to be almost equally good and had, and
local circumstances should be taken into consideration
before destroying it). Of no economic importance
(neither useful nor harmful) : the quail, pigeons and
coves, water-birds (dottrells, coots, stilts, grebes,
ducks, herons, etc., excepting the straw-necked ibis
—vide useful birds).
“Jay Pay”: Beware of bush vets, who
recommend cowdung as a good poulticing medium
and dressing for a wound. One trial with it will
convince the credulous. I have seen otherwise-
healthy wounds rendered quite septic by one appli-
cation of the muck, and to say the least of it, it is
an abomination in or about a wound. If you need a
poultice use bran or linseed.

“J.N.A.”: Professor Perkins, Director of
Agriculture in South Aus., is a firm believer in the
olive, and is doing much to popularise its cultiva-
tion. He advocates the planting of the tree plenti-
fully m the western districts of the State, where
wheat and slieep are practically the only products at
present. In support of this advocacy, he has had, and
is having, planted at the recently-established Govern-
ment Experimental Farm at Minnipa, groves of olives
—some half-dozen rows around each paddock of the
farm. A recent visit there showed that the trees
weie flourishing and that the nursery was well
stocked for further planting. They are, of course,
wire-netted to protect against rabbits.
‘Te TV hare” : The Bermagui mystery of 38
years ago is solved at last, at any rate to some
people s satisfaction, and the aborigines are debited
with the crime. Frank McCaffrey, of Wollongong
(N.S.W.), elicited this solution from one McNamara,
who was camped on the Montreal gold-field (about
three miles from Bermagui, South Coast) at the time
of the tragedy. Montreal is about 14 miles distant
rom Mystery Point. A blackfellow (it is stated)
called at McNamara’s camp, exhibited “sovereigns,
half-sovereigns and nuggets in plenty,” and “wished
to dispose of a pair of binoculars.” The police were
interviewed and apprised of these facts, but being at
the time liot-foot on another clue apparently didn’t
act on McNamara’s information. But that the abo-
rigines committed the crime, carried out, as was the
murder of Lamont Young, Schneider and the two
fishermen of Ulladulla must have been, with great
secrecy, skill and diabolical cunning, I for one refuse
to believe. They may, perhaps, have been accesso-
ries after the fact, and the silence of such of them
as knew too much may have been bought, with gold
and trinkets taken from the murdered men, but no
more. I visited Mystery Point, the spot at which
Lamont Young and his companions vanished into
space, a few years ago, and while in the vicinity
talked with several persons who were resident in the
district at the date of the tragedy. The information
resolved itself into the following theories and asser-
tions:—•
(1) The party led by Young (who was a geologist
and mining surveyor) were examining caves alleged
to exist in the cliffs about Mystery Point, and were
choked to death by foul gases. Searches made have
already disproved this theory.
(2) The four men were kndnanped by the crew of
a vessel m want of sailors and taken to some distant
country Unlikely, and, if true, would have been
proved long ago.
(3) According to one deponent, a half-emptied tin
of jam was found beside a fire which still smouldered
on a beach at Mystery Point the morning after the
tragedy. The jam, _it is alleged, was found to be
mixed with strychnine. Four men could hardly be
poisoned offhand without there being* some traces of
a struggle for life, nor would so damnatory a piece
of evidence have been left in full view by the perpe-
trators of a crime. 1
W AH sails and cordage were gone from the boat
used by Young and his mates, which was found
jambed against a large rock close in shore. One side
ot the boat was stove in. Deduction: the bodies of
t] he murdered men were wrapped in the sails, bound
with the cordage and cast into the sea, weighted
with heavy stones. In all probability (irrespective
of the mode in which the crime was committed) the
bodies of the four men were disposed of in some such
manner as this. Some assert that the boat’s side was
purposely smashed in in order to convey an impres-
sion of shipwreck.
The boat capsized and the four men were
drowned and their bodies devoured bv sharks. Un-
Jlk <”T- The sea was calm on the day of the tragedv.
(6) On the night of the happening one man, it 'is
strongly asserted, came from Mystery Point towards
Montreal carrying a large sack. He crossed Wallaga
Lake, a large inlet between the Point and Montreal,
disappeared. This man’s name has never come
out, but it is said, on good authority, that Young’s
theodolite and certain other items of his property
were, some months afterwards, offered for sale in
Melbourne. Schneider, too (Young’s field-assistant),
was said to have been seen again after the tragedy,
hut this assertion has never been confirmed.
The fact that not the slightest evidence of a death-
struggle could be found either in the boat or about
the Point is most remarkable. Young for a time
taught a geology class at a school I attended, and is
embalmed in memory as a powerfully-built man,
standing over six feet in his socks; not a man to
give up life easily. His mates were men in their,
prime. In any fight with aboriginals there would
have been dead on both sides, and probably blood-
stains and other evidences of a struggle difficult to
entirely conceal.
"Mac Gamba”; He was a young married
man, a shop-asSistant by day and a poultry-farmer
of the back-yard type by night. A friend who was a
successful exhibitor at local shows advised him that
his beloved Black Minorcas would have no chance of
a prize at the Show with scaly legs. Kerosene would
cure them. A tin was cut in halves and about fin.
of oil poured in. In the dark hours he crept to
the fowlhouse with his kerosene'and a lighted candle
and proceeded to dip the birds. The job was
nearly completed when one of the dipped ran
over the lighted candle, caught alight and fled
among the others, who also lit up. It was like
the Battle of Jutland, only quicker. Moral: If you
can't do your job in daylight, keep silkworms.
* * * •
“Quince”: There is a probability of
another imported animal becoming a nuisance in the
vicinity of Adelaide. North American squirrels,
evidently escapees from the adjacent Zoo, are making
themselves at home and rooting things up in the
Botanic Gardens. The director finds these aliens
responsible for the mysterious cutting off of young-
plants. They also got away with the crop of wal-
nuts, and have eaten all the pine seed and acorns.
The little fellows have frugal instincts, and when
food is plentiful store away supplies for the possi-
bility of lean times ahead, but evidently so many
good tilings are available on their hunting-ground
that they have not found it necessary to draw on
the hidden larder. It puzzled the gardeners for some
time to find why dozens of young trees were coming
up in unexpected places in the park. Now they
have the reason—the squirrels planted ’em. If
prompt, measures are not taken for extermination
orchardists are likely to have a tough proposition
with these handsome but rapacious consumers. I
understand they are a curse to American gardeners..
* * * *
“Nor’-West” : The rlottrel and other ground
birds are well camouflaged, but for the championship
I enter the quail. When perfectly still members of
the tribe positively defy detection. Many a time I
have stood over a pointing dog ami tried to detect
the quarry, but never with success. It is only -when
on the move that the bird can be seen. At certain
seasons the striped, or stubble, quail comes in
myriads to the plains of the North-West to feed on
the hard black berries of a little shrub called “fat
hen.” Another very small swift-flying quail is also
fairly numerous. It is chestnut in color, with white
underneath, and has only three toes —a member of
the “turnix” family. After the great drought these
birds swarmed to the North-West in countless num-
bers, and they got up past the Warialda district (as
a rule, they do not get so far east in great numbers).
The bush was fairly alive with them, and they were
shot in thousands for the Sydney markets. Experts
say that they came from China and Japan. Maybe;
but I know that they bred on the plains during their
sta>, and it is remarkable how well their plumage-
markings blend with our own Australian herbage.
“Wangai”: The Government teacher at
Torres Strait has to be something of a liuguist. The
following order for necessaries was handed in a few
days ago: 1 tolan uk, 20 padam, 2 ins manil, 4 henk
twan, 2 botils sklumstar, 1 gibbidy pipe. Transla-
tion: One towline hook, 20 fathoms 2in. Manila rope,
four hanks twine, two bottles Stockholm tar, one
G.B.D. pipe.

0. : Are any Abo. writers acquainted
with the Northern Territory buffalo? I shot one in ’92 at
Cook, on the Diamantina (about lat. 22), and became
curious to know if any have been seen further south.
One was got before that date on Dotswood, near
Townsville, but he was marching east to the sea, like
Shermap. My particular buff, was a very old gentle-
man, who, I suppose, had been handed the key of the
street by the young bulls, and was apparently emulat-
ing Dr. Morrison’s walk from the Gulf to Melbourne.
(I wonder if Morrison ever rehearses, in
his dreams, that great pedestrian stunt, more than
35 years ago.) Sent the head of the old beast to
Brisbane, and I think it still hangs in the office of
the Q.N.B. in Queen-street.

“Melos”: Tell “South C.” (B. 25/7/181
that the Bundabunda snake is found in all parts of
Australia, except Southern Victoria. The color scheme,
alteihate black and white rings, is characteristic of
this species, whilst that of the ringed sea-snake
varies greatly in the distribution of black, brown and
yellow. A smaller relative, abiding 1n AVestralia,
is yellowish below and brown above with two black
patches, one on nape of neck and one on the head.

MARRIED BY MATILDA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Miss Matilda Hennessey, J.P., clerk of Petty
coTpTe" ' H 1 Ba,lanala, ’ d . has married three
I was married by Matilda in the not-so-long-ago;
l was not Matilda’s bridegroom, and she wore no
dress of snow,
For Matilda was the pretty Clerk of Petty Session. Oh,
It was splendid being married by Matilda !
\ow the service it was snappy, for she had no time
to spare;
While she tied me to dear Hanna#! was glad we’d
wandered there,
For she quite enjoyed the wedding—though it wasn't
her affair—
She was better than a parson was Matilda!
As a widower of forty T’d faced altar-rails before-
The verger sneered behind the font, the parson
almost swore ;
But Matilda was so joyous that her feet scarce
touched the floor.
I’d have swapped a stout archbishop for Matilda !
So she waltzed us through the wedding, and no dreary
march was played.
We’d have done a whirling tango if a moment more
we’d stayed ;
But she’d other legal business, so her fee we gladly
paid,
And she blessed us with a handshake did Matilda.

bis machinery about with him in his head,
and can put his hat on his factory at any
moment and remove over the border. Here
he differs from even a newspaper man, who
is anchored down by his office, his printing
machinery and the necessity to live so close
to. his readers that he can reach a large
proportion of them before breakfast. So,
provided his Federal Government of lir.ee
Nations supplies a good copyright law,
Freetrade ( i.c .. no duties on books or the
material that books are made with) suits
Mr. Wells admirably. Hut if “The Time
Machine” or “The War of the Worlds” was
a thing that was turned out of equal quality
on a loom by any journeyman in any coun-
try, and Mr. Wells had no chance of a job
except by being as cheap as the rice-eating
novelist in Canton, or the semi-nude
romancer in Java who makes his large family
start romancing also at the age of eight, the
matter might strike him differently.
And he demands that his Federation
should clean and pacify and educate and
uplift such places, as Africa and Mesopo-
tamia, because they are wonderful sources.of
raw material. Never does it strike him
that they have abundance of raw
material to spare only because they
are poor, ignorant and decimated by
war. With plenty of uplift, they will
become bigger producers of raw material for
a while. Then with more uplift they will
become manufacturers and use their own
raw materials. Also they will become far
more populous and more luxurious, and will
eat their own raw materials. The overwhelm-
ing feature of the 19th century—more won-
derful than all its mechanical or literary
triumphs—was the increase of population.
Many countries accumulated four times as
many people in that century as they had
done in all the thousand centuries before.
Many went faster than that. In 1801 every
place in the world, save mere city States like
Hamburg or one-valley States like San
Marinlb, had materials of many sorts to sell,
especially food. Raw materials were a
drug in the market and made people tired.
They almost hated the sight of them. Then
Britain. Franco, Belgium, Germany. Austria
and Italy began to go out of the business and
shout for imports. The F.X.. Canada and
Japan are following suit. They want to
export manufactured goods and secure the.
profits of manufacturing. The opening up of
new raw-material countries is a pleasant and
profitable thing—and dashed temporary.
The book concludes with a rather heavy
yet interesting dissertation upon Democracy.
Possibly Mr. Wells puts a shade too much
stress on Democracy, though that is a mis-
take in the right direction. He says, for
instance, that “the small Greek republics
collapsed through sheer political ineptitude,
before the Macedonians.” Partly they did, for
their fragmentary condition and their bitter
jealousies didn't make for strength in face
of a common danger. Partly they collapsed
because it was the day when the spear was
the queen of weapons, and the Macedonian
spear was ISft. long, which was about Bft.
beyond the average, and there was a man
with a long head behind the long spear.
IChatto and Windus, London.]
Sex Hygiene and Sex Education, by Atkinson and
Dakin (Angus and Robertson, Sydney), mildly states
the case for frankness to the young about matters
on which frankness, no matter what we may say
about the irrationality of secrecy, is more difficult
than anything else in the scheme of life. Generally
—almost invariably—the young get their first glim-
merings of information in a dirty, furtive sort of
way from other kids and depraved adults. A subject
of overwhelming importance to the individual re-
mains during the most plastic years of his life a
matter about which he notices a conspiracy of
evasion in one place and an atmosphere of filthiness
in another. Atkinson (Commissioner of Public
Health in W.A.) and Dakin (Professor of -Biology
at the Uni.' of W.A.) want to end that, and write
suggestively about imparting knowledge decently for
the preservation of health and the protection of
morals. The ideal is easier to state than to reduce
to practical terms, aim it seems that the
authors rather beg the question they raise. They
distrust the class method, yet seem to lean towards
it. Now it is obvious enough that attempts to
teach what the authors are talking about and the
reader is thinking of to school classes (it is no good
side-tracking the thing by introducing terms like
“hygiene” and “ethics,” which, to the juvenile
mind, are more or less incomprehensible) would be
a very terrible business. The results, it is quite
possible to imagine, might be a million times worse
than the results of the prevailing chaos. With lads
and lasses verging on the adult stage it might bo
all right. With little children it might be all
wrong. And it does no harm to remember that
among these the smattering of “knowledge” picked
up at random is not nearly so demoralising as the
shocked adult often supposes. The book is really
an appeal to parents to take a sane view of their
responsibilities and of their children's future, and
to realise that round this matter of sex function
cluster many other issues relating to character and
health. A useful and suggestive little work. The
enlightenment of the adult would most likely carry
with it a solution of the difficulty about the young-
sters.

“G.” : A N. S. Wales bismuth show that is
likely to be heard of in the future is that held
by Alexander Biddell and Son at Duekmaloi,
in the Oberon district. The formation is
unusual, and. for the central tablelands,
unique, consisting of a big contact deposit
with the bismuth impregnating the stone
throughput. Kiddel is treating the stuff by
hand, and gets through 24 to 25 tons a
week, with the bismuth recovery at the rate
of 1 p.c. and a concentrate of between 50
and 60 p.c. The extent of the deposit has
not been definitely fixed, but it is claimed
that there is enough stone available to keep
a big battery going for years. Nothing
beyond picking is necessary to displace the
stone, which is almost on the surface. The
primitive jigger-box system of concentration
is being followed pending something
systematic. The Riddels hold 20 acres
under lease, and a Bathurst crowd have the
show under option, due to expire in a few
weeks, on the basis of £6OO for a two-thirds
right, the present lessees retaining the
other third. Minister Ball, who recently
had a look at the deposit, has recommended
the despatch of a Government geologist to
the field. The show was worked years ago,
but little was known about pro-
cesses.

“D.” : Why is not some attempt made in
Australia to rid the mining industry of the
worthless or undesirable director? In
America the mining director is a business
man, sometimes a mining specialist, but
always an executive head. In Australia the
director is often a dummy with no business
or mining experience, and often share-
brokers or speculators get on Boards merely in
their own interests to secure first-hand infor-
mation as to what is going on at the mine,
and do not care a jot for shareholders’ inter-
ests. As a result, of all this, mines are fre-
quently floated on reports containing exag-
gerated statements of ore values and possibili-
ties, or the poor suffering public is let into
things which require several thousands of
pounds to open up and which have not
sufficient capital to sink a shaft, let alone
develop and equip. They all come to grief
sooner or later, and when they do share-
holders mournfully brood over their losses
and the papers devote a few lines to what
is termed tne glorious uncertainty of min-
ing!
•'Bluebeard'’: E. B. Osborn gives a
thoughtful appreciation of Leon Gellert.’s
Songs of a Campaign in one of the
English weeklies. He mentions .the trio
of soldier poets —Rupert Brooke for Eng-
land. Alan Seeger for America, and Leon
Gellert for Australia, and that the resem-
blance is manifold between the trio. All
three were young, all-questioning, loathed the
war, yet saw that they must sacrifice all that
they had been and might be. Gellert, like
Alan Seeger. is steeped in sub-tropical sun-
shine, and there is no trace of the cold and
calculating self-critical spirit of the North.
Sayk Osborne: “The bleak Australian
commonsense, which is not to be distracted
from the ultimate issue by pomp of circum-
stance (battle honors or the clang of trum-
pets, patriotic songs or what not) comes out
in many of his poems. Yes, these Songs of
a Campaign will not be forgotten, for they
mirror the mood of Australia’s self-sacrifice
and have now the grave and time-defying
beauty of memorial.”
QUEENSLAND NATIONAL BANK.
HALF-YEARLY MEETING.
The ninety-first ordinary general meeting of the
proprietors of the Queensland National Bank, Limited,
was held at the Banking House, Queen-street, Bris-
bane, on August 15. The chairman of directors
(Mr. Geo. Edw. Bunning) presided, and there were
also present Hon. G. W. Gray, M.L.C. ; Messrs. James
Milne, James Love, W. H. Hart (directors), Mr. W. Y.
Ralston (general manager) and a number of share-
holders.
The report and balance-sheet for the half-year ended
June 30 was taken as read.
The Chairman, in moving the adoption of the
report and balance-sheet, said:—
Gentlemen,—l have much pleasure in submitting
for your approval the report and balance-sheet for
the half-year ended June 30 last, and as printed
copies have been in your hands for several weeks I
shall ask you to take the same as read.
You will observe that the net profit for the six
months amounts to £53,0/0 3s. 3d., which has been
dealt with as follows: ‘‘Private depositors’ repayment
fund ” £51,500 los. /d., and balance can led for-
ward in “profit and loss account, £1504 Bs. Id., and
I am ve#V pleased to announce that the final payment,
equal to 4d. in the £ on the face value ot the stock,
was distributed to our shareholders on the 12th inst.,
thus completing the conditions ot oor scheme ot
arrangement so Ist contemn the agreement with
the Government and our depositors, and consequently
the profits, for the current half-year will be available
for the payment of dividends to the shareholders,
After full consideration the directors have decided to
pay interim dividends for quarters ended September
30 to March 31; and, in order to make the time
between payments as equal as possible, the interim
dividends for quarters ended September 30 and March
31 will be paid about the middle of November and
May in each year. It is with great pleasure I have
to announce that the first interim dividend for the
quarter ending’ September 30 next will be payable on
the following November 11; and the .final dividends
for each half-year will, of course, be declared at our
general meetings, which are held in February and
August. The directors have further decided that the
interim dividend to be paid on November 14 will be
21 per cent, for the quarter ending September 30.
being at the rate of 10 per cent, per annum.
It is a great relief to the management to be finally
freed from the heavy yearly payments we were called
upon to make to our stockholders, and 1 congratulate
the shareholders after so many years' waiting that
they will from now receive a fair return on their
capital. \
The business of the bank continues very sound, and
our position generally is an undoubtedly strong one,
as. excluding capital accounts and interminable in-
scribed deposit stock, our liquid assets, which amount
to £5,724,628 9s. 8d„ are equal to 01.13 per cent,
of the whole of our liabilities to the Government and
the public. Our depositors’ position (both current
accounts and fixed deposits) is unique in its security,
as they are specially protected for all time under
clause 9 of our schepie of arrangement, which pro-
vides that, after the Government, they are a first
charge on the whole of the assets of the bank, which
on June 30 last, as you will see by a reference to the
balance-sheet, were £12,935,440 4s. Id.
In view of the large amounts required s>y the Com-
monwealth Government for war purposes by way of
loans, in the flotation of which the banks assiM very
largely, we in common with other similar institutions
are checking advances for speculative purposes as
much as possible,' and we would impress upon onr
clients, in view of the fact that they will be expected
by the Federal Government to contribute to the fullest
of their ability to future loans, that they should cur-
tail their requirements as much as possible consist-
ently with the proper conduct of their respective
businesses. It is also advisable to note that the
Federal Government is likely to be compelled to
impose much heavier taxation for war purposes tit, an
early date, and it is necessary to be prepared to meet
it.
Since our last half-yearly meeting eight of onr
staff have enlisted, and we much regret that since
that date we have received advices that six of our
staff have been wounded and that five have been killed
in. action, to the relatives of whom our most sincere
sympathy is extended. •
The directors -extend tlieir congratulations to Mi.
Ralston and the bank upon the attainment of the
objects for which he, with wise prevision, many years
since framed the final scheme of arrangement ratified
in 1897. The purposes he sought are now completed,
and at a period sonic few years prior to the date
anticipated when his proposals were adopted. It
been a fine achievement, entailing responsibility,
arduous work and careful forethought; and the results
have had momentous influence towards the prosperity
of the State, equally with the other interests directly
associated with this institution, and its stability has
afforded valuable support to the various industries
upon which the welfare of the community, is depend-
ent. These congratulations are also extended to the
staff and all who have assisted in securing the splen-
did result which places the bank amongst the leading
and soundest financial institutions in the Commonwealth.
Mr. G, W. Gray, M.L.C., in seconding the motion,
said: The report and balance-sheet is the most satis-
factory and comprehensive in the history of the
bank, and as an old shareholder and a very old cus-
tomer of the bank I endorse all that has been said
in the chairman’s speech relative to the general mana-
ger—Mr. Ralston —and we all owe him a debt of
/gratitude for the skilful way he has handled the
affairs of the institution, overcoming all difficult
financial problems during the past 21 years. He has
proved a strong man and a very able banker, whose
services, I trust, we may be able to retain for many
years to come.
Mr. John White also congratulated the general
manager and the management on the splendid achieve-
ment, and the motion was then carried unanimously.
Mr. J. W. Wassell proposed a hearty vote of thanks
to the directors, general manager and officers of the
bank, which was seconded by Mr. Win. McGregor and
carried by acclamation.
The Chairman acknowledged the compliment on
behalf of the directors, and the general manager, in
responding, said:—
Gentlemen, —I have to thank you exceedingly for
your kind vote of thanks to the staff and myself,
which, on this occasion, is particularly acceptable,
and I heartily congratulate the shareholders on tlieii
position as disclosed by the balance-sheet and the
Chairman’s speech.
I may mention that since our reconstruction the
bank, by the formation of companies, has made liquid
several large old assets which it was impossible to
dispose of or deal with as a whole. 1 refer more
particularly to the Millaquin Sugar Company, Limited,
with a paid-up capital of £500,000, and the Queens-
land National Pastoral Company, Limited, with a
paid-up capital of £850,000. We have disposed oi
numerous shares in these companies at from par to a
substantial premium, the proceeds of which hare
assisted us greatly in extending our business and m
strengthening the bank, and these companies are no«
regarded by the investing public as sound commercial
concerns, and the shares are well quoted.
I consider the shareholders and stockholders have
been particularly fortunate in their choice of direc-
tors from time to time during the whole of the teim
I have been connected with the management. No
body of men from first to last could have given more
thought and devotion to their duties than those
chosen, and the policy of the bank under their direc-
tion has always been sound and sensible.
It will give you some idea of what we had to face
at the time our scheme of arrangement came into
force when I remind you that before we could put you
in the happy position you are in to-day we had to
make good to the Government, the stockholders am
private ledger accounts over £3,500.000. The hea"
calls on our resources from time to time for thiV
purpose are now things of the past, and the bank i?
undoubtedly at this date one of the soundest financial
institutions in Australia.
In conclusion, I desire to thank the directors, oi
the consideration they have always shown to me, am
for the confidence they have always placed in me. >»
this connection I desire specially to mention II >
James Milne, who has been a member of the Board
either here or in London almost continuously from
the commencement until now. I sincerely thank tu
members of the staff for their loyalty and assistance
extending over so many veal's, and I am glad to say
that it is the intention of the directors to give piam
tical proof of their appreciation of the staff during
the current half-year, either by way of a.bonus on
salaries or a donation to their provident func , an
last, but not least, gentlemen, 1 have to thank you
for your continued courtesy and many 0
kindly feeling towards the staff and myself on vfrr
many occasions.

Mary is married
Jacks' at the front
A ND the many happy evenings you used to
spend en Jamille are gone—never to return.
Even when Jack’s booming baritone once more
fills the house —he will miss Mary’s accompani-
ments. You know he never could get past ragtime.
In the meantime you and mother
are dreading the silent evenings of
the coming months. They stretch
ahead in a seeming eternity of
ennui! You have both so long de-
pended on the “youngsters” for
amusement that any talent you once
possessed yourselveshas rusted away
into uselessness. But there is a
way the Beale Player way—to
realise all the enjoyment that you
think you are going to miss. The
“Beale” Player-Piano has every
attribute of the straight Beale-Piano
—including the steel tuning system
—together with a Player mechanism
of faultless technique. It also
excels in tone control. Thus it
enables anyone musically inclined
to give full rein to their rpusical

IMPORTANT.—LOOK TO YOUR TEETH.
There are no teeth like those furnished by Nature. For service, comfort and good
health, artificial teeth cannot be compared with a natural scz.
Sensible people will accordingly try to preserve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap and water are useful, but somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttlefish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifically prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free 1/S. Free sample posted. ROCKE. TOMPSITT & CO.. Melbourne.

all the old good qualities of the Hupmobile
with many improvements and refinements.
There never was a Hupmobile that could
compete with the present model in riding
comfort, economy of operation and all=round
performance. Yet Hupmobiles have always
been notably • strong on these very points.
Brief Specifications.
Two export body styles—2-seater roadster and 5-seater touring car. Right-hand drive. Wheelbase
112 inches. Tyres 815 x 105 m.m., clincher, plain in front, non-skid on rear. Small bore, long
stroke. 4-cvlinder motor. Equipped for export with magneto. Options: Color, blue or battleship
grey; top material, Neverleek (black or khaki). Write for complete illustrated manual and prices.

IF STOMACH HURTS TRY THIS.
If you suffer from dyspepsia, indigestion,
pain after eating, gastritis, heartburn or
stomach trouble due to acidity or food fer-
mentation—and over ninety per cent, of
stomach complaints are due to these causes
—go to your chemist and get a bottle of
Bisurated Magnesia. The next time you
eat or have pain in the stomach take half a
teaspoonful in a little hot water and note
how quickly all pain and discomfort ceases.
It is said that thousands of soldiers use
this simple remedy at the Front, and many
attribute their freedom from all digestive
troubles, in spite of exposure, hasty eating
and poorly-cooked food, to its regular use.
Chemists say that its almost invariable suc-
cess is due to the fact that Bisurated Mag-
nesia quickly neutralises the excess and
stops fermentation, thus promoting healthy
natural digestion. Being absolutely harm-
less as well as inexpensive, this simple
remedy should be kept on hand in every
home where economy and good health are
sought.— Advt.

Specimen Section of a
“ COLO ” Tyre
Eight Points of the “COLO” Tyre
(1) TREAD.—Corrugated, presenting small sur-
face to the road, thereby saving friction.
(2) TREAD.—A great thickness of rubber, which
is practically cut- and chip-proof.
(3) BREAKER STRIP, which, being made of
loosely-woven, heavy fabric, binds the tread
to the carcase of the tyre.
(4) PURE RUBBER.—Pure Rubber is placed
under Breaker Strip.
(5) CARCASE.—Composed of five plies of heavy,
long-fibre canvas, heavily impregnated with
rubber.
(6) PULL AIE SPACE. '
(7) SIDE WALLS. —Realising the severe work
tyres are called upon to do in the country,
we have placed a great thickness of rubber on
the side walls, preventing water from entering,
as often happens in other makes of tyres after
a few months'’ service. Other makes have
very little thickness of rubber at this point.
(8) BEADS are heavily reinforced and skin-
coated with rubber, to prevent rust.

Kyrle Bellew used to claim that he held a
record. He had, lie said, made a date with
more fair admirers at one moment than any
attractive actor of his day and generation.
A letter came to him while he was at the
Melb. Princess’s with Mrs. Brown-
Potter, signed “Gwen,” telling him of
the unbounded admiration Gwen had for
him, and beseeching him, if he had any sym-
pathy for the tender regard of pure young
maidenhood, to be on a particular seat in
the Botanical Gardens at a specified hour on
a day mentioned. Impelled by curiosity,
Bellew attended, and presently a procession
of college girls, extending for quite a
HE OUGHT TO KNOW.
HE : “I can’t understand how it is people
keep on coming to this sort of show.
If you’ve seen one you’ve seen them
all.”
SHE : "Oh, hut some of them are different.”
H E: “No. I assure you; and I’ve seen every
one for the last fifteen years.”
hundred yards, two deep, began to file past,
and all cast adoring eyes on “Curly.” An
impulse fired Bellew to stop one maiden
under the nose of an indignant teacher.
“Which of you is Gwen, my dear?” lie said.
Tlie girl giggled. “We’re from the Lady
Gwendolin College.” she said. “We are all
Gwens.” Then Bellew understood. He had
an appointment with a whole school.
“Fax" : The incident of the double-knock in
the third act of “The Thirteenth Chair”
seems to have been suggested by the wireless
telegraph surprise in Justin McCarthy’s com-
edy, “The Dictator.” Old Rosalie Le Grange
addresses a question to space, and gets what
she takes to be a supernatural response. Two
knocks, sounding exactly like those she is in
the habit of producing with her foot, are
heard as though in response to her appeal to
the spirit of “Laughing Eyes.” The old girl
is astonished at her success. “I didn’t do
that;’ she mutters. In “The Dictator” the
hero has instructed his valet to keep a tele-
graph apparatus clicking in the adjoining
room, in order to gull a Mexican-Spaniard
swanker who is threatening to have him
“chotted.” The clicking is supposed to be a
message in response to a “wireless” which
the threatened Yank has addressed to the
U.S. Government. The swarthy revolution-
ist is half convinced by it. He goes away for
a while, promising to return later and have
the New Yorker shotted unless he can ex-
plain his arrival in the country. After his
departure the clicking occurs again, where-
upon the worried Yank calls to his valet to
stop working the “wireless” swindle now
there is nobody present to be deceived by it.
protests that he isn’t accountable for
the clicks. Hero rushes into next room and
discovers that the messages which he scat-
teied abroad, without any hope of fetching
a reply, have really been intercepted by a
L.b. warship. “We’ve got a bite!” he
cries. If “The Thirteenth Chair” were a
farcical comedy instead of a melodrama the
fraudulent old medium would cry “Got a
bite!” when she hears the butler’s two
knocks at the door.
“M.P.A.”: A glaring instance of the
stmking-fish tendency in Australian picture
circles is the latest move by a party of show-
men to send Marcus Clarke’s story, “For
the Term of His Natural Life,” to U.S. to
be produced as a movie drama. George
Smith, a well-known Melbourne showman,
who hails from Britain, has, it appears,
secured the rights for production. Of late
wo have seen what Australians can do on
the screen in “The Woman Suffers,” a real
Australian story photographed splendidly
by one of the Higgins brothers, and pro-
claimed by the trade as first-class work.
There is no reason whatever for Clarke’s
tale to be put before the camera outside the
land of its birth, and a bunch with such
sentiments deserve to get it where it’ll do
them the most good, when the nhoto-drama
comes to light, from every man in the busi-
ness who claims to be real Australian.
. There’s an artistic restraint about the
picture-show advts. that is a pleasure in
itself. Take a few of the well-chosen
words concerning “The Kaiser, the Beast
of Berlin.” They promise an intellectual
evening’s entertainment. “You shall fol-
low every one of the mad dog’s unspeakable
abominations, and when his black course
has been arrested you will see if the picture
doesn’t give this lust-maddened wolf-hound
of Potsdam his just deserts. See the inside
life of this cancerous, degenerate viper. See
the Beast of Berlin sent mad with a refer-
ence to his Withered Soul.” That’s the
strong point of these publicity agents—they
never overdo things.
“8.J.”: “Business Before Pleasure” is
not, as has been suggested, the first plav
seen in Australia in which the biograph has
furnished first aid to the dramatist. The
earlier stages of the race between the motor-
car and the train in “The Whip” was
shown on the screen, and brought to an
unconvincing finish on the stage, in the
midst of swaying canvas and pink flares.
“0.K.”: The tradition of the altitude of
the original Chang, the Chinese Giant,
has been clouded by recent obituary notices
of a “Chang” who was born in Scotland, and
“had all the appearance of a native of the
Celestial Empire.” He is spoken of as
“Chang” Hay, and was called “the Chinese
Giant” on the strength of a mere 7ft. in
height. Seven feet! Chang, the Chinese,
was fully Oft. high and fairly well-propor-
tioned at that. Sergeant Bates, the Ameri-
can giant, who was boomed later, was some
inches shorter. Bates married Anna Swan,
an American giantess, and they parented at
least one phenomenally large' infant; but
Chang died a bachelor, as far as I remember.
He was a cultured man in manner and
speech, and the money he earned as a giant
was not his only means of living. London
sassiety had ,some good stories of him. A
well-known politician one day took the giant
to his club, and, after lunch, proposed a
stroll down Piccadilly. The sight of the
human steeple drew a crowd, and the dis-
concerted host hailed a hansoln cab, remark-
ing that they could continue their talk in com-
fort. “If the roof were taken off the cab,”
said the courteous Chinaman.
From New York :
Margaret Anglin is the most effulgent of the fixed
stars in the U.S., and her recent interpretation of
the Medea at the Carnegie > Hall, New. York, was
applauded by'the best critics. Young Lionel Atwill,
one of the Firm’s imports for “The Whip” in Aus-
tralia, has moved up into the society of Alla
Nazimova, in a series of Ibsen roles. Australian
actor Cyril Keightley, commended for a nice portrait
in It. C.. Carton’s comedy, “Off Chance,” at the
New York Empire, did Cassius to Tyrone Power’s
Brutus in a production of “Julius Caesar” in another
Broadway Theatre. Laurette Taylor, the original
“Peg o’ My Heart,” Julieted with-a Romeo of her
own choosing at the Shakespeare Playhouse, New
York, and later played Portia, assisted by Australian
0. P. Heggie as Shylock. Laurette’s hubby, Hartley
Manners, conceived the bright idea of staging his
failure, “Out There,” for the benefit of the Red
Cross, and with the help of an all-star cast, including
G. M. Cohan, George Arliss and Arnold Daly, raised
683,000 dollars in a few weeks. Hilda Spong,
who lately found some Shaw parts suitable to her
maturity, seemed to be scarcely trying in “The
Fountain of Youth” at the Henry Miller Theatre,
N’York. Pretty Charlotte Ives, who never reached
a top place in the estimation of Australians, is well
established as the heroine in “The Man Who Stayed
Ac Home,” at the 48th-street Theatre.
Beatrice Miranda has been reaping fresh
laurels at the Shaftesbury Theatre, London,
with the Carl Rosa Opera Co. In a new
opera, of British origin, “Dante and Bea-
trice,” written by W. J. Miller and composed
by Stephen Philpot, the Australian singer
has been welcomed with enthusiasm. The
opera is in the style of Balfe and Wallace.

“Stargazer”: Somebody says “The orches-
tra never revolts.” Wrong again. The
orchestra is long suffering, it is true, its
forbearance is at times miraculous, but
occasionally even the fiddler turns. Our
friend Graff was an Austrian and as decent
a little dirty man as ever breathed. Being
down to it on one occasion, he took service
in the orchestra at the then Opera House,
Melb., while a prowling dramatic company
was indulging itself in a grand metropolitan
season. “The Specked Earl” proved a suc-
cess, to everybody’s great amazement, and
played on and on, week after week, till its
“demnition” insistent drivel got on the nerves
of poor Graff, and one night, when the
specked earl had decoyed the virtuous village
maiden for the 52nd time, that sensitive soul
arose in his place in the small orchestra,
whacked his fiddle on the floor and cried
aloud : “Mein Gott! Mein Gott! I am seek
of dis.” Sudden uproar. The actor-manager
stepped out of his heroic part for a moment
and glared down at Graff: “Plow dare you,
sir?” he said. “I am seek, seek, seek of der
tarn rubbitch dot you talk out,” the musician
replied. “Why you d—d, dirty, little fiddler,
I’ll pull your nose!” gasped the hero. “Dot
iss all ride,” said Graff. “You can pool my
nose, maybe, bud you do not addle my prain
some more. ' And Graff snatched up his
fiddle and pounded out. tearing a few mili-
tant bars from the Soldiers’ Chorus from
"I aust' out of his damaged instrument. An
usher booted him as he passed. The play
was resumed, but ten minutes later an
“S’NICE,” AT SYDNEY TIVOLI.

the bull to the nervous El Tomato
(Chris Wren).
eldritch shriek from a violin in the gallery
stopped proceedings. Again Graff was booted
out; but: in the middle of Act 3 from the
back stalls Graff’s fiddle screamed another
section of the Soldiers’ Chorus, horribly
out of tune, and for the third time the rabid
little fiddler was ejected. In our bachelor
abode he explained: “I will down sit no more
for free week, but revenge iss a seat.”
“Peter”: Supplementing “Adam’s” par.
(B. J.5/8/T8) let me state that Mary Pick-
ford was a comparatively prominent star
under her correct name of Dolly Nicholson
for the Vitagraph Co. and other movie firms.
Ten or more years back the name of Dolly
Nicholson was almost as potent with the
picture public as her present alias, and 1 can
well recall the rapture with which West’s
first audiences at the old Olympia in Mel-
bourne used to greet the beguiling little
figure with the Smile of roses. The name
of Mary Pickford was adopted when she
bounded on to the regular stage in America
and clung to her when she left to play under
the Biograph.
Dot Boucicault has decided not to renew
his tenancy of Wyndham’s new theatre in
St. Martin-lane. He will take a rest for a
season. About Christmas time he hopes
to produce a new play which Pinero is writ-
ing for his wife, Irene Vanbrugh. Mean-
while he will fill in time by appearing in a
series of one-acters at the Coliseum and other
variety establishments on the circuit con-
trolled by old Melbournian Oswald Stoll.
THE OLD PHOTOGRAPH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You were the smartest thing, my dear.
The town you gathered in.
The smallest kinklet in your ear,
The little ringlets that fell clear
From temple white to chin —■
I mind them all as if it were
But yesterday you made a stir.
You came on'tripping to the lights,
A thing of eerie grace,
In (aren’t they’?) white cotton tights,
And crinkled trunks. Your vocal flights
AwOke the placid place.
Besides those tights you wore, ah me 1
Elastic-sided boots, maybe.
And on your head, sweet, as you skipped
A hat (with feathers too),
Yet, fair Priscilla, you unshipped
A ton of hearts, and rich men dipped
Their chimney-pots to you.
And here’s your picture at the show
Good eight-and-forty years ago,
Side on to give your dimple deep,
One hand an elbow holds,
One foot upon its toe you keep,
There’s swansdown on your smock. I weep
At what my eye beholds.
Here on the card as time has flown,
Ah, what a hopeless fright you’ve grown!
VICTORIA.

VAGABOND BRIDE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Make her bed of the mosses green.
This is where she must lie,
With the broad leaf shadow and green leaf sheen
Between her face and the sky ;
And a bird will sing at even and morn
When little brown dusks and stars are born.
Come, soft sleep, on a merry wind,
Ruffle the branches light,
Bring the dreams that the elfmen bind
With flowing ribbons of night,
The dear white dreams that go two-and-two
Through the happy meadows of I-love-you.
Kings go forth with a sword of fire
That queens may weep alone;
And the great of the earth must kiss desire
In a darksome house of stone.
But the old, wise stars in the roof above
Shine all the night in the House of Love.
King am I of the wide free air,
Queen is she of my heart.
The bushes bow to a royal pair
As we toss the green apart;
And all the world love-homage pays
In notes of gold on the grassy ways.
Jewel-box of the shining night,
Opened with crystal keys—
Here’s choice of gems for each bridal night
To wear on the veil of the breeze;
And the great white day has a wedding song
In the high cathedral of Think-no-Wrong.
Lift of the wind, lisp of the tree —
Heart of my heart, sleep well!
There shall be sweets for the honey-bee
In star and cup and bell;
And a bird will sing....Ob, swift love, fly
For the first fine look of her morning eye.
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
It is an irrelevant sort of fact that it was
a Boer general, probably De la Rey, he
being the man on the spot, who indirectly
invented the idea of the tank that Hies low.
The matter w r as known before in a general
way, but he reduced it to a science. He
decided that, in a war of few men and large
spaces and swift movements —a war princi-
pally of rifles, because the heavy guns
couldn’t be shifted fast enough—-the defence
of hill positions was a fool thing. It was
good enough in the days of slow-firing mus-
kets, and it had its advantages for mounted
infantry men with bayonets (only,the Boers
didn’t carry bayonets, which was fatal to
them in the end), but with quick-firing rifles
it was a waste of chances. The downward
shot hit one man in the head or else missed
him. The horizontal shot went through two
or three men at times, and generally hit some-
body. So after losing two or three liill-
defence battles De la Rey and Cronje, at
Modder River, made use of a watercourse
meandering over a dead level plain, where
there was no place for a Boer to run to if he
was hunted out. There they shot horizontally.
They raised hell, which was the only elevated
spot in their position. Similarly the high-
flying airship drops something on the head of
the enemy or else misses him. It is splendid
for attacking buildings, but when it is a
question of breaking up a marching column
the low-flier, which can almost rake it from
end to end, is the real terror. It is Modder
River on a far bigger scale, and with a mov-
able Boer instead of a fixed Boer. Another
point is that certain of the swift entrench-
ment devices used in this war are of U.S.
origin. At least they were a Roman dodge,
but the Americans revived and improved
them. When the .men of Grant or Meade or
Lee were feeling their way on rather open
ground towards a concealed or suspected or
strongly-posted enemy, and a halt seemed
advisable, they dug themselves in with the
velocity of some burrowing animal that puts
its nose to the ground and works its feet like
a whirlwind and is swallowed up. As another
side-issue, either Foch or Haig, whoever was
responsible, should be remembered as the
first general who opened the battle with a
concerted charge of tanks. The new tank is
small and swift, and comparatively cheap
and easy to build, consequently it is numer-
ous. So instead of being a rare and lone-
some fortress walking like the Tower of
London, it has become light cavalry or light
tankarray. The charge of 600 tanks with
the infantry running behind and to some
extent protected from rifle-fire lends a sport-
ive and frisky aspect to war. The Mel-
bourne Cup is a drab old tea-meeting by
comparison.
“Good Australian” :
Have just done the cross-country trip to Perth by
the transcontinental railway. Barring the three
changes between Melbourne and Perth it is quite a
comfortable railway journey, and compares favorably
with all the other long-rail journeys that I have
made in foreign countries. The coaches are excel-
lent, the food good, the service satisfactory and the
road-bed smooth. It was a “dry” train—with plenty
of free grog- in every cabin. It beats all the long
overland railway trips in Canada and America,
because the corridor sleeping-cars are so infinitely
superior to the Pullmans in universal use in North
America. The American sleeping-car is a travelling
indecency; the Australian sleeper is a model of
privacy. As to the scenery, well, there isn’t any
after the Adelaide hills are traversed. One stretch
of 500 miles of a straight line across the Nullabor
(meaning no-tree) Plains without a culvert or bridge
is unparalleled.
The cane-cutters of some North Queens-
land districts are on strike for a modified
form of piece-work. They ask a minimum
of 255. per day—the man who earns less
than that amount to get that amount, and
the man who earns more to get more. At
Ingham the strikers stopped a train in the
street, uncoupled the engine, and told the
driver to eat his hat, or words to that effect.
It sounds very dreadful, but it isn’t quite
like stopping the Adelaide express that is
eating up time and space on a sft. 3in.
track and carrying the world’s mails.
Queensland possesses about three dozen rail-
ways (886 miles in all) of the 2ft.-gauge,
and the trains amble through streets quite
affably, and stop to speak to passing friends.
There are about as many types of carriage
as there are carriages—some of them in-
vented by a carpenter and some by a
plumber, and some apparently by a prophet.
Nobody Regards interference with that kind
of train very seriously, so long as no damage
is done and the language isn’t too dreadful.

The Inky Way.
“Rosna” (London) : The London Institute
of Journalists is taking a leaf out of the book of
the A.J.A., and has evolved a scheme to im-
prove scribes’ prospects on the lines of the
Australian award. As there’s no chance of
getting a minimum wage fixed by law yet
awhile, the Institute has hit upon a neat
plan of classifying all papers according to
the minimum salaries paid by them to every
qualified journalist on their staffs. In Class
A the minimum is six guineas; B, five
guineas: C, four guineas; D, three ; E, two
and a half. Each paper i§ politely in-
vited by the Institute to inform that body
as to which class it belongs, and since each
one is likely for business reasons to wish to
grade itself as high as possible the plan
should make for better “screws” all round.
The classified lists will be published by the
Institute; also an unclassified list of those
rags which haughtily refuse to divulge what
they pay their minions. Cadets of less than
three years’ experience may be employed in
due proportion (about one to five qualified
members of the staff) at a minimum salary
of one guinea for the first year, one and a
half the second, and two the third. A 46-
hour week is also demanded, and three weeks
annual holiday on full pay. Women scribes
are being included in the scheme equally
with their brethren.
