“Arthur R.”: Hayseed was reading the
meteorological notes posted at the G.P.O.
He waded through them until he reached the
notice, “For particulars of rainfall see
bulletin.” “ ’Strath, that’s hard,” he mut-
tered ; “see bulletin, and I’ve just bought the
blanky Herald.”
* * * *
“Trion”: The recent agreement between
the A.J.A. and country newspaper proprie-
tors in Bananaland is easier worked on paper
than in practice. The proprietors find it
hard to knuckle down to the 46-hour-week
and the' time-book, while the clause in the
agreement providing one clear day off per
week for all journalists has them fairly up
a tree. The two Maryborough dailies solved
the latter problem by refusing to attend any
Saturday functions. This shut-down scheme
is working well —in the meantime.
* * * *
“Hargo”: “Musketoon” (B. 18/7/T8)
wants to know of a more versatile news-
paper man than Woodlands, of Canowindra
Star. Let me introduce Andrew Wilson
Cross, who founded the Daylesford (Vic.)
Herald 35 years ago. I was his first appren-
tice. His sheet was tri-weekly, and he did
his share of the reporting, paragraphing,
article-writing and proof-reading. Columns
of matter came from his “stick” without a
line of copy. On “dis” days he would take a
turn at jobbing or working anything up to a
five-color job. For years he did the Lynch
Family of Bellringers’ work, cutting and de-
signing his own blocks. An electric-bell system
of communication was installed throughout
the office by him, and afterwards came elec-
tric-light, which he extended to business
places, churches, etc. He made a small
engine for driving his lighter machines when
there was no use for the bigger power ; also
he constructed a motor-car. xY bit of special
ruling didn’t pull him up, and when he in-
stalled the monoline he mastered that. Num-
bering appliances on his machines were
adopted from clocks, as also was a distance-
recorder on the bike which he used for
pushing outside business and collecting.
Andrew deserted the Inky Way to join an
auctioneering firm, so his end was peace.
Marvellous how these old lies persist.
Thus E. I->. Osborn, in the Illustrated
London News (13/4/’18), on the verse
of Leon Gellert: “The savage splendor
of poppies in the corn is discerned through a
periscope.... They are nodding symbols of
the sultry magnificence of Australian scenery
with its songless birds and glowing scentless
flowers .” Of_course, we all know where E. B.
Osborn got it from. He wasn’t relying on
his own ear and nose, but on the nose and
oar of an early immigrant who was too home-
sick to observe accurately. Any ancient
blither lasts these plodding bookmen a long
time.
* * * *
From London:—
A couple of veteran English editors were added to
the roll of knig'hts at the beginning of June. .John
Merry le Sage, the “G.O.M. of Fleet-street,” con-
tinues in harness at 81. His briskness, vivacity and
engaging- juvenility of manner and aspect are excel-
lent advertisements for the healthiness and virility
of journalism as a profession. Previously general
manager, he has since the death of Edwin Arnold,
supervised the editorial department of the Daily
Telegraph as well, and is now styled managing-
editor. He spends the whole day in office, but
leaves the night work to the long-experienced sub-
editor. Le Sage started on the paper as a reporter
60 years ago, was soon promoted special correspon-
dent and did tours through France, Germany, Italy,
Russia, America and elsewhere. He did good work
in the Franeo-Prussian scrap of 1870, and was shut
up in Paris during the Commune. The other new
journalistic knight, A. S. Jeans, has been for half a
century located in Liverpool, where he edits a couple
of combined morning papers merged into one, under
the style and title of the Post and Mercury. He is
the son of a Scottish editor, and has mounted up-
wards from the junior-reporter rung.
In the second issue of the “Handbook of
the Commonwealth Parliament” the figures
incidental to elections in all constituencies
since Federation are brought up to date, and
details given of the voting at the various
referenda. This makes it a useful document.
Reproductions in half-tone of past and pre-
sent members of the Federal Legislature,
vvith short political biographies, make it
picturesque. Anyone who wants to know
what G. Reid or Tom Playford or anyone
looked like in the long ago can get the
facts in an instant, and incidentally become
acquainted with the Moloneys and Hughes
and Bruce Smith and Frank Anstey and
multitudinous other persons. Whether it
was worth using up a lot of expensive paper
on this job just now is another matter. The
printing is done excellently anyway. ,

A WOMAN’S
LETTER
Sydney, August 20, 1918.
My Dear Arini, —
The late M. Albert Metin, of the French
Commercial Mission, was in Australia, it
seems, about 25 years ago. At that time the
distinguished Frenchman was professor in a.
university in his native land, and came to
probe social problems on this side. With
Him, or here about the same time, was M.
Andre Siegfried, who returns on a second
visit as one of the leaders of the party
due in September. It is related of the late
Albert Metin, first, that he was a Freetrader
(he had since changed his views) when lie
made that long-ago trip—fated to be his
last —this way. And second, that he sol-
emnly recorded in a book relating the won-
ders he had seen the amazing fact that Aus-
tralian farmers (i.e., sheep-farmers, graziers
and squattocracy generally) often “dressed
for dinner.” Instead of the red shirts, nan-
kin breeches and flowing beards in which he
expected to find them cutting damper with a
jack-knife, they were as swallow-tailed as
waiters. More amazing still, he found they
had discarded the Jew’s harp, and concertina
and bought grand pianos for their daughters.
Some of them even had given up the habit
of unbuttoning their waistcoats when the
roast of goat came in.
Agent-General Wade gave his arm. to a
Maoriland girl, Mary Hay Foster, of Auck-
land (the bride’s dad was at home in M.L.)
up the aisle of a Kensington (London)
church, a few weeks ago. Then tin* heroine’s
mother took charge, and handed her over to
her soldier ’groom. He’s Lieut.-Colonel
Stephen Allen, D. 5.0.. a Staffordshire man,
and son of the late W. S. Allen, M.P., of
Woodhead Hall, in the pottery country.
The official opening of the South Coast
Red Cross haven for convalescent soldiers at
Bomaderry was an event of acute interest to
the Coasters. Governor-General Ferguson,
at first mistaken for the village policeman, in
a dark-blue suit and constable-like lid, made
a brief and telling speech, with a sly allu-
sion to himself and to one Dundonald—who
had an estate next to his own in Scotland —
as “landowners, and therefore not so
popular as they might wish to be!
Dundonald,” said his Ex., “had lived
for years in Australia. Then he in-
herited the property and returned home, and
in the kirk the following Sabbath the minis-
ter chose for his text: ‘This is the heir —
come, let us kill him that we may
seize his inheritance,’ or words to that
effect. So I was not surprised at
hearing that Dundonald caught the next
boat for Australia.”
The Red Cross executive (Sydney) feeling
that such a gathering would not be com-
plete without the light of its presence, jour-
neyed to Bomaderry for the opening; but it
was the South Coast’s day. From Kiama to
Coolangatta, from Gerringong to Moss Vale,
from the beautiful Kangaroo A 7 alley to any
hamlet you can remember by the broad
bosom of the Shoalhaven River, the farmer
and settler came. And with them Mrs.
Farmer ancl the Misses Settler. They came
in motor-cars, in carts, on horseback,
hanging on to an inch or two of sulky, bob-
bing up and down in an ancient ’bus,
in old-fashioned gigs, and on foot along
wonderful country roads, emblazoned with
groves of golden wattle. Wide fields,
where the gorgeous coral trees are burst-
ing into bloom, and hills that fold in
luscious green pockets, and snug homesteads,
made a pastoral amphitheatre that included
some of the best farming land in the State.
The Home, standing in spacious grounds,
much trampled on Saturday afternoon, is
beautifully situated in the hamlet. It
holds easily about 52 soldiers. With a wide
balcony to help, in a rush time, more men
can be housed. Small brass tablets on the
doors of airy wards give the name of the
South Coast town or settlement responsible
for their upkeep. The brothers McKenzie,
formerly of Shoalhaven (they’re now in
Queensland), bound themselves over to pay
the rent. Matron Emmott will keep the
V.A.D. girls up to time-table in beeswaxing
floors and washing saucepans. The Cul-
lens (the C.J. is an old Jamberoo boy) were
in the throng of visitors from Sydney, with
Commandant George Lee and his lady. The
Macarthur Onslcws motored to the scene,
and the Throsbys steered their petrol-chaise
from Moss Vale to the party. Mark Morton,
M.L.A. for the district, mentioned the dona-
tions —over £2OOO in hand. Mayor Shepherd
(Berry) and Mayor Bennett (Nowra) wel-
comed the Governor-General. So there were
no ruffled feathers.
Governor-General Ferguson motored on
from Bomaderry on Saturday afternoon, in
a smart shower of rain, to finish the week-
end at the Jervis Bay Naval College. The
mariner now in charge, Commander Grant,
is making the sailor boys’ training-school a
very popular national institution.
The Royal Art Society’s annual show, the
39th, opens on Monday, September 2, in the
galleries of the Education Department in
Loftus-street. This year the mulberry-stain
walls have been spring-cleaned and painted
a demure buff color, the shade that doesn’t
assault the eye and distract the patron from
his fell purpose.
The matinee organised by the Tait firm
in the Palace Theatre last week brought
a substantial cheque into the lockers of the
American-Australian League of Help. But
it won’t stay there. The League is merely
the channel by which the money goes, via
the Red Cross, to feed our starving pris-
oners of war in Germany. One of the
memories of the show was the frock
in which Mrs. Guy Bates Post looked
like a grey dove with a heavenly blue plume
on her head. The passengers and crew of
the American mail-steamer which arrived in
Sydney on the day of the performance sent
along £72 as their contribution to the cause.
This brave Sydney
girl is Sergeant-
Chauffeur Olive
Kelso King, of the
Servian army, and
at present detailed
for duty at the medi-
cal headquarters in
Salonika. She is one
uf the veterans of
the war. She was in
England when the
tumult started, and
immediately volun-
teered for service—
any kind of service
—at the Front. As
she was a competent
amateur motor-driver
and possessed an in-
stinct for mechanics,
which she soon brought into practical shape
for service conditions, her offer was promptly
accepted. Leaving England with the Scot-
tish Women’s Hospital unit as ambulance-
driver, she lived and worked through the
horrors of the German rush into Flanders.
Three years ago the Scottish healers moved
further afield to face the dangers of succor-
ing the wounded in Servia. Of the heroism
of these gently-reared women, who calmly
moved about amidst the crash of guns and
bandaged battered humanity amidst the riot
of burstihg shells, Mrs. Elizabeth Abbott
recently told Sydney many thrilling tales.
Mrs. Harley, a sister of General French,
died of wounds, and many nurses and doc-
tors went down typhus-stricken weekly. But
the girl who joined as a “private” (a penny
a day was her official pay) in the Servian
army, and is now sergeant-chauffeur, has
come through untold perils almost without
a scratch. She has faced Death near the
battle-zone, or crossing flooded rivers with
her ambulance, so' often that she must have
become familiar to the rustle of its wings.
Olive Kelso King asks Australians to do
something for the heroic Serbs. They are
in desperate straits for the, poorest kind of
food. The proposal, which has the hearty
assent of the military authorities, is to estab-
lish a Servian canteen in Salonika. It will
eventually become self-supporting, but
£lO,OOO is needed to give it a solid founda-
tion. Most of Salonika’s business quarters
were swept away by fire earlier in the war.
The wily Greek and other local traders seized
the chance to exploit the unfortunate Serbs.
So the soldiers, who lost all when they
lost their country, can hardly buy sufficient
to eat to keep them alive. Subscriptions
may be forwarded to the hon. treasurers
(banker Russell French is one) at 118
Pitt-street, Sydney.
Mrs. Clifford Pain (her late husband was
son of the cleric who, until recently, bish-
oped in Gippsland) adds another name to
the long list of war widows. Her man was
recently killed in France.
There’s “A Corner of Blighty,” 20 Place
Vendome, Paris, where the Aussies spend
some of their time on leave, which would be
glad of some help. During the past 10
months of its useful career the Corner has
been a home touch for 44,000 soldiers. Aus-
tralian. British, Canadian and American
cap-and-apron wearers do all the work, and
cheer up our war-worn heroes of the West-
ern Front. It costs the boys nothing for
the use of a good library, tea-and-music
rooms and a cosy place to write their letters.
Cigarettes, tea, sugar, biscuits, etc., sent
to Miss Butler (founder and manager)
“Corner of Blighty,” A.P.O.S. 5, 8.E.F.,
France, pay no Customs duty.

In Sydney, looking for better health
and already finding it, Ethel de Cas-
tilla, of Victoria. For 25 years this
delicate mortal —now resting eyesight that
jibbed at newspaper work —shook a versatile
pen on the Inky Way. During the last
few years Miss de Castilla, no longer in any
kind of harness, has taken on honorary jobs
in connection with her pet hobby—Gregan
McMahon’s Repertory plays, and the Club
wherein budding talent meets to discuss
itself. Of George Reid, Australia’s old
friend, the Victorian has a very pleasant
memory. She was in. England in fateful
1914, and published a book of verse just as
the bolt fell. Having asked the stout gentle-
man to read it, he approached the task
with legal caution and secured the lady’s
promise that she wouldn’t mind if lie said
what lie thought about it. “He opened it,
read on, and at last looked up with a greatly
relieved expression. ‘Why, this is charming,’
lie said. ‘l’ll send a copy to a friend on the
press?’ It got a sheaf of good notices,”
mused the writer, “but the sudden tragedy
of war blotted it out as a commercial proposi-
tion.” Ethel de Castilla (grandfather was
a Spaniard, who settled in England as a
young man) will be happily remembered for
the first two stanzas in her graceful poem,
“An Australian Girl.”
A member of the Australian press delega-
tion drops a line from New York en route, to
London. “I forget how many years ago since
George Bibbs d —d Chicago, but we found it
still sizzling. Even almost overpowering
hospitality couldn’t switch off the heat of
U.S. i i^i July. South of Cape York, we have
it in quality. Business men were wandering
about the streets with their coats under
their arms, looking for strangers and iced
water.... But they’re going to help us win
the war. There’s no mistake about their
resolution and driving power.”
Stepped ashore from the mailboat and
went on to Melbourne, Leila Doubleday, the
Victorian violinist, who had such thrilling days
in Europe when Germany burst its swelled
head. In Austria in the autumn of 1914 (she
had studied earlier in Vienna, and since then
mostly in Switzerland), the Australian girl
was about the most delighted passenger
aboard who saw Sydney Heads last week
from the U.S. boat’s deck. Leila’s musical
mother —or was it an aunt? —was very kind
to Amy Castles in the years that are dust.
Historic old St. Patrick’s Church, on
Church Hill, almost opposite the kirk in
which the late militant Dill Macky once
thundered the Word, is in charge of French
priests. Their golden jubilee has just been
celebrated in the crowded city parish in
which they have so long toiled and kept the
peace with their Presbyterian neighbors
across the way.

A Sydney University girl, Kathleen Her-
lihy, drops in from the United States with
a few flattering remarks about the original
methods of that country. For instance, she
quotes as a subtle chastisement for both
sexes the following notice which appears on
many railway cars: “What’s the cause? Is
it gallantry or the advent of woman suffrage,
or the war-spirit of self-sacrifice that is daily
causing more men to give up their seats to
women, and more women to be cour-
teous in their thanks V” On the service
flags, that proudly float from almost every
house, a red star takes the place of the blue
when the soldier or sailor of the American
home is wounded. A gold star marks the
death in action of the family’s hero. “Since
the great offensive in France,” continues the
Australian, “red and gold stars are becom-
ing more frequent.” Kathleen is studying
secondary-school methods in U.S.
At last week’s lecture on the Light Horse
and their crusading in Palestine, the lec-
turer’s lantern-slide showed a smart-looking
horseman aboard his mount on the screen.
“I’m not quite sure,” said he, “but I think
that’s Colonel .” A girl, who was watch-
ing eagerly, unconsciously uttered her
thought aloud. “No, it isn’t! That’s Lieut.
Cameron Sharp, of S. Australia.” And
she ought to know, for she’s engaged to the
Light Horseman, who’s been four years at
the sandy front.

This Comforts Fund cleared nearly £BO by
its entertainment in St. James’s Hall on
May 31, but a family of 1000 men in France
can eat that up in a twinkling. Like other
Comforters the 2nd D.A.C. has been so busy
helping a procession of Days that it must
now proceed to tear the clothes off the
backs of its friends, relatives and acquaint-
ances to buck up its own finances. The
address of the depot is 117 Pitt-street.

Melbourne, August 26, 1918.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Mrs. Major James received an ovation at
Spencer-street on her return from W.A.A.C.
work in France. Returned warrior Coun-
cillor Charlie Atkins led the welcome, and the
Hennessy daine was on hand with a bouquet.
Overseas Clubites and Wattle Leaguers were
amongst the crowd, which also included Mrs.
Brig.-General McNicol. Mrs. James has
two aviator sons; one is out of action with
a sightless eye, the other is convalescing
after a fall and concussion of the brain. She
said good-bye to this son, Lieutenant Ron-
ald. in hospital in England, then journeyed
through America home. On her way to the
Melbourne express in Sydney she suddenly
met this same son, making for the same
train !
Lieutenant Frank Fischer was killed last
week in France. He was well known in
Melbourne with Haughton’s, the wool firm,
and was a brother of singer Elsa Stralia.
According to a recent announcement in
Grand Fleet orders, Australia’s midshipmen
will, receive six weeks’ home-leave on com-
pleting two years’ service. For the original
batch of lads who left the Jervis Bay Col-
lege, the spell will be due at the end of this
year. The first Australian midshipman to
Hie gathered to Davy Jones was a fine lad
named Cunningham, who was drowned in
one of the new submarines. Another of the
lads, MacKenzie, was in the same trouble on
another sub., but he succeeded in gaining the
air-lock and was saved.
Janet and Doris, the two daughters of the
late “Jack” Murray, of Warrnambool, are
launching out for themselves as beauty-
parlor specialists in Collins-street.
From London:—
Protest.—That the British Dominions Woman’s
Sufi rage Union, as at present constituted, has no
right to pass contentious resolutions in the name of
the British Dominion Women when the speakers’
names on the posters advertising the Conference are
all English, the principal speakers English, the vote
open to the public, the majority of whom are
English, and the three principal office-bearers, who
are secretary, press-secretary and treasurer respec-
tively, are English. There is no president or vice-
president, and very few properly constituted del-
egates of affiliated societies. Hon. delegates proposed
some of the resolutions. As the committee refused
to include this protest in their report of the Confer-
ence we resigned.
AGNES E. DOIG,
FLORENCE G. HERRING.
Mrs. Doig and Mrs. Herring, of Sydney,
were Australian delegates.
The Glaciarium indulged in a carnival on
Saturday night. There were seven or eight
prizes and only fancy-dressers were allowed
on the ice. It was crowded, and seats for
onlookers could not be found after the show
started. Rink-manager Russell had deco-
rated the place with a canopy of peach
blossom that dropped its pink petals almost
to the heads of the skaters. Chairman H. B.
Howard Smith sat in a box, and the
Baillieu family and the Lascelles occupied
other pens. Doris Arm.vtage, who carried
off the ladies’ championship of Australia last
week, was among the skaters. “Professor”
Dangle;, who has returned invalided from
the war, is back again as demonstrator; and
Dunbar Poole, the skate-dancer who ran the
Sydnej Glaciarium two years ago, is now
installed as chief vlider.
The Norton Grim wade pair, with flying
son Lieut. Fred, have gone to Bananaland
for a few weeks’ sunshine.
The mayoral O’Donnells, of St. Kilda,
lately gave a party, for which the St. Kilda
Town Hall was divided into sections and the
floor crowded with tables loaded with food.
In addition to the tuck-in there was a long
musical programme. Di Gilio played dance
music occasionally, and the younger toes in
the crowd got restless. Mrs. O’Donnell was
wrapped in a large Paisley shawl cape and a
daughter was in pale blue. Mrs. Agar
Wynne, wearing a high collar of pearls and
diamonds, sat at the chief tables with her
husband, and mounted the dais when he pre-
sented the mayoral pair with a brass fern-
bowl and a long speech.
The widow Staugliton lent her ballroom to
Mrs. Lloyd the other Wednesday night for a
dance in aid of the 6th Brigade Comforts
Fund. It was a night of pouring rain, but
the South Yarra and Toorak girls arrived
in swarms.
The children’s charities will appeal to good
citizens on Wattle Day, Friday, September
20. Committees are already getting busy.
The Prahran Red Crossers gave Mayoress
Einbling a gold card-case at a party last
week. Mrs. Einbling, in her turn, gave silver
presents to Mrs. Secretary Young, Mrs.
Girdler, Councillor Willis and Town-clerk
Romanis for their hard work. It was a
general square-up.
Poor Walter Kirby goes from one hospital
to another. In Brisbane he has again re-
tired to a sick bed. Pleurisy is the latest
affliction.
Vair Dodds Lyon went off to India with
hubby Gordon Lyon, who is to control ship-
ping arrangements between Australia and
Calcutta. Everything was very hurried, and
Mrs. Dodds was hastily installed in the
Lyon house in charge of the children.
Ex-Gaud Mayor Hennessy’s portrait (by
Webb) was unveiled in the reception-room
at the Town Hall on Friday afternoon.
Stapley (in large round glasses) occupied
Ins mayoral throne, Hennessy was at his
right hand, and Mrs. Stapley supported the
llennessy dame on the left. One man kept
referring to Hennessy’s “business” in his
speech, and David during his return of
thanks informed the up-to-date person that
he had retired from business 27 years ago.
Artist Webb referred to his subject as
captivating.” A small replica portrait and
a string of pearls were presented to the
Hennessy better-half. Merchant Howard
Berry lingered lovingly over the gams. The
recipient, in brown ninon, although nervous
made the speech of the day.
Buckley’s ball was a gorgeous spectacle in
Melbourne Town Hall. The dancers whirled
under a sort of tented roof composed of
hundreds of yards of silk strips (the silk
will be probably “thrown out” as a bargain
at the next sale) and deep pendant silk
shades to the chandeliers. The electrics
were all turned low and streams of colored
light were flooded from corners of the bal-
cony. Sometimes the dancers were bathed
m twilight-purple, sometimes in scarlet and
olt-times in a rose-pink radiance. One of
the handsome mannequin girls wore flesh-
pink tights and two flowing strips of cloth-
oi gold. She was a dream.
The mayoral Wynnes, of Kew, who are
nearing the end of their long innings, gave
&n At Home to their official brethren and
sistein last Friday. Irma Caron warbled
fetchingly, and the company got the surprise
ot its life when Mrs. Caron, Irma’s mother,
sang Tosti’s “Good-bye” in a voice as clear
and strong as a girl’s.
Miss Philadelphia
Robertson, who was
made 0.8. E. (Order
of the. British Em-

Miss Robertson is an
Australian and
sister of the famous
Rev. Hume Robert-
son. She had a good
bu s i ness training,
and early in the
war was working in
England for the Red
Cross. As official
secretary of the head-
quarters of the Australian Red Cross she is
the right hand of Lady Helen Ferguson. Her
quarters are at Federal Government House.
When the work started, Miss Robertson was
stationed in the State dining-room, but sit-
ting on a red velvet chair which was sur-
mounted by a gold crown, she was oppressed
by the opulent, unbusiness-like stateliness of
the surroundings. Then Lady Helen allowed
her to be skied to the tower-room, which
opens on to the leads of the ballroom roof
and has a glorious view. It was the den of
the late Major Quilter, and when he went
to England, just before the war, Captain
Tommy Curwen moved in, and loved the
view so much that he reserved it against his
return. But it was not to be. Poor Curwen
went down in the big fight.
Adelaide’s gossip:—
The sum of £2O was eashlioxed for ihc purchase
of comforts for the Third Light Horse as the result
of a concert given by the pupils of the Blind, Deaf
and Dumb Institution at Brighton on Saturday. On
the same day Walkerville held the second of its
monthly Paddy’s Markets, the proceeds of which
are split between Trench Comforts and Red Cross.
The casualties mount up. Major Wells, of the
A.A.M.C., who enlisted in 1914, has died from
wounds; Lieut. A. Wald, another 1914 man, has been
killed in action; Lieut. Degenhardt, whose father
was at one time mayor of Port Pirie, has died of
wounds, so lias Corporal Colin Greig, a son of one
of the oldest members of the Stock Exchange. Lieut.
Wald, who won his M.C. early in the year and had
been recommended for a bar to it, had only just
returned to duty after an accident.
The League of Loyal Women, which is about the
biggest patriotic society born of the war, had its
annual business meeting on Tuesday afternoon with
the Galway lady in the chair. The membership has
now topped the 10,000 mark, and in the Trench
Comforts branch of its work alone the takings for
1917-18 show an advance of over £16,000 on the
previous year’s figures.
Two of the last batch of V.C.’s have fallen to
South Australians, though one of them, Lance-
Corporal Axford, is a West Australian by enlistment.
The one of whom this State can claim undisputed
possession is Corporal Philip Davey, who already
had four wound stripes and the M.M. to his credit.
Hi? four brothers all enlisted, one of them when he
was still under 15, and altogether the family list
has a V.C., three M.M.’s and a French decoration.
Davey’s latest distinction brings its third V.C. to
our pioneer battalion, the 10th. He won it in a
daylight attack by bombing a machine-gun crew,
capturing the gun and using it in beating off a
counter effort.
Lawyer Stanley Skipper has got his commission
and is booked to sail soon.
Mrs. Evelyn O'Leary and her two small daughters
are on their way to New York. Dr. O’Leary returned
to England some months ago with hospital work in
France as his objective.
The Simpson Newlands’ house in North Adelaide
has been sold to the Methodists, who will use it as
a hospital.
Lieut.-Colonel Newman, who has been in tempo-
rary command of the 10th Battalion, has got the
D.S.O. He is reported severely wounded.
For some time it has been believed here that the
Le Huntes had lost both their son and their son-in-
law in the war; but now the good news has come
that one is in Switzerland and the other with his
battery in India.
Particulars have come through of the performance
that won Capt. C. E. Howells, of the Royal Air
Force, his M.C. He was leading a patrol of three on
the Italian front when he saw 10 German ’planes
escorting two double-seaters coming over the Italian
lines. He attacked and brought down four
machines himself, while one of his companions
accounted for two others, and the balance were
driven down. In pre-war days he held a commission
in the citizen forces, but resigned it to get to the
front as a private.
W. J. Denny, at one time Attorney-General of
S.A., and still a member of the Assembly—he was
re-elected last March, in his absence —writes to
friends in Adelaide that he is recovered from his
wound, has been given the rank of captain, and
was returning to military duties.
Georgina Thompson, heiress and orphan
of the woolly Hugh Thompsons, of Cocketge-
dong station (N.S.W.), is engaged to Alec
Murphy, a returned Anzac, son of the widow
Murphy, of Mornington. The last-named is
one of the chief teacups in the Alexandra
Club.
Beryl Fanning, now Mrs. F. T. Kingsley
Newell, is honeymooning at Coombe Cot-
tage. Melba was one of the very few wed-
ding guests. She is staying at State Govern-
ment House. Beryl was dainty in white
chiffon and an enveloping lace veil for the
quiet ceremony on Thursday afternoon.
Perhaps it’s because the sporting instinct
is in our blood. Anyhow, even with a high-
class musical function it's marvellous how
things liven up when the sporting element
enters. Last week in the Melb. Town Hall
there was a new departure from the conven-
tional concert line. Two rival choirs —the
Victorian Festival Choir and the Melb.
Choral Union—gave a joint concert. Each
choir sang separately under it* own con-
ductor’, and then they were massed for two
items, each of which gave one conductor a
chance with the combined forces. There were
many advantages. Expenses were halved,
and the receipts were doubled. The interest
was heightened to an intense degree because
the concert almost took on the nature of a
competition. Amongst the audience were
scattered excited little bands of ardent bar-
raekers, and while one conductor was mount-
ing the platform the other would be seen
sprinting round to the front of the house
to be in the best possible position to gauge
effects. From the outstanding success of
the venture there will probably be more of
the kind to follow.
The casual frock-fancier can always find
something to tickle her eye at the Chappell
concerts at the Auditorium. Several of the
lady artists show that fashion has wrestled
with music for the possession of their souls.
Particularly i s this the case with the Melba
protegees, Stella Power and Peggy Center.
The dark-haired Stella sang the other night
in a blue and carmine mixture, with start-
ling shoes and stockings. Peggy Center was
n .°t less picturesque when she tripped into
view on Saturday. Her dressmaker had
confiscated much of the white corsage and
thrown in some sort of wrap, which was
magenta and diaphanous. The skirt was
white in terraces of trimmings, whilst an
imitation red flower, about as large as a
cabbage, was suspended in the same place
where the Australian Scotchman wants to
hang his sporran when he gangs to the war.
Flautist John Amadio’s pretty little wife,
who has been playing hubby’s accompani-
ments, appeared with him at the last munici-
pal concert. She is one of Stock Exchange
I red Roberts’s daughters.
Next month Melba will open a memorial
exhibition of the late Hugh Ramsay’s paint-
ings, at the rooms of the Fine Art Society.
Ramsay was a gifted young Australian, who
went to Europe and had two pictures hung
in the Paris Salon during his first year
there. A damaged lung forced him to hurry
back to Australia; but. even our sunshine
could not save him, and he died in Mel-
bourne 14 years ago, leaving 50 pictures,
mostly portraits, several of them works of
real genius, in the possession of his family.
Ten of the paintings will be on sale, to
public galleries only.
Leila Doubleday, the child artist of a few
years ago, is back in Melbourne with her
violin and her soulful expression. The
photo shows the
latter to advantage.
Yo-ung Leila is
quite what she pro-
mised to become
when her friends
packed her off to
Europe to develop
talent that is near
akin to genius. In
her face there is a
good deal of her
clever mother, to
whom Amy Castles
owed her equipment
for a career. The
war came unkindly
to the brilliant vio-
linist. She had the
bad luck to be per-
forming on Austrian
territory when the horror started, and for
three months she was interned. After her
release she went to London. Later she went
to Switzerland, and for two and a half
years there she made music for repatriated
soldiers.
Rain like that which preceded Noah's
Flood had no effect in stopping the first-
night rush to Polini’s new show, “The In-
visible Foe.” The train and tram people
were overcoated and muffled, but the motored
folk sported boiled shirts and flimsy chif-
fons. Percy Stuart advertised his laundry,
and his lady wore, in conjunction with other
things, some filmy lace and a white swans-
downy cape. Tennis-player Dunlop and Dr.
Murray Morton were in full regalia, and the
Davis pair brightened the circle. Percy
pean’s wife was in a box with a Tait wife
in a rose-tulle gown. Best was among the
starched crowd. Nellie Veitch looked like
Christmas in white fur and holly berries.
The Monasli dame wore , green tulle, and
partied with mining-man Bloch and his fair
daughter. Pol ini got a fine effect by wear-
ing a dull black glacd frock in a room set
with rosewood furniture upholstered in sun-
set pink velvet.

DEWARS
In the case of Whisky the
General Verdict is in favour
of Dewar’s on three counts,
MELLOWNESS —PURITY
RELIABILITY
Mellowness. That alluring perfection
of flavour that begets the happy smile of
appreciation. Dewar’s never robs one of
pleasure—be the anticipation never so great.
Its charm, of flavour is elusive and exclusive
—soft, rich and yet crisp.
Purity. The safeguard of health and the
rock on which the reputation of Dewar’s
stands. It is this domination of Purity in
all the stages of preparation that makes
Dewar's the Aristocrat of Whiskies whose
companionship is ever a source of unceasing
delight and happiness.
Reliability is the crowning virtue of
Dewar’s. All its merits in Distillation,
Blending, Purity, Age and Flavour contribute
to this one great end: —that no matter when
or where you buy Dewar’s you can, without
any fear of disillusionment, rely on an unvary-
ing measure of rarest pleasure that makes
Dewar’s a perfect treasure.

How arc the imported nags going to
shape in the big Spring handicaps? bast September-
November the dark ones from England wore more
honored in the preach than the performance. The
previous Spring was a great time for the foreigners.
One of them just missed the Epsom, another won
the Metrop., three? tilled the Caulfield Cup places,
and two of them were only beaten by a Maorilander
(Sasanotf) in Melbourne Cup. There is a stronger
force of imported horses among the entries this
season than ever before, so unless they make a great
show in the results it will seem hardly worth while
to bring horses all the way from Europe for racing
purposes. At present a lot of them look very
dangerous, and it would he interesting to hear the
best price on offer against an English-bred horse
winning the Caulfield Cup, anyhow.
* * * *
Some worth-while racing transpired at
Roseliill (Sydney) on Saturday, one of the results
thereof being to make the argument about the Epsom
more involved than ever. Two performances stood
well out from the rest, and there were a few others
worth noticing. Poitrina, starting at 9 to 4, won
a division of the Highweight, six furlongs, with list.
91b. aboard. Cerise Colors beat a first-class field in
the Auburn Handicap, seven furlongs, carrying Bst.,
at a write-your-own-ticket price in the ring. The
main point about these happenings is that Poitrina
has 9st. 61b. in the Epsom, and Cerise Colors, a
hard-worked battler from the pony courses, 7st. 61b.
There is nothing in the big mile that on recent
running looks anything like so good as these two,
just now anyway.
In the Highweight Poitrina was giving the im-
ported Louvetier 241 b., Bimeter 131 b., Franco 321 b.,
and others up to only a few pounds below Aewt.
She ran off with a six-lengths lead, was cantering
over the last half-furlong, and won pulling up in
“lffj.” Previous races had shown that weight
matters nothing to this very beautiful mare, and
Saturday’s gallop was strong confirmation. Of course
she may not run out a mile. She failed in the
Doncaster with 9st. lib. ; but it is worth remember-
ing that at two years old she won the A..LG. Easter
Stakes, seven furlongs, and that several things inci-
dental to training Poitrina have been found out late
in the day. She always runs best when “big,” and
worst, when fined down by work. Kelso has her in
rare good condition just now.
Behind Cerise Colors at the end of the Auburn
Handicap were George Graves, Amadis, Volpi, Dame
Acre, Norburv, Portrait, Promised Land, Rebus,
Petalster, Satin Bird, all of which have been backed
for the Epsom—a few with some persistence. Of
course, most of them are yet fairly backward in
condition. But Volpi was not. He was backed
like a certainty, and trainer Booth was very confi-
dent about him. Nor was Promised Land wanting
in condition. For him there was firm support, and
the George Graves and Portrait people were moder-
ately hopeful. Naturally since he had been run to
a short price for the Epsom, the travelling Volpi
was watched with greatest interest. He got away
fairly well, was conveniently placed at the turn, and
coming through on the rail was in front just below
the Leger. There he was done and finished tefitli,
after covering the last furlong as rapidly as a
barrowful o’ bunnies. Meanwhile, Cerise Colors had
been arguing the point with George Graves, King’s
Word and Amadis; He shook them off. Then the
Sydney Cup winner Rebus came flying along at a
mile a. minute —-just too late. The ex-pony struck
the line with his head to spare. Amadis was doing
fairly well at the end. George Graves will do
better presently. His /condition can be much im-
proved. Portrait shut up at six furlongs, and finished
last.
Earlier in the week Rebus was backed for both
the Epsom and the Metrop, a circumstance which it
is not likely the prad’s running on Saturday caused
the stable to regret. Rebus is a very versatile
animal. Over a mile it is against him that he
begins badly, but over the last furlong or two be
can unwind himself at a phenomenal rate. Previous
to the Sydney Cup win he had shown this several
times. He is a common-looking chap, and sweats
himself into a lather with excitement before getting
on the course.
Louvetier, an imported horse with a poor record,
trained by W. Booth at Roseliill, carried a heavy
wad in Poitrina’s race. He got away very badly,
though that made no difference. The noticeable
thing was tlrat at the finish he was ahead of them
all, except the flier in front. For some reason or
other this nag is under popular suspicion of being
something special. Saturday’s exhibition didn’t sup-
port this much, but the fact that Louvetier is still
on the large side and a conspicuously good looking-
moke may be no cause for expecting him not to be
beard from subsequently. He is in the Epsom with
7st. 101 b.
Kennaquhair, big and lusty looking, made 14
others look like hacks in the Rosohill Handicap on
Saturday—-which is rust what most of them are.
Tie parted company from the rest at the turn, and
running on arrived four lengths ahead of the im-
ported Publican, Old Cagou was a trier, but never
looked like having a chance. Most likelv he reckons
the time is ripe for a rest. Most stallions .do long
before their ninth year.
Kennaquhair was favorite for the Metro-
politan before winning Roseliill Handicap on Satur-
day, and shortened to something that had to be
looked for with a microscope soon after. He may
be at a better price on the day than he was early
in the week. As he carried 9st. at Rosehill, where
the first five furlongs were run in lmin. 34sec., and
finished the mile and a quarter going easily in 2min.
7sec., the circumstance of having' Sst. 41b. in the
Metropolitan doesn’t seem to count against him on
the time test.
Adrian Knox, chairman of the A.J.C., in
some remarks the other day about the abolition of
place-betting, said he had often been amazed at the
odds accepted by the public from the place-fielders.
There is nothing remarkable in this, but it would be
interesting to know whether Knox’s amazement
extends to the odds the public take about places
under the 60, 20, 20 totalisator system. It cannot
have escaped his notice that the odds are often more
wildly insane than anj'thing the most ravenous place-
holder ever tried to perpetrate. There are occasions
when there are no odds at all—-when the backer of
a second or third runner is handed back less than
he put up. If there is anything to be said for such
happenings as that this page would like to hear
what it is, but though the request for information
has been many times made nobody breaks the
silence. Take the second and third place-bets paid
by the machine at Rosehill on Saturday: Maiden —
13 to 1, evens. Highweight—First division, second
horse backers lost 25 per cent, of their stake, third
2 to 1 ; second division, second backers lost 40
per cent, of their stake, third backers laid 20 to 1
“on.” Rosehill Handicap—s to 2 and 2 to 1.
Parramatta Mile —Second backers lost 20 per cent.,
third 7 to 1. So in five races the punter on n place
could find a place-getter each time and lose on three.
Just compare this: At Rosehill (Sydney)
on Saturday the tote was considered to have “done
well,” the turnover being ,£12,171 on six races.
At the recent Winter Meeting of the Canterbury
Jockey Club (M.L.) the turnover was ,£127,665 in
two days; There were a couple of days’ trots at
Christchurch during the same week and .£92,144
went through the machine, making £219,759 for the
four days’ proceedings. On the last race of the
four days there was £12,286 on the machine—
over £lOO more than went on all day at Rosehill.
Of course, in a way, these M.L. figures can be taken
as indisputable evidence of public lunacy and positive
proof that the allegation about the machine having
a restricting influence on punting is a silly old gag;
but that is not the point of the arithmetic when
considering the manner of the totalisator’s use in
N. S. Wales in comparison with other places.
In M.L. the machine gets practically all the betting,
or at any rate all the betting on the ground. Here-
abouts it gets the fag end.

There was wild cheering out at Victoria
Park (Sydney) on Saturday when the div. on
Larinot, winner of the second division of the
Encourage Stakes, was hoisted on the totalisator.
Only two tickets on Larinot were registered on the
machine, and the lucky holders drew £lO5 3s. 9d.
each. One of the ticket proprietors was a returned
soldier, who was half shares with a mate. The
other was a civilian. A curious feature of the
matter is that even the trainer of the winner never
regarded the nag seriously. The horse never visits
the training track, and almost any afternoon can be
seen with the owner’s daughter aboard being hacked
round Botany.
“F.” : The'late John Hill, who long time
trained racers at Glenelg (S. Aus), seemed to have
modelled his style to match his pleasant-looking face.
At the time he brought Auraria to Melbourne to
win Derby and . Cup (Wallace beat the game little
mare in the Derby), Hill bore a general resemblance
to the English trainers of half a century earlier. His
clean-shaven face suggested the old-fashioned stud
groom of old sporting prints. His claim to have
gone straight all his racing days was doubtless an
honest claim. He might have sat for a picture of
Rectitude—in the turf sense of the term before the
turf changed for the worse. One of Alf Vincent’s
pages of Melbourne Cup characters included a very
good portrait of Hill, with a saddle over his arm,
talking to David James, the owner of Auraria.
The “old course” at Victoria Park.
Adelaide, will always remain a sort of enchanted
ground for a railway-man of Ballarat (Vic.)- He
drew Pistolier in the G. N. Hurdles of S.A., and the
chances seemed so good that he resolved to take a
hand in person. He first took the 400-mile journey
to Adelaide, made handsome (though really unneces-
sary) promises to owners and jockey, and then stood
by and saw the bearer of his fortunes —something-
well over £3OOO was still “going for’’ him —come
home practically alone. The old Pistol-Enigma geld-
ing carried list. 131 b., and was giving over a stone
to everything else.- He is a Port Adelaide Cup
winner on the flat, and though strong over hurdles
in S.A.. thirds have been his best at Flemington and
Caulfield.
S.A. thinks it may have a really good
three-vear-old in Bullock and Patterson’s Stagegirl
(Comedy King—Petruvia). She was clearly that
State's best two-year-old, and has started the new
season by winning the Adelaide Guineas. Fitness,
the only other well backed, was a bad third, but
McLachlan’s Paratoo (Pistol-Xena Bell) ran Stage-
girl to half a head. The Fulham Park Plate winner,
Allen and Yates’s Lord Setay (St. Anton-Lady Dud-
ley), is another boost for the local sire. He won
comfortably from Mrs. Hawker’s much-better-fancied
Arrasene (The Welkin-Chenille), bought last March,
and still in Scobie's hands for training.
“Trot” : The explanation of what “blinds
and paralyses” the men (B. 15/8/’IS) who encour-
age! the 2-year-old racing they know ought to be
either repressed or prohibited is that, this racing
creates a demand for young horses and keeps prices
up. Of course there would be an equal demand, and
prices would not be hurt in the end if the stakes
were up for 3- and 4-year-olds, but asNthe breeder
doesn’t see that way there is a vicious circle work-
ing. The influence of breeders is in the direction of
paying big stakes for 2-year-old journeys in order
that the occasional topnoteher can win a hatful and
set buyers competing for more topnotchers at the
next sales. Those people who argue against 2-year-
old racing, and particularly against the running of
fillies at that stage, are undoubtedly right. Every
trainer knows this. So, too, does every man who
watches racing from the outside with intelligent
eyes. Someone ought to prepare a tabulated state-
ment each year showing what had been done with
every nag from the yearling stage upwards, and
collect the details of training-stable ruination into
Statistical information .convertible into terms of cash
—wasted and otherwise. The results would be sur-
prising, even to the most confirmed hater of present,
methods. The waste of horse and the waste of cash
and effort are among the outstanding features of
racing, and most of it is due to abuse of the
immature animal.
A. McPherson, the miniature five-eighth
of Western Suburbs (Sydney), who topped the
scoring with 96 points (45 goals and tw T o tries),
had the busiest time in N. S. Wales ltugby League
this season. His job was to kick the club to victory,
and bo was a faithful servant. Although he headed
Western Suburbs’ aggregate scores in ’96 arid ’97
this season was his best in the League. Harold
Horder (South Sydney) was close up with 92 (22
tries and 13 goals). Other be'avy scorers were F.
Burge (Glebe) with 83 (27 tries and one goal) ;
Wally Messenger (Eastern Suburbs), 77 (7 tries and
28 goals) ; arid A. Oxford (South Sydney), 75 (7
tries and 27 goals). As most of these players have
been at the top of their club efforts for many years,
the League cannot blame itself for dragging many
new stars into the limelight.
The sprint championship of N. S. Wales
Rugby League on Saturday temporarily allayed the
high-talk which has wafted around Sydney football
enclosures during the season. In the 50 yfrds Tom
Boland (Wests) won from S. Cunningham (Easts)
and Harold Horder (Souths) in 5 4-ssec. In the
100 yards, Horder won from S. Hughes (Annan-
dale) and Cunningham in 10 3-ssec. The aggregate
of points gave Ilorder the title. As the sprinters
carried football togs and boots it looks as if the
timekeepers were dreaming when the gun went.
Next time the League runs off a championship it
might reasonably make competitors carry a ball.
Side by side with the war cables posted
outside Melbourne Age last Saturday afternoon was
the special announcement that a football match had
been postponed on account of the rain.

Last week-end the Metropolitan Cup, the
last of the important metropolitan coursing events,
was decided at Liverpool (N.S.W.). The stake
brought out 32 dogs, and White Wonder, Down the
South and Wild King were joint favorites in a very
good betting stake at 6 to 1. White Wonder was
knocked out in the first round, and Wild King and
Down the South went under in the first ties. After
that Grand Crystal was favorite, with Black Corsair
and Our Corsair next in demand. Once again the
public were off the target. Grand Crystal was
beaten pointless in the third ties hv Black Corsair,
and Our Corsair heat Vera Hope. As both Corsairs
run in the same ownership they divided the stake.
The winners are puppies by Corsair (imp.) out of
Daisy Dell, and as they are only 18 months old
their performance in heating older tvkes suggests
big things to come. Only three Corsair pups have
run in Svdney to date, and they have won three
stakes. The Metropolitan Purse and Plate for bow-
wows beaten in the first round and first ties of the
Cup were won by Hope’s Bride and Wild King.
A feature of the meeting was the clash of
Wayback Tilly, who has now run five seasons and in
addition has reared two families. She won her first
course in the Cup, but was beaten in the first ties,
In the Metropolitan Plate she got into the last four.

The Werribee Coursing Club’s meeting
(Vic.) proved disastrous for N. S. Wales dogs. In
the Comedy King Challenge Stakes for 16 dogs at
20 guineas each, the N. S. Wales animals were
drawn against each other in the first round. That
meant half of them going out first pop. Tn the
second run Beauty Spot was beaten by Quair Hope,
runner-up a few weeks back to Achieved in the
Waterloo Cup at Booty Hill. Beauty Spot’s failure
was a sad knock to local coursing, the Stake being
regarded as hers for the asking. To make matters
worse, Bramble, a kennel mate of Beauty Spot, was
beaten in the thi'rd round of the Bracelet Stakes at
the same meeting. The consolation the downcast
brigade got was to see Quair Hope and Another
Century, winners from Beauty Spot and Bramble
respectively, each annex the stake.

“0.K." ; Head the other day in Melb.
that ‘‘ thc 17 sportsmen who attended the
i1.0.C. grounds on Saturday enjoyed an afternoon's
starling shooting.” Why this insistence on the
term sportsmen? The murder of a dozen trapped
stallings without a miss hardly entitles a man to
pose as a sportsman. Tiger-hunting is sport. Fox-
hunting is sport where the country is difficult, and
the horsemen take some risk of breaking their necks.
Rowing, cricket, footracing, football and swimming
matches are sport. Horse-racing is sport, inasmuch
as it involves the breaking-in and (raining of mettled
steeds, and the risky riding of them bv skilled
athletes who are employing their wits at high ten
sion. But shooting small birds on a gun-club ground i
It’s about as sporty as the solemn winter dissipation
of curling, which consists in giving a gentle pu«h
to a polished iron weight on a polished ice track.

“Camelero” : This is one of Billjim’s little
games in Southern Palestine. A ring is made in
the sand with eight openings marked in the circle,
each opening being marked from one to six.- A
Scarab beetle is then dropped in the centre of the
ring and Billjim’s cash is piled on the various num-
bers. The Scarab is then given a start, and which-
ever opening in the ring it departs from the bettor
who has his cash on this opening fathers in the
piastres. I won £3 in one afternoon.
“B.” : The Gregory family (B. 22/8/’18)
holds a unique spark in Australian cricket.
In April. 1871, Dave, Ned and Charles
Gregory (N.S.W.) played Sam Costick, Con-
way and Tom Wills (Vic.) in a single-wicket match
on the old Albert Ground, Sydney. The Gregorys
won by 54 to 49. The match caused more excite-
ment than a fire. Shortly after the game began
umpire Thompson no-balled Dave Gregory. The
next ball Gregory swung his arm. and the ump.
again called “No-ball!” but Dave had the ball in
his hand. Umpire, players and a crowd of 5000
then got entangled in air argument, which raged till
Bill Caffyn took Thompson’s place. These three
Gregorys were also in the N, S. Wales team against
Victoria in ’7l, with Nat .Thompson (captain),
Monty Faithfull, George Moore, Dick Hewitt, Ned
Sheridan, Joe Coates, Bill Caffvn and Charlie Ban-
nerman.
“Wart”: Re the Speck’s mountain
shrimp, mentioned .by “D.F.” (B. 25/7/T8). The
little chap seems to be fairly common in most of
Tassv’s streams right down to where they feel the
tide influence. Fishing for blaekfisli in the First
Basin, just above Launceston, I once pulled a shrimp
up on the bait, which was. an ordinary common
worm. Then I pulled the worm off and put the
shrimp on for bait, and it was not long before I
pulled out a blaekfisli. That gave me an idea, and
next time I went fishing J took some sea shrimps
and caught one of the best bags of blaekfisli that
I had ever caught. The only better bait I know of
for blackftsh is a piece of boiled, freshwater crayfish.
“Dale”: Can anyone beat these instances
of queer behaviour on the part of bullets? I
was out with a fisherman on the Murray when he
took a shot" at a swan flying overhead. He fired
with the pea-rifle straight into the air and the pellet
struck the water about three feet from the boat. We
found the bird subsequently on the edge of the
billabong and the bullet had ploughed straight down
the breast-bone and glanced down to the water again.
On the way home lie fired at a rabbit which was
squatting in the fork of a fallen tree. The bullet
slid round the fork, missed the rabbit, and hit the
ground almost at our feet. It was a most interest-
ing day.

O.B.: “Our Native Land” gets -us:—■
Oh Australians! Australians of this beautiful coun-
try we own.
Fight for our native land of which we are born ;
Never let our enemies see that we are fly-blown,
But show the world the grit we’ive borne.
Oh, Never let us see Australia’s doom,
In a country of plenty and plenty of room ;
For there’s not a better country with a Kingdom,
That ever acted with more wisdom.
Fight for your country, your native soil,
Think, think, to have to be drove to toil:
Its enough to make ones heart flutter and boil,
Fight, fight, and settle this turmoil.
Oh Australians! Australians when will you realise,
We’re fighting the worst enemies under the skies;
Go: go and fight where your brethren lies,
Soften your hearts and hear their cries.
Oh Australians! Australians have you no fear
No sympathy to show or to shed a tear;
For the loved ones that’s gone this four year,
Bring it to your thoughts and give up this blare.

Thomas Corbin, of Adelaide, just dead at
75, was the senior doctor, by registration, of
South Aus. He was under Paget at Bar-
tholomew’s Hospital, London, in the early
’sixties, and came to Australia in 1864 as
surgeon on the sailing-ship Orient. His
first job was on the Burra (S.A.) copper-
mine, but he soon became house-surgeon at
the Adelaide Hospital and worked up a large
private practice. He had been living in re-
tirement for some years, but two clever sons.
.John and Cecil, carry on the family pro-
fession.
It is now officially announced that the
Rev. Paul Joseph was killed in Prance last
May, at the time when he was, reported
missing. An eloquent preacher, he rather
carried the Congregationalists of Adelaide
off their feet two or three years ago. He
was a son-in-law of old Dr. Bevan, and had
a reputation as a financier which turned
out to be undeserved. A Tasmanian legacy
was a misfortune to him. He bought the
late Chief Justice Way’s splendid home, took
the deeds of Hindmarsh Square church to a
bank and financed the building of a “Temple”
on a commanding North Terrace site. It
is known that he made no personal profit
out of these transactions, but who is to stand
the loss has hardly been settled even yet.
Anyhow building was suddenly stonned with
the walls a few feet up (as they still
remain), and the preacher enlisted as a
private. As The Bulletin remarked then,
his career was like melodrama, all through.
The last act was finely played.

Business, Robbery, etc,
Price-fixing has some elements of humor.
Australia has devoted 50 years to promoting
reliance on imported goods, so that, as regards
nine articles out of ten, we have no
voice in deciding the price; also in
shoving nearly half the population of
the continent into four cities, so that
the things we produce locally—meat, butter,
wheat, etc. —may have to travel the longest
possible distances to a market. Then it
provided breaks of railway gauge to make
sure that the goods will be bogged as much
as possible on the way to market, and prac-
tically gave up shipbuilding to make sea-
carriage to the markets as difficult as pos-
sible. Finally it became excited and started
to put everything right in a day by declaring
that though the principal causes of the
trouble must not be interfered with, the
effects were prohibited.
* * * *
“Hawker”: lie the damfoolish conserva-
tism of Australian manuiacturers, noted by
“Australia Preferred” (B. 25/'7/’IS). Years
ago I pointed out to certain importers that
the ordinary English-made enamelled sauce-
pan was a slippery, capsizable affair, and
asked why they didn’t order straight-sided
vessels, like a stewpau, only deeper. They
told me they had done so often, but that
the British declined to depart from estab-
lished methods —that if the importers didn’t
like his goods they were at liberty to go
without. Similar representations were then
made to German firms, who instantly grabbed
the opportunity to fill the orders. The
article wasn’t wo good, but it was cheaper
and was what the consumer asked for.
Another instance. Soon after a well-
known separator came into use I discerned
an improvement which I thought would
bring cash to the makers and to myself. The
Australian agents were disinterested and
aloof, and finally chivvied me outside. Then
1 wrote to a separator manufacturing firm
in Stockholm, and by return mail got a
courteous reply stating that my notion had
already been adopted, as I would see by the
newest machines. They invited me to offer them
any other improvements I might evolve, and
promising to pay for them if adopted. The
llun is an uncivilised scoundrel, but there
are many of his business methods we might
adopt with advantage. If we don’t he’ll
have a good show of recapturing his lost
trade.
* * * *
“O. Ships”: If the Federal Government
decides in favor of concrete ships when it
comes out of its long trance, Geelong (Vic.)
will be the first in the business. There is a
site all ready linked by rail with the Fyans-
ford cement works, which are only a few
miles away. Back in February, 1917, E. J.
Stone, of Stone and Siddeley, a firm which
has done a lot of concrete work at Geelong
and elsewhere in Australia, put up a pro-
posal to build concrete vessels at Geelong,
and offered to lodge £lO,OOO, to be forfeited
if he did not turn out a satisfactory sea-going
ship. But the Government apparently
reckoned that there was too much business
and too little talk about Stone’s plans and
pitched them out. When Hughes went to
America he found that someone there had
done the trick —but then, of course, the
Americans are terrors for doing things.
* * * *
“0.K.” : Australia, for the number of its
population, must be the largest consumer of
briar pipes of any country in the world.
Millions of pipes are imported, though possi-
bly the mallee-roots of Victoria are as suit-
able for pipe-making as the Italian roots
that we get in manufactured form. The one
thing certain is that new characters in Aus-
tralian woods are being revealed every year.
Perhaps the Melbourne University school of
pipe-making for returned soldiers has some
practical ideas. The Ruwolt works at Rich-
mond (Melb.) lately got a University order
for five pipe-making machines, and will
supply them at cost price.
* * * *
“Rags”: Before an Inter-State Commis-
sion, sitting in Adelaide to investigate the
prices of clothing and boots, the secretary
of the Warehousemen’s Association said
that that body did not fix the prices,
nor was there any understanding regarding
them. The resolutions passed by the mem-
bers were strictly private, but there was no
reason for such privacy. L. C. Hunkin
(representing the Trades and Labor Coun-
cil) mentioned that storekeepers had noticed
there were alterations in travellers’ prices
following the meetings, but Commissioner
Lockyer prevented any reply by interjecting:
“If that is so it will come out. in evi-
dence.” It is a remarkable coincidence
that when inquiring for quotations from
the wholesalers the prices given are exactly
the same in each instance. If the rates are
not fixed there must be an “honorable under-
standing,” with perhaps a heavy fine if the
members depart therefrom. Anyhow, the
“Association price” is an everyday term in
the rag trade, and if one does happen to
get a cut rate the opposition houses are
very anxious to see the invoice.
* * * *
Another of the Sam Hordern properties
will pass under the hammer in Sydney at the
end of the month. It is the Coronation
Hotel on the corner (N.) of Park and
George streets, a new building. The corner
has been a beer-pump site for many a year.
Tradition says that the father of the first
baronet, Dan Cooper, had a bakery on the
ground. In the ’forties John Greer,
a builder, and father of the late
James Greer, solicitor, owned the property.
About the year 1850 George Simpson, who
had kept the St. Andrew’s Tavern on the
angle of Kent and King streets, and the
Currency Lass, corner of Hunter and Pitt
streets (now Empire corner), opened it as
a pubbery under the sign Simpson’s Hotel;
a few years later it was kept by Charles
Beal, a" name well known in cricket circles,
and the Beals had it well into the ’seventies,
when it became the Town Hall Hotel. On
being rebuilt it became the Commonwealth
and then the Coronation.

BOARD OF DIRECTORS.
George Slade, Esq., Chairman.
Robert James Black, Esq. Hon. Sir Arthur Lawley, G.C.S.I.
Gordon lluntly Campbell, Esq. John Graham O’Neill, Esq.
Andrew Cunningham, Esq. Edward Vincent Reid, Esq.
DIRECTORS' REPORT.
Presented at the Ordinary General Meeting of Proprietors, held at Winchester House,
Old Broad Street, E.C. 2 ( Hall No. 174), at 12 o'clock noon, oh Wednesday,
May 15, 1918.
The Directors present their Report accompanied as usual by audited accounts for
the year ended 31st December last.
The profits, after deducting management charges, all taxes, including the Federal
Income and Land Taxes and State Government Land and Income Taxes, and providing
for all bad and doubtful debts, amount to £83,245/2/11.
The Directors recommend the payment of a dividend at the rate of 7 per cent, per
annum, less Income Tax, on both the preference and the ordinary shares for the six
months ended 31st December, 1917.
These payments, with the interim dividend paid on the 2nd November, 1917, and
a grant to the Officers’ Provident Fund of £3OOO, absorb £49,399/13/0, leaving a balance
of £33,845/9/5 of the year’s profits, £30,000 of which has been transferred to the
Reserve Fund; the balance of £3845/9/5 is added to the amount brought forward from
last year, thus increasing the amount to be carried forward to £24,526/1/1.
The Reserve Fund is increased to £355,000.
Under the provisions of the Bank’s Articles of Association, the directors retiring
are Mr. Gordon lluntly Campbell and Mr. John Graham O’Neill, who, being eligible,
offer themselves for re-election.
A resolution will be proposed that the remuneration of the directors be increased
from £3OOO to £3500 free of Income Tax. The present amount was fixed in 1893 at a
low level, owing to the circumstances of the Bank, but the business of the Bank has
expanded very greatly since that date, involving increased demands upon the time
and adding largely to the responsibilities of the directors, who therefore feel justified in
asking the shareholders to grant the above increase in the remuneration.
The Bank’s auditors, Messrs. Price, Waterhouse and Co., retire, and again offer
themselves for re-election.
The transfer-books are closed from the 2nd May to the 15th May, both days inclusive.
Warrants for dividends will be issued, payable on and after the 17th May next, to
those shareholders whose names appear on the Register on the 2nd instant.
By order of the Board,
r W. H. BARBER, London Manager and Secretary,.
71 Old Broad Street, London, E.C. 2, 3rd May, 1918.
BALANCE-SHEET, 31st DECEMBER, 1917.

AUDITORS’ REPORT,
To the Shareholders of the London Bank of Australia Limited: —•
We report that we have examined the above balance-sheet, dated 31st December, 1917, with the
books at the head office and the branch returns from Australia, and have obtained all the information
and explanations we have required. In our opinion the balance-sheet is properly drawn up so as to
exhibit a true and correct view of the state of the bank’s affairs, according to the best of our informa-

Fey.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE day’s affairs, with all their squalid
load
Of vain achievements and belittling
aims,
Are past and done with, saving they fore-
bode
A fellow day of like unyielding claims
For ill-graced service, quickened by the goad
Of rebelling Need, that urges as it tames ;
And murmured from the drowsy lips of God
Is Even come, to ease us of our hod
And lead us over vagrom ways with wistful
fancies shod.
The highway from the city passes by,
But all the hum of homeward traffic seems
A requiem mass, made perfect harmony
By muffling distance and the purple streams
Of Even, misting down on land and sea
To drape as with a veil of yearning dreams
The grassy slopes, the streamlet feeding
pond,
The valley densed with nikau leaf and frond,
The battlement of city rooves that blur the
sky beyond.
As some swart beauty wraps a jewelled
shawl
About her gleaming shoulders, so the Night
Wraps all the city ; plying ferries crawl
From shore to shore, with twinkling shafts
of light
Waked on the fluttering waters; off to trawl,
The fishing craft steam seawards out of
sight;
A glimpse of phantom loveliness, aloof
From bawling day’s insistence, sent in proof
Of Beauty’s subtle weavings, woven fair in
warp and woof.
And now it is that Beauty’s self is seen;
She doffs her glad apparelings of Day,
And, as it were her tender mood to wean
Our thoughts from pain and arid cares away,
She pilfers from the moon its silvern sheen,
She borrows from the Dawn her veils of
gray,
And weaves them into garments that caress
Her perfect limbs and lissom, hiding less
Than even more revealing all their rounded
loveliness.
If moonlight fleshed as women were to be.
If music could be truly wrought in line.
It is as these to-night she comes to me
And feys me to a fellowship divine
With her own suave delight: I seem to see
A World Enchanted, its enchantment mine,
And yielding those most intimate delights
Which Hope and Memory cling to, through
the plights
Of Toil and grisly Circumstance, where
little else requites.
Delicious music fills the scented air
With lull and flood of undulating sound.
Borne from the flutes and lutes and viols
rare
Of wild-eyed minstrel women; wonder-
gowned
In robes of glimmering opal, how their hair
Tumbles, like moonlit waterfalls, around
Their gleaming limbs, whose swayings to
and fro
The tempo governs—swift in allegro
Or slowed to faint cadenzas, limpid, languor-
ous and low.
Here are the loves that once I loved, and
each
Appears to me in mid-love’s royal guise,
Flushed with a nuptial longing, and the
speech
Of Love’s inviting fluent in her eyes,
Ere dolorous surfeit, self-ashamed, impeach
Love’s swift unloosings of her own sweet
ties;
And here, as well, the glad loves yet to be
Stir fevers in the veins unceasingly,
To make the glimpsing Present swell the
Future’s amorous fee.
Here, too. the friends of other days declare
Their friendship as it was in days ago.
And yet again vvo drink, in vintage rare,
The old-time toasts, exulting as the glow
Of Youth’s bravura thrills us; here I bare
My body for the combat, here I throw
Glad challenge at the hostile Fates and
swing
Bude blow for blow, as loud the Vikings
Sing
Within my blood their battle songs and cheer
me, triumphing.
So is it swindling Fancy works her wiles
To cheat the bickering home, the greedy
mart.
The thing of dirty shifts and petty guiles
That fools call Life and live for: through
her art
She wins us to our own. and reconciles
The world of fact by Active worlds apart,
Where we may raise, to each his heart’s
desire.
Meet sanctuary and shrine and altar fire _
To Beauty’s goddess self, than whom none is
nor can be higher.
JWAORILANO.
BERNARD INGLEBY.
A Dog Fight.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE forthcoming tight between The
Dove, the old dog of Hobart Town,
and Fincher, the new dog from
Launceston, was talked of in the island for
weeks during the latter part of 1836. The
Launceston fancy lobbed into Hobart Town
two days before the fight, and fixed their
camp at the Plough and Harrow. There
were five of the party, the pannikin boss
of the crowd being a big, square-jawed Irish-
man, with the eyes of a ferret, and a broken
nose, who was known to the “crooks” as
“Flash Pat.” Before he was transported he
ran a dog-pit at Westminster, and he came
to the island with a reputation of being a
“leery cove,” who knew as much about the
training of fighting dogs as anyone.
For a year or more he had kept what he
called has “peepers skinned” for a bull-
terrier with “ginger.” but in the end he had
to breed one himself. He trained the pup,
taking care that the dogs the youngster fought
in his novitiate were mongrels that he could
master. By degrees Flash Pat set Pineher
to fight better-bred and tougher dogs.
Fincher put up a showing from the first, hav-
ing the ’nght fighting strain in him. Fat told
his pals that he felt sure that Pincher, young
and game till death, could “bate The Dove.
Pincher looked a likely dog, too, with his
strong jaw, well-developed chest, powerful
shoulders, light neck and strong loins. True
he had not The Dove’s experience, but the
young Tin was not without ring craftsman-
the challenge Pincher was described as
“a white bull-terrier, spotted with black,
weighing 59 pounds,” and the challenge was
“for the champion’s collar of Tasmania and
a side wager of 100 dollars, the entrance
money to the side of the ring to be divided
equally, the fight to take place a month from
the date of signing the articles.”
A notice was put up in the bar of the
Plough and Harrow, and it was well read by
convicts, sailors, and whalers. It ran :
THE CHAMPION’S LAST FIGHT.
The Old Dog, “THE DOVE,”
Gives Seven Pounds
To The Young Dog, “PINCHER,”
From Port Dalrymple.
Bred by “FLASH PAT.”
A rare evening's sport for the Fancy. Time and
Place from the licensee of the “Spotted Cow.”
When it was known in Hobart Town that
the sports from the Tamar had arrived with
Pincher, the bar of the Plough and Harrow
was crowded by those in the game who were
anxious to see' the hope of Port Dalrymple.
They liked Pincher’s quarters; the pitch of
his jaw ; the depth of his chest. The backers
of The Dove began to kick themselves. They
might have known, they reflected, that the
old bull-terrier’s teeth no longer had their
vyce-like grip, and that up against a young
dog, such as Pincher appeared to be, The
Dove hadn’t a “possible.”
The fight was arranged to take place in
the back yard of a wayside pub on the road
to Launceston, half a mile out of Hobart
Town. The fancy had to be fly as to who
got the office, as the Lieut.-Governor, Colonel
Arthur, had proscribed the sport in the
Colonial Times as “the pastime of idle
blackguards.”
When “Patch,” a Jew “fence,” the best-
known receiver of stolen goods in Hobart
Town, and owner of The Dove, with his
shadow, ail iron-haired ticket-of-leave man
named Bennie, went down to the trying-
out ground, aoout 50 of the fancy had already
ranged themselves around the ring, which
was about 10 feet square, with a skirting-
board, two feet high, forming the limit line
of the fight.
The arrival of The Dove was the occasion
of the mixed crowd straining their necks to
see if the old dog looked fit and well. He
followed his lead, made of strong leather,
ornamented with brass, looking as quiet as a
lamb. He bestowed a glance on and a sniff
at Pincher, and then wagged his stump of a
tail. He gave a half bark, half yawn, as he
looked up and smiled into the oily face of
Patch. '
“Mein tear poy, vas you tinkin’ tings?”
asked Patch, stooping down to pat the dog.
The Dove pushed his grey muzzle into the
Jew’s crook-like hands and licked some grains
of snuff off them. Not liking its taste the
dog cleaned his tongue on the lap of his chops.
“Te-he ! Te-he !” exclaimed Patch. “You
vas a funny dog, eh? Vas you saying you vas
not up to snuff, eh? Veil, veil! Vot about,
dis vonderful Pincher, eh ? I vas tinkin’
you vas frightened ob him. eh ?”
Patch stroked the head of the old dog; a
head as smooth as a clenched boxing-glove.
Flash Pat crossed over to look at The
Dove. He could not withhold his tribute
of admiration, though he qualified it, saying
to Patch —
“He’s a rale beauty, the sweetest dog I
ever see’d. It’s a pity in wan way to bate
him. I’m not the bhoy to crow down such a
dog, but we’ll bate him badly.”
“Stingaree Joe” was the master of the
ring, and Joe never looked better than when
he was a “judge” (he knew judges’ ways),
though he did get along with a limp sugges-
tive of the “chain step.” His black hair
was well oiled, and lay thickly parted in
the middle, on a shrewd-Cockney’s head. He
grew a heavy moustache above a Napoleonic
goatee, and his busy eyebrows were curled.
He was quite gay with a bright bird’s-eye
neckerchief, and a claret-colored waistcoat. On
his hands were two massive brass rings in
which were set agate stones, and they served
the purpose of knuckle-dusters to keep his
circle in harmony.
Joe blew a whistle. Patch and Flash Pat
led their dors into the ring, when Joe put.
up his hand for them to halt while he indi-
cated the conditions under which the fight
was to be fought.
Eight rules governed the fight, one of
which, the fifth, provided that “when the
dogs are not in holds the judge may at his
discretion from time to time allow the dogs
to be taken to their respective corners for
the space of two minutes to be sprayed—at
the expiration of this time the fight must be
at once resumed.”
An ill-conditioned, currish fellow, who had
been well served on his Appearance if
whipped, stood outside the ring, with a
bucket of vinegar and water.
Stingaree Joe, according to Rule 1, gave
the order for each dog to be ready to go out
to scratch.
Patch put The Dove into the ring at
his end: Flash Pat did the same with
Pincher at the opposite end. Both dogs
were then facing each other, ready for the
fight. Pincher was growling and straining
to get at The Dove, but his owner held him
with a tight grip. The Dove sat with his
eyes on Pincher. apparently easy in confi-
dence with Patch’s hand lightly on his head.
“Are you ready?” asked Joe.
As he asked Patch put his hand on the
loins of The Dove and the old dog rose with
a low growl and sprang ready to meet
Pincher, who was still straining at his lead,
looking for trouble.
“Let the dogs go !” cried Joe.
The crowd at the ringside gave a subdued
murmur. Patch and Joe stepped quickly
out of the ring.
The two dogs advanced, but with a differ-
ence which marked their methods of fighting.
Pincher shot forward with a headlong, swift
rush, which threatened to overwhelm the old
dog with sheer impetus of onset. The Dove,
on the other hand, moved as quietly as if he
were treading on eggs, with his weight held

perfectly balanced and his nose pointing
straight at the rushing Pincher. Pincher
had often, in former fights, caught his oppo-
nent unprepared for his startling rush, and
had contrived to fasten on the other dog’s
throat before it quite kneAv what had hap-
