This time Pincher met liis master at tac-
tics, for when he Avas almost on top of The
Dove, that wily old stager made a slight
bound to the left. So Avell timed and exact
\v as it that it seemed almost leisurely, and
as the impetus of Fincher’s Avas carrying
him past, The Dove banged his right shoulder
on to Fincher’s flank, and knocked him clean
off- his legs.
Turning quick as a flash The Dove made
for the prostrate Pincher, Avliose party yelled
with dismay to be soon quickly turned to
shouts of triumph Avhen their champion, with
a smart roll over, regained his feet, and
faced the oncoming of The Dove with almost
a veteran’s caution.
“Me dog’s a darlin’,” shouted Flash Pat.
“He’ll take some batin’.”
“Four ter vun on mein dog!” cried Patch.
There were a few instants of expectant
silence as tin l tAvo dogs circled slowly about
each other, The Dove moving his head and
neck from side to side with an undulating
motion.
“See the old dorg fiddlin’ with his ’ead ;
hain’t it pretty? The other dorg never knows
when Vs going to bite. He can’t see a bite
coining,” cried Bennie.
“Five to vun!” yelled Patch. “Ach, vill
no vun pick up monish? Acli, how vas your
dog likin’ dat?”
The question Avas the outcome of an in-
cautious movement by Pincher of his head.
The Dove took instant advantage of it and
struck at the left side of Fincher’s neck Avith
the celerity of a snake. Simultaneously it
seemed Pincher dodged, but too late. The
Dove missed the spot he had aimed at by a
fraction only and fastened on the top of the
shoulder, at the same time sharply swinging
himself to the right and out of the reach of
Fincher’s bite. The Dove tugged and pushed
Avitli the intention of again throwing the
young ’un off his feet.
The crowd roared.
Pincher braced himself on his legs. With
a turn of his head he grabbed fiercely at the
old dog, but OAving to the position The Dove
had Avith its jaw fastened on to Fincher’s
shoulder, and with his body almost in the
same line as Fincher’s, and The Dove’s tug-
ging and pulling away from Pinchers bite,
the young ’un Avas unable to muzzle on to
The Dove.
Their flying movements were so quick that
the pox-pitted crowd almost lost the dogs’
identities amidst the flurry of dust which
they tore up.
"Zee obit dog has no teeth,” shouted
Patch. “Eh? Eh? He has no teeth! Yot
a shoke!”
Pincher Avas displaying a pluck and en-
durance under difficulties which partly
buoyed up his backers. Surely The Dove
Avas tiring, while the other animal in the com-
bat of roughing and bollocking seemed
stronger than ever.
“He’ll never keep his grip,” they yelled;
“The Dove is done. The old beggar is
done!”
"’E hain’t, ’e hain’t,” cried Bennie.
“Wait, you , wait 1”
"Five to vun on mein dog!” shouted
Patch, his eyes glued on the bull-terrier’s.
There Avas no doubt of it. In this rough-
and-tumble sort of fight Fincher’s might,
Aveight and youth were telling, but The
Dove was still holding on as only lie could
hold, Avaiting for something to occur by which
he could improve his grip.
The dogs fell and rolled over for one
moment. Pincher Avas on his side with the
Dove on top. Quickly and quietly the old
dog shifted liis grip higher to the throat. He
relaxed his body, and put all his strength
into his jaws to hold on.
Pincher had no chance now of reaching the
old dog. The Dove’s grip prevented that,
and what could Fincher’s pluck and “ginger”
avail Avith his Avindpipe compressed? For
a few moments he put up a tremendous
struggle, whirling the old dog, unresisting,
about. Then, almost suffocated, Pincher fell
on his back, Avhile The Dove stood still,
holding his grip on the throat. He looked
as if he were waiting simply for the judge’s
verdict in his favor.
“The Dove -wins!” cried Stingaree Joe.
Patch and Pat Avent into the ring. The
old dog allowed Patch to choke him off

lie walked quietly back to his corner, Avhere

revive liis property.

Ted Denny’s Treasure.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
nyro one at Peewee had heard of Ted
i Denny’s treasure till Ted recovered
from the long fever he contracted col-
lecting decomposed rabbits for John Hobson,
of the Buttercup run. Ted came up out of
his bed looking like the ghost of the earlier
Denny, a haunted man.
It was Denny’s never-to-be-shaken belief
that just before taking to his bed be had
hidden somewhere something of enormous
value, a vast treasure, some beautiful, desir-
able object the exact nature of which was
still unreAmaled.
Denny spent hours, sitting thinking, think-
ing till the sweat poured out of him, trying
to recollect Avhat it Avas he had hidden and
where he had hidden it. He grew no fatter,
his face remained haggard and grey. The
sheer effort of pursuing his delusion told on
him like violent physical exertion. He
always seemed just upon the point of grasp-
ing the alluring fact; illumination seemed
ever on the eve of bursting in his brain, but
always he missed, always the expected light
failed him.
Ted Denny had never had more than a
couple of pounds of his own. A five-pound note
to him had the aspect of awkward riches.
But Ted had been a consistent prospector in
the promising country about Peewee. and
eventually he fastened upon the belief that
the missing thing was a wonderful nugget
he had found somewhere about Buttercup.
The man who had been a useful standby
for farmers and others needing casual labor
became a restless, useless waster drifting
about seeking his hidden riches. To one
feature of his former life he remained true:
he was still attending chapel with monoto-
nous regularity; he still taught Class B in
the little Wesleyan Sunday-school at Peewee.
For months Ted kept his secret, but event-
ually knowledge of the mystery possessing
him leaked out, and after that the man’s
movements had always a peculiar interest to
his neighbors.
Peewme at the bottom of its heart believed
there avus something at the back of Ted
Denny’s obsession. Many affected levity,
and Ted met with much derision: but the
fact that perhaps, after all. Denny had hid-
den aAvay a great hunk of gold before the
fever took full hold and wiped the where-
abouts of his hoard from his memory, .caused
a lot of hard thinking in the district, and
digging for Denny’s treasure became a popu-
lar pastime that lasted at least a year.
Denny himself was seeking all this time,
like a puzzled hound on a broken scent. Dis-
jointed' scraps of memory flitted in his mind
like motes in a sunbeam. He clutched at
them, and they scuttled in eddies, leaving only
confusion. Then Denny sat down and cried.
The spectacle of Denny sitting in the bush,
weeping over a fresli-dug hole, a turned log
or an uprooted rock, became too familiar to
be recorded at Peewee.
Two and a half years had passed on that
summer’s Sunday morning when Ted Denny
sat in the middle of the packed congrega-
tion in the little hot chapel, while the Rev.
Abel Irons preached one of his most frothy
and fanatical sermons. Peewee Wesleyans
appreciated no sermon that was not fanati-
cal and frothy.
Suddenly Ted Denny jumped to his feet,
lie pointed a long arm. with a long hand
and a long finger, at the preacher. His
face was white as Death’s. His black eyes
blazed excitement.
“I know!” he yelled. “T remember.”
The preacher stopped; the congregation
swung round and stared at Ted Denny.
“I know Avhere I buried the treasure. 4 -’
said Ted. “It’s down by” . He said
no more, but turned, and, scrambling over
Brother Best's rheumatic feet, treading on
the toes of Sister Sarah Tregowan, he wal-
lowed across the congregation, fell into the
aisle, picked himself up and ran.
The faithful stared from the door to the
Rev. Abel Irons, and from the Rev. Abel to
the door, then breaking from all moral re-
straints, fighting and tumbling for the exit,
the congregation poured out of chapel and
followed Denny, who was heading for the
creek, with an old pick he had snatched
from the blacksmith’s scrapheap.
Denny went down under the pile bridge,
and dug- furiously. The congregation pressed
about him, staring with wide, expectant eyes.
For four minutes Denny dug by the third
pile, occasionally pawing Avith bis hands like
a rabbit hound.
A cry broke from the crowd. Denny had
recovered his treasure; it Avas precious
metal; lie held it in his trembling hands,
staring incredulously, still trying to think.
_ Two days before Ted Denny had taken to
his bed of the fever, John Hobson, of Butter-
cup. had been knocked on the head in the
darkness under cover of his own barn and
robbed of his most valuable portable pos-
session.
What Ted Denny noAV held aloft, regard-
ing Avith dull wonder, was, as all clearly
perceived, John Hobson’s gold Avatch!
E. DYSON.
Mother Song.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
DREAM baby, dream baby, you shall be
born
With the song of the lark, with the
green of the corn.
With bloom of the apple, with murmur of
bees,
With tlu> mating of birds in the blowing
gum-trees.
Dream baby, dream baby, you shall have
birth
While on marsh reeds the elves blow a fan-
fare of mirth.
When the streams all a-tiptoc run suddenly
free
And the cliff-flowers glow in the wind of the
sea.
Dream baby, dream baby, you shall be born
With the first silver note of Boy Blue on his
horn,
When wind-bells their blue music through all
tile fields spill
And each daisy smiles out from a fresh
laundered frill.
Dream baby, dream baby, through the tall
grass
On the high winds of laughter all Faery shall
pass ;
Rich treasure you’ll glean from each petal of
clover
And a jewel from each star that shines the
world over.
1)> earn baby, dream baby, you shall be born
With the sony of the lark, with the green of
the corn.
With the bloom of the apple, the murmur of
bees.
With the mating of birds in the blowing
gum-trees.
